Weekly Briefs

24 briefs

  • US

    • FOMC held at 3.50–3.75% Wednesday, but Warsh's debut was decisively hawkish: half of 18 submitting Fed members projected at least one hike by year-end, with the average projected end-2026 rate rising from 3.24% to 3.83%.
    • US PPI rose 6.5% YoY in May — the fastest in over three years — with core PPI ex-food and energy up 4.9%, both above consensus and reinforcing the case for further tightening.
    • Two-year Treasury yields surged 13 basis points Wednesday to 4.18%, the largest single-day move in over a year, as fed funds futures repriced a hike as early as September.
    • Factory output was little changed in May after an upwardly revised 0.7% April gain, the first sign that war-related supply disruptions are beginning to weigh on manufacturing activity.

    Monetary Policy

    Kevin Warsh's inaugural FOMC meeting produced the expected hold, but the surrounding communication represented a meaningful hawkish shift. The committee statement was cut from 341 to 130 words, stripped of forward guidance and all reference to the maximum employment mandate; the sole concluding line read "The Committee will deliver price stability." Warsh declined to submit his own dot plot, signaling intent to de-emphasize that tool, though his colleagues' projections moved sharply: the average end-2026 rate rose to 3.83%, up from 3.24%. Goldman's Rob Kaplan suggested the Fed may need to act as soon as September if inflation remains elevated. Warsh also announced five task forces covering communications, the balance sheet, alternative data, AI and productivity, and the inflation framework — signaling structural regime change whose outcomes are unlikely to materialise until year-end. The 10-year TIPS yield rose to its highest since May 2025, and the dollar recorded its strongest two-day rally in three months, complicating the administration's stated preference for currency weakness.

    Industrial Production and Data

    May factory output was little changed following April's upwardly revised 0.7% gain, with total industrial production rising only 0.1% — the first meaningful deceleration after four consecutive months of gains. The Federal Reserve's own data suggests supply-chain disruptions linked to the Hormuz closure and elevated tariff-driven input costs are beginning to restrain activity. May PPI of 6.5% YoY confirms those cost pressures remain acute, leaving the Fed with limited room to look through inflation even as growth softens.

    Equity and Corporate Markets

    Risk assets oscillated across the week. S&P 500 futures gained 1.2–1.3% Monday on Hormuz relief, paused Tuesday as deal-durability concerns emerged, slumped on the hawkish Fed Wednesday, then partially recovered Thursday as Iran interim deal optimism and lower oil prices provided an offset. The Nasdaq outperformed throughout, supported by SpaceX momentum — the stock rallied approximately 49% over four sessions to a $2.75 trillion market cap, overtaking Amazon to become the fifth-largest stock globally, and is preparing a bond offering of at least $20 billion. Travel stocks led Monday's gains, with United Airlines up 4.3% and Delta up 3.9%, while Exxon and Chevron each fell roughly 2.5%. Nvidia received $85 billion in orders for a $25 billion investment-grade bond sale. The DOJ cleared Paramount-Skydance's $110 billion acquisition of Warner Bros. Discovery. A record $119.2 billion flowed into US equity funds in the week through Wednesday per BofA/EPFR data, though BofA warned a Republican Senate loss represents the clearest near-term downside scenario. ---

    Asia

    • China's May retail sales fell 0.6% YoY — the first contraction since the Covid reopening in late 2022, badly missing consensus — while fixed-asset investment contracted 4.1% in January–May, worse than forecast.
    • The Bank of Japan raised its benchmark rate 25 basis points to 1.0% Tuesday, the highest level since 1995, in a widely anticipated move; the Nikkei hit a new record while the yen remained above 160 per dollar.
    • Bank Indonesia raised its BI-Rate 25 basis points to 5.75% — its third hike in approximately one month — and the Bangko Sentral ng Pilipinas raised 25 basis points to 4.75%; Taiwan held for a ninth consecutive quarter.
    • The MSCI China index fell 1.5% by Thursday and is approximately 20% below its October peak, approaching bear market territory, as weak consumer data and disappointing Alibaba and Tencent earnings weighed on sentiment.

    China: Domestic Demand Deterioration

    Tuesday's data confirmed a deepening bifurcation in China's economy. Retail sales contracted 0.6% YoY in May, with auto purchases plunging 16% and home appliance sales declining by double digits as government trade-in subsidies were reduced to 250 billion yuan from 300 billion in 2025. Even catering expenditure — historically resilient — rose at its slowest pace since late 2022. Fixed-asset investment shrank 4.1% in January–May, the worst since 2020, with private capex down 7.1% and manufacturing investment contracting for the first time in six years. Industrial production accelerated to 4.5% growth, driven by high-tech manufacturing expanding 15% in value-added terms, illustrating a demand-side rather than supply-side failure. Some analysts estimate April GDP growth near 4%, below the 4.5%–5.0% full-year target. PBOC Governor Pan Gongsheng separately signaled at the Lujiazui Forum a potential shift toward an overnight policy rate as the primary benchmark — a structural evolution that would meaningfully increase the central bank's influence over short-term funding costs.

    Bank of Japan

    The BOJ's Tuesday hike to 1.0% was accompanied by a commitment to hold bond purchases at approximately ¥2 trillion monthly through April 2027, when quantitative easing ends entirely. Statement language softened slightly — replacing "real interest rates are at significantly low levels" with "financial conditions are accommodative" — suggesting the tightening path remains intact but is communicated with less urgency. Futures markets assign roughly 50/50 odds to a further hike as early as October. The yen's failure to strengthen above 160 per dollar illustrates the limits of the BOJ's capacity to move the currency unilaterally. The Reserve Bank of Australia held at 4.35% — its first pause this year — warning inflation remains elevated while acknowledging tightening is beginning to weigh on activity.

    Southeast Asia and Regional Markets

    Indonesia's three consecutive hikes reflect urgency in defending the rupiah amid dollar strength amplified by the Fed's hawkish pivot. India's monsoon deficit, running approximately 40% below normal as of mid-June per the India Meteorological Department, introduces additional risk to agricultural output and rural incomes. The KOSPI gained 5.2% Monday on Hormuz relief, lifted by SK Hynix and Samsung, reflecting the region's acute sensitivity to energy import costs. South Korea's financial regulator opened an inquiry into Mirae Asset Securities after the firm failed to secure any SpaceX IPO allocation for clients despite serving as underwriter, with retail demand exhausting a $300 million tranche in under one minute. ---

    Europe

    • UK core CPI held below consensus in May, easing near-term Bank of England tightening pressure; the MPC voted to hold Bank Rate with market pricing compressing from three projected hikes to one.
    • BMW guided full-year profit margins as low as 1%, citing weak Chinese demand and Middle East disruption; shares fell 6.5% Wednesday, dragging Mercedes-Benz down 3.1% and Volkswagen 2.0%.
    • Andy Burnham won the Makerfield by-election Thursday with 24,927 votes versus Reform UK's 16,000, opening a formal path to challenge Prime Minister Starmer; UK gilts fell on the result.
    • Goldman Sachs characterized sterling as the most overvalued currency in the G10.

    ECB and Monetary Policy

    Despite falling oil prices following the Hormuz ceasefire, ECB officials pushed back firmly against assumptions that energy relief removes the case for further tightening. Chief Economist Philip Lane defended this month's hike — the first developed-market tightening in response to war-driven inflation — arguing the conflict has inflicted durable economic damage that cannot be quickly unwound. Overnight index swap markets imply more than one additional hike by year-end. The stagflationary bind remains genuinely unresolved: one analyst framed the risk starkly, noting Europe risks hiking itself into recession while the US ends up with the more entrenched inflation problem. ECB updated projections show both higher inflation and weaker growth, with inflation revisions more pronounced.

    Bank of England and UK Politics

    UK core CPI printed below expectations, and the MPC voted to hold Bank Rate in a split decision, with Chief Economist Huw Pill the sole dissenter seeking a hike. Rate expectations collapsed from three projected increases to one. UK rate expectations have been tracking oil prices with unusual fidelity, suggesting the monetary path remains heavily contingent on Hormuz durability. Burnham's decisive by-election victory Thursday materially raises the probability of a Labour leadership contest. A formal challenge requires at least 81 parliamentary supporters to trigger a confidence vote; Starmer has committed to remaining in post. For markets, political uncertainty arrives alongside already-strained fiscal dynamics. The Bundesbank separately published research estimating demographic aging has suppressed German annual growth by approximately 0.4 percentage points per year over the past decade, rising to 0.6 percentage points in coming years.

    Autos and Corporate

    BMW's profit warning crystallizes a sector-wide vulnerability: European automakers face simultaneous pressure from softening Chinese demand, Middle East supply-chain disruption costs, and an accelerating structural shift to EVs where Chinese manufacturers hold a cost advantage. French President Macron refused to withdraw France's digital services tax despite Trump's threat of retaliatory wine tariffs, setting up a bilateral confrontation at the Evian G7. Rathbones shares fell 16% in London after the wealth manager disclosed £60 million in additional remediation costs following a regulatory review. ---

    LatAm

    • Brazil's Banco Central do Brasil cut its benchmark rate at its June meeting, diverging from the tightening bias adopted by Indonesia, the Philippines, and several regional peers.
    • Peru's presidential race shifted to election courts after Keiko Fujimori took a narrow lead, with hundreds of thousands of ballots in dispute, introducing near-term political uncertainty into the Andean mining sector.
    • Brazil's Supreme Court convicted Eduardo Bolsonaro of unlawfully interfering with judicial proceedings related to his father's coup-plotting trial, adding legal pressure to a political movement ahead of the 2026 electoral cycle.

    Brazil Monetary Policy and Political Risk

    The BCB's rate cut diverges from the hawkish pivot underway globally and reflects the committee's assessment that domestic disinflation has progressed sufficiently to warrant easing. The durability of the cycle depends on how quickly Hormuz-driven oil price relief passes through to domestic fuel and food costs, and whether the external environment stabilises — a materially more uncertain proposition following the Fed's hawkish Wednesday pivot. The Bolsonaro conviction adds legal pressure to a political movement already weakened by Jair Bolsonaro's prior sentencing. How the broader Bolsonarista base responds ahead of the 2026 electoral cycle remains to be seen. Separately, Brazilian investigators suspect Banco Master and related entities made payments — including an apartment and concert tickets — to relatives of Senator Jaques Wagner, a senior Lula ally, deepening governance concerns.

    Regional Political Economy

    Mexico's ruling Morena party moved to annul a local election swept by centrist opposition, raising early questions about institutional responses to potential midterm losses in 2027. In Venezuela, the post-Maduro commercial reopening is accelerating — Brazilian firms dispatched a business delegation to Caracas, while US energy majors including ConocoPhillips, Chevron, and ExxonMobil are re-engaging with Venezuela and Libya, consistent with a broader Trump administration strategy of placing more global oil supply under American commercial influence. The World Bank's revised global growth forecast, cut on the back of Hormuz disruption, carries direct implications for commodity exporters across the region, though lower oil prices also represent a terms-of-trade headwind for producers including Colombia and Ecuador. ---

    Global

    • The US and Iran signed an interim memorandum of understanding Wednesday to reopen the Strait of Hormuz; WTI crude fell approximately 3% to around $74/bbl and US gasoline dipped below $4/gallon for the first time since March, though peace talks were subsequently delayed and Brent steadied near $77/bbl by Thursday.
    • Brent crude fell below $79/barrel at its weekly low — an approximately 15% decline over four sessions from pre-deal levels — as Goldman Sachs and other Wall Street banks cut price forecasts; Goldman projects near-term Hormuz flows recovering to only ~70% of pre-war levels.
    • The Fed's hawkish pivot drove the dollar's strongest two-day rally in three months, prompting Goldman Sachs to cut its 2026 gold target by $500/oz and placing EM local-currency assets under material pressure.
    • A Bloomberg diffusion index of 52 central banks shows tightening has again become the modal global outcome, with the BOJ at 1.0%, ECB signaling further hikes, and the Fed repriced for a September move.

    Hormuz: Reopening Mechanics and Caveats

    The interim accord represents a material de-escalation of the most significant energy supply disruption since the 1970s. Approximately 40 supertankers carrying roughly 80 million barrels of crude were positioned inside the Persian Gulf awaiting transit by Thursday, and at least 12 million barrels appeared to be moving through the strait on June 18 per Bloomberg ship-tracking data. However, normalisation is proving non-linear: the main internationally recognised traffic separation scheme remains partially uncleared of mines; peace talks were delayed as Lebanon ceasefire tensions flared, killing four Israeli soldiers; and shipping operators including Maersk, Hapag-Lloyd, and Mitsui OSK Lines indicated they will wait for confirmed safety conditions before transiting. Goldman Sachs projects near-term flows at approximately 70% of pre-war levels. Tehran has indicated intent to retain some traffic management role after the 60-day MOU expires, suggesting the two parties may still interpret the arrangement differently — a mid-April precedent, when a brief apparent opening collapsed within days, warrants ongoing caution.

    Nuclear Diplomacy and Structural Consequences

    The 60-day negotiating window on Iran's nuclear programme represents the central unresolved issue. Iran was operating more than 20,000 centrifuges when the conflict began — far in excess of the 5,060 permitted under the 2015 JCP

  • US

    • May CPI rose above 4.0% YoY — a three-year high — driven by energy pass-through from the Iran war, though core inflation came in below consensus, tempering the immediate policy response.
    • May non-farm payrolls printed at 170,000, beating expectations for the third consecutive month, effectively removing residual recession risk and prompting Goldman Sachs to drop its 2026 rate-cut forecast entirely.
    • SpaceX raised $75 billion on Friday in the largest IPO in US history, with shadow-market pricing implying a first-day gain of at least 35% and retail investors alone placing over $100 billion in orders.
    • The Nasdaq 100 suffered its sharpest single-session selloff since Liberation Day on Friday before staging a partial recovery; Bitcoin fell below $60,000, down more than 50% from its 2025 peak.

    Labor Market

    The May payroll print of 170,000 — meaningfully above consensus — marked the third consecutive month of solid job creation and effectively negated the Sahm Rule recession signal that had briefly emerged in 2024–2025. The strength removes the Fed's primary justification for easing, though the labor market's resilience also reflects genuine underlying demand rather than mere statistical noise. NFIB small business optimism, however, told a more nuanced story: the May reading slipped below expectations, erasing all post-election gains, with hiring plans deteriorating and capital expenditure falling to its lowest since 2009. The divergence between large-cap resilience and small-business stress is widening.

    Monetary Policy

    Markets repriced aggressively over the course of the week. Two-year Treasury yields surged to their highest level in over a year by Tuesday; overnight index swaps shifted from pricing cuts to pricing meaningful probability of hikes; and Goldman Sachs formally abandoned its 2026 rate-cut call. May CPI topping 4% YoY on Wednesday reinforced the hawkish pivot, though core's below-consensus reading kept a near-term hike off the table. Incoming Fed Chair Kevin Warsh — facing his first FOMC meeting next week — confronts persistent political pressure from President Trump, who told NBC that raising rates would be "the wrong thing to do," even as the bond market signals the opposite. Consensus now places the Fed on hold well into mid-2027.

    Equity Markets and Corporate News

    The Nasdaq 100's Friday selloff was technically overdue — the index had traded further above its 50-day moving average than at any point since 2002 — with momentum strategies bearing the brunt of unwinding. The index remained down roughly 7% from its weekly peak even after two sharp intraday recoveries, though it retained a year-to-date gain of approximately 12%. BofA's Savita Subramanian set a year-end S&P 500 target of 7,100, implying roughly 6% downside, citing 17 of 20 valuation metrics as statistically expensive. Oracle fell approximately 10% in after-hours trading Thursday after reporting elevated data center capex that disappointed near-term earnings expectations. OpenAI confidentially filed for a US IPO during the week, working with Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley on a potential fall listing. Lennar reported new orders and forward guidance below expectations, citing persistent headwinds in the housing market. Adobe fell nearly 6% Friday on CFO departure news. ---

    Asia

    • China's May exports rose 19.4% YoY on Monday, materially beating the 15% consensus, led by a 111% surge in integrated circuit sales and a 66% jump in computer shipments — the fastest chip export growth since 2013.
    • South Korea's Kospi fell more than 8% on Monday before surging 8.2% on Tuesday — 90-day volatility reaching its highest since 1998 — as AI-driven momentum reversed and then partially recovered.
    • Bank Indonesia delivered two emergency off-cycle rate hikes during the week, raising its benchmark to 5.5%, as the rupiah hit a record low and the Jakarta Composite extended year-to-date losses to nearly 40%, the worst equity performance globally.
    • Taiwan raised its 2026 GDP growth forecast to 9.6% and South Korea's current-account surplus is projected by Goldman Sachs to exceed 10% of GDP — conditions the bank described as an "AI super surplus."

    China: Trade and Domestic Pressures

    China's May trade data were the region's most significant macro release of the week, allaying fears that the Iran war is materially impeding its export engine. The $105.4 billion trade surplus — the largest since January — reflected AI-related hardware demand driving export volumes to levels not seen in years. Imports surged 27%, partly reflecting base effects. Separately, May CPI came in at 1.2% YoY on Friday, just below consensus, while PPI rose at the fastest pace in nearly four years — a configuration capturing the economy's central tension: upstream energy cost pressure from the Iran war that producers cannot pass through to weak domestic consumers. Beijing responded on multiple fronts: directing state banks to curb interbank lending to prevent bond market bubbles, publicly rebuking Alibaba and JD.com over misleading promotional claims, and advancing a reported $295 billion sovereign-funded AI data center buildout over five years.

    The AI Super Surplus

    South Korea and Taiwan are the week's most structurally significant macro story. Goldman Sachs estimated South Korea's current-account surplus could exceed 10% of GDP in 2026, with Bank of America raising its own estimate to 15% ($289 billion). SK Hynix has reportedly sold out its entire 2026 high-bandwidth memory production, predominantly to Nvidia. Taiwan's 9.6% GDP growth forecast would represent the largest export surge in 50 years. The concentration of supply capacity in these two economies, combined with US export controls constraining Chinese access, is producing an increasingly lopsided distribution of AI-cycle benefits across the region.

    Indonesia: Institutional Credibility at Risk

    Bank Indonesia's two emergency hikes — the second following a record rupiah low — reflect the severity of a market crisis that is fundamentally institutional in origin. Foreign investors have withdrawn more than $3.5 billion from Indonesian equities in 2026. Capital Economics characterized the hikes as "merely a sticking plaster," and the root cause — President Prabowo's interventionist policy signals, including direct control over commodity exports — has not materially shifted. Student protests over the cost of living added political pressure. Without a change in the underlying policy framework, rate hikes alone are unlikely to restore durable investor confidence.

    Japan and Geopolitical Signals

    Japan's May corporate goods prices rose 0.9% month-over-month, with the annual rate reaching its steepest in three years — above expectations and reinforcing the Bank of Japan's gradual normalization trajectory. China's deployment of maritime agency vessels to question foreign merchant ships near Taiwan — described by analysts as rehearsal for a potential quarantine — added geopolitical risk premium to regional assets. Xi Jinping's visit to Pyongyang, his first in seven years, signaled Beijing's intent to reassert influence over North Korea amid that government's deepening Russian alignment. ---

    Europe

    • The ECB raised its deposit rate by 25 basis points to 2.25% on Thursday — its first hike since 2023 and the first G7 rate increase since the Iran war began — with Governing Council member Nagel explicitly signaling a potential second hike in July.
    • German factory orders fell more than expected in April, consistent with a second-quarter contraction in Europe's largest economy, though industrial production grew for the first time since the conflict began.
    • UK Defence Secretary John Healey resigned Thursday over Prime Minister Starmer's failure to accelerate defence spending commitments, materially weakening the government ahead of the G7 summit.
    • Intesa Sanpaolo bid €30.6 billion for Banca Monte dei Paschi di Siena, signaling renewed appetite for Italian banking sector consolidation.

    ECB: A Consequential but Contested Decision

    The ECB's Thursday hike stands as the most significant developed-market monetary policy action of the week. Policymakers concluded that oil-driven inflation — now running well above target for more than three months — combined with evidence of broadening price pressures made further inaction untenable. President Lagarde argued the eurozone economy retains sufficient resilience to absorb tighter conditions; critics, including Berenberg's Holger Schmieding, explicitly invoked the ECB's 2011 error of hiking into a deteriorating growth environment. Markets are pricing approximately 75 basis points of total tightening by year-end, a path the consensus views as likely too aggressive given current growth dynamics. JPMorgan Asset Management called Thursday's move "one and done"; Pictet and Carmignac argued the case for holding had been legitimate. The tension between anti-inflation credibility and growth risk has not been resolved by the decision itself.

    German Economy and European Politics

    The German data remain internally contradictory. Factory orders declining more than expected in April reinforces the case for a second-quarter contraction, while industrial production growing for the first time since the Iran war began suggests stabilization rather than sustained recovery. Germany also failed this week to win a non-permanent UN Security Council seat for the first time in its history — a diplomatic signal that Foreign Minister Wadephul attributed partly to Berlin's Ukraine and Israel positions, but which appears to reflect a broader erosion of standing in the Global South. Chancellor Merz faces record disapproval ratings domestically. Austria separately lost its final AAA credit rating from a major assessor. European leaders including Starmer, Merz, and Macron convened in London with Zelenskyy and called on Putin to accept an immediate ceasefire, though Russia has not accepted direct talks.

    UK: Growth Fading, Politics Deteriorating

    April GDP data showed British economic momentum fading after a solid early-2026 start — arriving alongside Healey's resignation, which leaves Starmer vulnerable to leadership pressure ahead of NATO defence spending discussions. Bloomberg Economics' ten-year Brexit assessment, published Monday, concluded the departure has cost the UK between 2% and 4% of GDP, with a central estimate of 2.5% equating to roughly £30 billion in forgone annual tax revenue. A UK poll showing 52% of Britons now favor EU rejoining has made the topic mainstream, though the political path remains heavily contested. Chancellor Reeves separately announced plans to ease double-taxation burdens on wealthy US investors relocating to Britain — a signal of deliberate capital attraction strategy amid bilateral trade repositioning. ---

    LatAm

    • Peru's presidential election remained unresolved through the week, with leftist Roberto Sánchez holding a narrow lead of approximately 30,000 votes over conservative Keiko Fujimori; overseas ballots, expected to lean rightward, may determine the final result.
    • Argentina received an S&P credit rating upgrade, citing improved government liquidity for debt servicing — a tangible milestone for the Milei administration's fiscal consolidation program.
    • Trump's statement that he will not reauthorize USMCA sets up a protracted renegotiation with significant implications for Mexico's manufacturing-export model.

    Peru: Electoral Uncertainty and Institutional Resilience

    The presidential count's evolution — Sánchez leading after rural ballots, with diaspora votes potentially reversing the margin — has produced a risk premium on Peruvian assets that is likely to persist until mid-July adjudication. The investment implications may be more limited than the political drama suggests: Sánchez lacks the legislative support required for market-concerning structural reforms, while Fujimori's Fuerza Popular controls the largest congressional bloc. Central bank Governor Velarde's near-two-decade tenure provides an institutional anchor that arguably matters more for investor confidence than the electoral outcome. Colombia's President Petro publicly criticized Trump for expressing support for the opposition candidate, adding a geopolitical dimension to domestic political stress in the region's third-largest economy.

    Trade, Tariffs, and Bilateral Tensions

    The Trump administration's use of environmental and labor standards — Amazon deforestation, forced-labor prohibitions — as tariff justifications against Brazil represents a strategically durable framing that future US administrations would find politically difficult to unilaterally abandon. Brazil's trade minister held discussions with US Trade Representative Greer during the week to negotiate arrangements. Mexico faces a more acute structural challenge: automakers formally complained that the average tariff on Mexican auto exports stands at approximately 19%, above the 15% rate applied to some Asian competitors, while USMCA renewal uncertainty deepens the medium-term investment risk for a manufacturing sector central to the country's export model. Mexico did, however, award 37 renewable energy projects adding 7,411 megawatts of expected capacity — a constructive signal for the energy transition.

    Regional Energy and Venezuela

    Iran war disruption to global crude flows is generating consequential trade realignment within the region. Venezuelan exports to Asia are rising as commodity traders redirect flows, with India now absorbing volumes previously taken by China — providing Caracas with some fiscal support, though Trump administration sanctions maintain ongoing uncertainty. US sanctions on Cuba's state energy company threatened to block a privately arranged fuel supply deal, an acute development given the island's deepening energy crisis. Bolivia's President Paz authorized a harder crackdown on protests extending beyond five weeks, reflecting economic grievances amplified by commodity market disruption that suppression alone is unlikely to resolve. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude oscillated between $88 and $97 during the week, ultimately falling more than 4% on Thursday and Friday as ceasefire optimism grew; Trump claimed Friday that the US "ended the war with Iran," though Iran has not formally confirmed any agreement.
    • The US conducted strikes against Iranian targets on at least two consecutive days mid-week, targeting air-defense systems, surveillance networks, and communications infrastructure — representing an effective collapse of the April 8 ceasefire.
    • El Niño was officially declared by the US Climate Prediction Center, with the Japan Meteorological Agency characterizing it as potentially among the strongest on record, introducing material additional upside risk to global inflation.
    • Morgan Stanley estimates global AI hyperscaler capex at $805 billion in 2026 — 79% above 2025 levels — rising to $1.12 trillion in 2027, a demand figure that appears likely to exceed available supply capacity.

    Iran Conflict and Energy Markets

    The week's dominant global theme was the tension between renewed military escalation and nascent diplomacy. US strikes on Iranian targets on consecutive days mid-week marked an effective breakdown of the April ceasefire, yet crude markets absorbed the news with notable composure — Brent remained below $100 throughout a conflict now exceeding 100 days. Three factors appear to explain the relative stability: China drawing on commercial reserves rather than competing aggressively for spot cargoes, rising Hormuz transit signals suggesting limited flows, and market confidence that the conflict will not escalate to full-scale regional war. That confidence is an assumption, not an established fact, and constitutes the principal tail risk to the global macro outlook. Iranian crude exports to China have fallen from approximately 1.8 million barrels per day in February to around 160,000 barrels per day in May — acute fiscal pressure that may be accelerating Tehran's willingness to negotiate.   • US

    • May nonfarm payrolls — the week's defining macro release — came in at 89,000 on Friday, roughly in line with the 88,000 consensus, with unemployment steady at 4.3%; the print confirmed the labor market is cooling without yet breaking.
    • ISM Manufacturing PMI beat expectations on Monday, hitting its highest level in four years, while ISM Services input costs rose at the fastest pace in nearly four years on Wednesday — a combination that complicates the Fed's policy calculus materially.
    • PCE inflation has converged with CPI near 4% YoY, well above the Fed's 3% upper band, while real personal income has declined 0.6% over the past year — a pace Goldman Sachs associates with recession conditions.
    • The S&P 500 extended a nine-session winning streak driven almost entirely by AI-related technology names before pulling back Thursday–Friday as the Broadcom guidance miss weighed on the complex.
    • The Trump administration proposed new tariffs of at least 10% on roughly 60 trading partners on Tuesday, reviving protectionist measures under a Section 301 forced-labor framework after earlier duties were struck down by the Supreme Court.

    Labor Market

    April JOLTS data released Tuesday showed job openings surging to their highest pre-pandemic level, with vacancies once again exceeding unemployed workers — a clear beat relative to recession fears. Friday's May payrolls print of 89,000 confirmed the directional trend: the labor market is softening but not collapsing, with average hourly earnings growth tracking at 3.4% year-over-year, below prevailing inflation. The tension between resilient headline employment and deteriorating real income is the central domestic macro puzzle. Gasoline at a national average of approximately $5.70 per gallon has added roughly $200 per month to household fuel costs since late February, with lower-income cohorts substituting toward public transit — LA Metro weekday ridership is up approximately 8% year-to-date, Boston rail up 13%, Caltrain up 21% in April. The New York Fed corroborated reduced gasoline consumption among lower-income households in its May report.

    Inflation and Monetary Policy

    The ISM Services survey on Wednesday showed input costs accelerating to their fastest pace since 2022, with the Beige Book the same day describing prices rising "at a moderate to strong pace" — energy costs from the Iran conflict transmitting through freight surcharges of up to 50%, packaging, fertilizers, and groceries. The Logistics Managers' Index transport price gauge reached 96, the highest in the report's ten-year history. Fed Governor Logan flagged rising concern that rates may need to move higher; a Brookings paper by Christina and David Romer warned the Fed risks losing credibility by tolerating inflation persistently above target. New Chair Kevin Warsh's focus on trimmed-mean PCE — which is declining — provides some optionality, but with UCLA's Anderson School projecting core CPI peaking at 4.5% by year-end, the easing case has materially weakened. US money fund assets hit a record $8.3 trillion, consistent with a market pricing extended higher-for-longer.

    Trade Policy

    The administration's new tariff proposal — structured as a 10% baseline on most partners and 12.5% on China, Japan, South Korea, Brazil, and Switzerland — adds complexity to an already fragmented trade landscape. Bloomberg Economics estimates the effective US tariff rate rises by only approximately 0.5 percentage points from its current 10.7% level, assuming the expiring Section 122 duties lapse in late July without replacement. A second Section 301 probe targeting industrial overcapacity is expected shortly; analysts identify the potential stacking of these levies as the primary escalation risk. Separately, targeted reductions in steel and aluminum tariffs on select goods — cut to 15% from 25% — were announced to incentivize domestic investment, creating a bifurcated trade posture that will require careful monitoring.

    Equity and Capital Markets

    The AI fundraising cycle reached exceptional scale this week. Alphabet announced an $80 billion equity offering on Monday — including a $10 billion Berkshire Hathaway anchor — representing one of the largest equity raises in corporate history, with Goldman Sachs estimating total 2026 corporate buybacks at $1.3 trillion and new equity supply from IPOs and follow-ons at approximately $675 billion. SpaceX launched investor briefings ahead of a $75 billion IPO targeting June 12 at a $1.8 trillion valuation; Anthropic filed confidentially for a fall listing. The S&P 500's technology sector has outperformed the ex-tech index by 33% over the past three months — the strongest relative quarter on record. The Shiller CAPE ratio has exceeded its September 2000 peak, and a majority of global allocators now expect multiples to expand further. Broadcom's Thursday guidance of $16 billion in AI chip revenue for the current quarter, below consensus, triggered a roughly 14% premarket decline and pulled semiconductor names broadly lower into Friday. Bitcoin fell below $70,000, extending a decline of nearly 45% from its peak, as capital rotated into semiconductor equities. ---

    Asia

    • South Korean exports surged 61% YoY in May on a working-day-adjusted basis, with semiconductor shipments up a record 169.4% — a sharp beat that anchored the region's AI-driven narrative for the week.
    • South Korea's Kospi fell 5.5% on Friday as foreign investors sold more than $10 billion of Korean equities during the week, the steepest weekly foreign exit on record, illustrating the volatility inherent in concentration around a single demand theme.
    • China's May Caixin manufacturing PMI showed broad-based deceleration in revenues and margins, missing prior momentum, with Bloomberg Economics holding its 2026 China GDP forecast at 4.5%.
    • The Bank of Japan signaled a 25 basis point rate increase at its June meeting, yet the yen weakened back through 160 per dollar — a level that previously triggered intervention — underscoring persistent skepticism about Japan's rate trajectory.
    • Indonesia's Jakarta Composite fell as much as 5.7% Wednesday to its lowest since May 2021 after anti-corruption prosecutors raided President Prabowo Subianto's flagship free-meals program headquarters.

    China: Deceleration and Capital Controls

    China's macro picture deteriorated through the week. The Caixin PMI miss was reinforced by China Beige Book findings of weaker revenues, declining margins, soft retail sales, and continued housing disappointment — services the sole partial exception. Consumer inflation remains subdued despite producer prices rising at their fastest pace since the pandemic, reflecting demand constraints that limit pricing power. Beijing simultaneously intensified its capital outflow crackdown: regulators threatened to confiscate gains from unlicensed overseas brokers, tax authorities pursued wealthy individuals on offshore income, and reports that some banks had suspended mainland clients' Hong Kong account openings sent HSBC and AIA shares sharply lower on Friday. Citic Securities estimates the campaign could affect up to HK$250 billion of Hong Kong-domiciled assets, against a backdrop of estimated record resident outflows of $807 billion in 2025. The yuan has strengthened to a three-year high, partly reflecting exporter repatriation, though financial institutions are trimming appreciation forecasts on apparent central bank signaling. Xi Jinping is scheduled to visit Pyongyang on June 8–9 for his first trip to North Korea since 2019.

    Semiconductor Boom and Its Limits

    South Korea and Taiwan remain the clearest beneficiaries of the AI capital expenditure cycle. Taiwan revised its full-year growth forecast upward, projecting export performance at a five-decade high; TSMC's CEO stated AI chip supply will remain constrained for years despite ongoing capacity expansion. SK Hynix announced plans to double wafer capacity. Yet Broadcom's Thursday guidance miss demonstrated how quickly sentiment can reverse in a trade this concentrated: the Kospi fell 5.5% on Friday alone, and foreign investors liquidated Korean equities at a record weekly pace. Goldman Sachs nonetheless lifted its targets for both the Kospi and Taiwan's benchmark index, and Nomura frames AI hardware as a structural fourth variable in Asia's growth model alongside labor, capital, and China integration. HSBC's Frederic Neumann cautions that the boom remains narrow and any pullback in AI hardware demand would expose underlying fragilities — a risk difficult to quantify but rising as valuations extend.

    BOJ and Currency Stress

    The BOJ's apparent readiness to raise its benchmark rate toward 1% marks a remarkable inflection for a central bank that spent decades battling deflation. Governor Ueda reiterated the tightening trajectory unambiguously. Yet the yen's persistence near 160 per dollar — a level that previously triggered Ministry of Finance intervention — suggests markets remain skeptical that rate increases alone can resolve Japan's structural imbalances. Brookings' Robin Brooks attributes the weakness to Japan's debt overhang. Overseas investors have directed $73.6 billion into Japanese equities year-to-date, even as foreign selling of Korean equities stands near $70 billion — a flow pattern that reflects Japan's market breadth and corporate governance improvements rather than superior near-term earnings momentum. India coordinated RBI and government action to support the rupee, which fell to a record low mid-week, through eased foreign access to bonds and reduced capital gains taxes on FII bond investments. ---

    Europe

    • Eurozone CPI rose above 3% YoY on Tuesday for the first time in more than two and a half years, beating prior readings and cementing expectations for an ECB rate hike at the June meeting.
    • Eurozone Q1 GDP was revised on Friday to show a contraction — missing prior estimates of flat growth — principally due to a sharp decline in Irish national accounts, though the result did not appear to alter the ECB's tightening trajectory.
    • UniCredit secured commitments taking its Commerzbank stake above 30% during the week, adding significant momentum to its €38.6 billion acquisition bid.
    • Andy Burnham formally confirmed he will challenge Keir Starmer for the Labour leadership, introducing a degree of governing uncertainty into UK politics.

    ECB Policy and Inflation

    The breach of 3% eurozone CPI on Tuesday is the most consequential European data point of the week. The acceleration — driven in meaningful part by energy costs linked to the Strait of Hormuz disruption — moves the ECB into territory where further accommodation is difficult to justify. Executive Board member Isabel Schnabel's earlier statement that the bank can no longer "look through" war-driven inflationary pressures had signaled the rhetorical shift; Tuesday's print confirmed the data are moving in the same direction. Market pricing shifted decisively toward a rate hike at next week's meeting, with two additional increases priced for 2026. The growth revision announced Friday — eurozone Q1 GDP now showing a contraction rather than stagnation — is principally attributable to Irish national accounts distorted by multinational profit-shifting, a known measurement issue, and analysts do not believe it reflects genuine underlying weakness at the regional level. The tension between a contracting growth print and a tightening bias nonetheless warrants monitoring.

    Banking and Corporate News

    UniCredit crossing the 30% ownership threshold in Commerzbank during the week effectively triggers a formal takeover offer process under German law and increases pressure on Commerzbank's board and regulators to engage substantively with the €38.6 billion bid. Cross-border European bank mergers have historically encountered political resistance in Germany, and the path to completion remains genuinely uncertain, but the momentum shift is real. Separately, Akzo Nobel fell 19% in Amsterdam after Nippon Paint and Sherwin-Williams abandoned acquisition efforts simultaneously — among the sharpest single-session moves in European equities this week. Inditex gained 5.7% in Madrid on stronger second-quarter sales momentum, offering evidence that consumer spending in key European markets retains some resilience despite the inflationary backdrop. UBS raised $1.5 billion in Additional Tier 1 capital — its first such issuance since Switzerland paused plans to tighten AT1 rules — signaling a selective reopening of bank capital markets.

    UK Political Economy

    UK political risk accumulated through the week. Andy Burnham's formal entry into the Labour leadership contest introduces governing uncertainty, though markets have not yet priced a meaningful risk premium into sterling or gilts. The gilts market is on track for its third annual loss in five years; financial institutions are advocating for a retail distribution push, citing the gap between the 4%–5.5% yield on government bonds and the 1.7% average savings rate, with pension funds reducing gilt exposure and increasing reliance on overseas buyers and hedge funds. Survey evidence points to UK firms planning 4% price increases over the next year, and large retailers have shed nearly 18,000 jobs over the past 12 months — consistent with the impact of higher employer National Insurance contributions. UK house prices fell at their fastest pace in nearly a year, compounding the political headwinds facing the Starmer government alongside leaked diplomatic exchanges involving former US ambassador Peter Mandelson. ---

    LatAm

    • Colombia's first-round presidential election produced a significant surprise: right-wing outsider Abelardo de la Espriella won and heads into the June 21 runoff as the favorite, extending a regional rightward political pattern.
    • Brazil faces a dual tariff exposure — a 12.5% forced-labor levy and a separate 25% unfair trade practices rate — with President Lula immediately reframing the threat politically as "TariFlávio" ahead of elections.
    • Venezuela's oil production has surged approximately 45% in three months to around 1.2 million barrels per day following Maduro's January removal, with Exxon and ConocoPhillips in active re-entry talks and Chevron committed to raising output 50% by end-2027.
    • Mexico's USMCA-compliant goods retain their tariff exemption under the new US framework, providing structural insulation, but President Sheinbaum's hardening posture toward Washington introduces near-term political risk for nearshoring sentiment.

    Colombia: Political Realignment

    De la Espriella — a celebrity attorney running on a pro-Trump, law-and-order platform — effectively dismantled the traditional Uribista conservative movement while positioning himself against the left's Iván Cepeda in the June 21 runoff. Investors consider Cepeda more radical than outgoing President Gustavo Petro, meaning a de la Espriella victory would likely be received as market-constructive at the margin. The result extends the rightward regional pattern seen previously in Brazil, Argentina, and Chile, and would strengthen US hemispheric alignment if confirmed. Post-election rhetoric was inflammatory on both sides, underscoring political fragility ahead of the second round.

    Venezuela: Supply Diversification Premium

    Venezuela's oil trajectory has shifted materially since January. Acting President Delcy Rodríguez moved rapidly to reform oil and gas laws, signal royalty flexibility, and engage the Trump administration — which responded with cabinet-level visits. Operating costs on existing fields of approximately $8/barrel imply substantial margins at current Brent prices above $90.

    Global

    No significant coverage for this period.

  • US

    • University of Michigan consumer sentiment hit its lowest reading since the survey's 1978 inception, a sharp disconnect from equity markets at all-time highs driven almost entirely by AI semiconductor names.
    • Fed Chair Kevin Warsh, sworn in Friday, faces markets now pricing rate hikes by early 2027 — a complete reversal from the cut expectations that prevailed in late February — with Thursday's PCE print (consensus near 4%) the week's pivotal data point.
    • S&P 500 closed May up 4.9%, on pace for a ninth consecutive weekly gain, with the SOX semiconductor index contributing roughly 369 of the index's 672-point year-to-date advance; Goldman Sachs raised its year-end target to 8,000 on Tuesday.
    • Dell surged 40% in premarket Thursday on an AI server outlook that far exceeded consensus; Micron reached $1 trillion in market cap in 48 trading days — the fastest doubling on record.
    • Ken Paxton defeated John Cornyn decisively in the Texas Republican Senate runoff after securing Trump's endorsement; a little-known hard-right candidate also unseated an oil-industry-backed incumbent on the Texas Railroad Commission, introducing regulatory uncertainty into the Permian Basin.

    Equity Markets and the AI Momentum Trade

    US equities extended their extraordinary run through the week, with the S&P 500 reaching successive record closes driven by a narrow but powerful semiconductor complex. Micron Technology surged 19.3% on Tuesday, joining a ten-member club of US companies valued above $1 trillion and reaching that threshold in 48 trading days — the fastest doubling on record — underpinned by a projected earnings increase exceeding 1,200% through fiscal 2027. The concentration of returns is striking: the SOX index contributed roughly 369 points to the S&P 500's 672-point year-to-date gain, against a more modest 79 points from the remaining Magnificent Six. MSCI momentum stocks are on track for their strongest two-month outperformance versus the broad market on record. Goldman Sachs lifted its year-end S&P 500 target to 8,000 on Tuesday, citing AI tailwinds and earnings momentum, with Ed Yardeni running the highest tracked estimate at 8,250. The AI infrastructure theme continued to produce uneven outcomes across the software stack: Snowflake surged approximately 36% Wednesday after a $6 billion AWS agreement and raised guidance, lifting MongoDB (+10%) and Datadog (+5.5%), while Salesforce fell roughly 3% on a disappointing revenue forecast — illustrating the tension between monetizing AI features and the risk that AI reduces the complexity of workflows enterprise vendors serve. Dell's Thursday premarket surge of 40% simultaneously lifted Hewlett Packard Enterprise (+20%) and Super Micro Computer (+12%). The week closed with household equity exposure now above even 2000 dot-com peak levels, a dynamic Bank of America's Michael Hartnett characterized as "bubbly," noting retail mania and compressed volatility.

    Monetary Policy

    Kevin Warsh's tenure as Fed Chair began with markets wasting little time pricing its implications. The 5s30s Treasury spread compressed to its tightest level in a year, reflecting a higher-for-longer repricing at the front end, as traders assigned high probability to the first rate hike arriving by March 2027. The inaugural Bessent-Warsh breakfast produced a carefully calibrated signal: the Treasury secretary framed his expectations around Warsh doing "the right thing for inflation and growth" — invoking the statutory dual mandate rather than political objectives — and did not request rate cuts. Minneapolis Fed President Kashkari, speaking in Seoul on Thursday, reinforced the cautious stance, warning that elevated energy prices and unanchored inflation expectations preclude declaring victory. Thursday's PCE print, with consensus near 4% driven primarily by energy costs, represented the week's pivotal data point; any significant upside surprise would likely accelerate the timetable for tightening. The tension between a potential cyclical oil-price disinflationary impulse — if the Hormuz deal holds — and persistently elevated real yields leaves the Fed's near-term path genuinely uncertain.

    Consumer, Political, and Corporate Developments

    The record equity prints stand in sharp contrast to a University of Michigan consumer sentiment reading that, by Bloomberg's account, is the weakest since the survey's inception in 1978. That divergence — financial asset inflation alongside deteriorating household confidence, partly reflecting elevated energy costs from the unresolved Strait of Hormuz situation — represents a tension the market has thus far chosen to set aside. In corporate news, Gap fell 15% after cutting its sales outlook, underscoring uneven consumer spending beyond the AI complex, while IBM gained 6% on a $10 billion quantum computing commitment. The Trump administration's effective freeze on onshore wind permitting is creating measurable economic consequences, with trade groups estimating $50 billion in stranded investment risk across approximately 150,000 jobs — a policy divergence that sits uncomfortably with the strategic case for domestic energy diversification at a moment of Middle East supply disruption. The Texas Railroad Commission primary result, in which energy majors including Occidental, ExxonMobil, Chevron, and ConocoPhillips failed to protect their preferred candidate, introduces genuine regulatory uncertainty into the US's most prolific producing basin. ---

    Asia

    • Taiwan raised its 2026 GDP growth forecast to 9.6% from 7.7% on Thursday — a substantial beat relative to prior estimates — citing AI-driven semiconductor demand, as the island overtook India to become the world's fifth-largest stock market.
    • South Korea's Kospi surged 24% in May, its best monthly performance in recent memory, driven by Samsung and SK Hynix; SK Hynix surpassed $1 trillion in market capitalization on Tuesday, controlling 57% of the high-bandwidth memory market.
    • Huawei unveiled its "LogicFolding" chip architecture on Thursday, claiming 1.4nm-equivalent performance by 2031 without EUV lithography — sending SMIC and Hua Hong shares sharply higher and challenging the effectiveness of US export controls.
    • Beijing launched its most forceful crackdown on cross-border equity trading in recent years, costing Futu Holdings founder Leaf Li an estimated $1.7 billion in a single day; China also began restricting overseas travel for top AI professionals at firms including Alibaba and DeepSeek.
    • Hong Kong overtook Switzerland as the world's largest cross-border wealth hub, with $2.9 trillion in international assets under management, fueled by mainland Chinese inflows and a Hang Seng that rose 28% last year.

    Semiconductors and the AI Investment Cycle

    The dominant story across Asia this week was the extraordinary wealth and growth concentrated in semiconductor supply chains. Taiwan's GDP revision to 9.6% for 2026 — up from 7.7% — reflects an island economy that has become the physical backbone of the global AI infrastructure buildout. Taiwan's equity market overtook India in total market capitalization on Monday, driven primarily by TSMC's sustained rally. South Korea's Kospi posted a 24% monthly gain through Thursday, an extraordinary move for a major index, with the rally becoming self-reinforcing for the MSCI Emerging Markets index, which gained 7.8% in May to an all-time high. SK Hynix's crossing of the $1 trillion threshold on Tuesday — following Samsung's earlier milestone — reflects the company's 57% share of high-bandwidth memory, a component for which demand is expected to outstrip supply through at least 2027. The rally has become sufficiently concentrated that some funds are being forced to sell positions that have breached internal concentration limits, a technical dynamic that could introduce short-term volatility even as fundamentals remain supportive. Samsung's workforce separately accepted a wage agreement providing an average bonus of approximately $340,000 per chip worker, averting a strike that would have threatened near-term production.

    Huawei's LogicFolding Disclosure

    The most consequential technology development of the period was Huawei's public disclosure of its LogicFolding architecture at a chip conference in China on Thursday. Presented by semiconductor chief He Tingbo, the technology seeks to improve chip performance by shortening internal data-transmission distances rather than shrinking transistors — an approach designed explicitly to circumvent China's inability to procure ASML's EUV machines. The company targets 1.4nm-equivalent chip production by 2031, roughly three years behind TSMC's current roadmap. Bloomberg Intelligence notes that if commercially viable, this would help Chinese AI firms maintain competitiveness despite Nvidia export restrictions. Material engineering hurdles remain — heat dissipation, manufacturing complexity, and economic scale — but the market reaction was immediate. ByteDance's separate discussions around $70 billion in capital expenditure for 2026 signal an equally aggressive posture; Taiwan's tech firms are borrowing a record $14.5 billion to fund their own expansion. The disclosure reinforces an argument previously advanced by Nvidia's Jensen Huang: export controls may be accelerating rather than halting Chinese semiconductor self-sufficiency.

    China Capital Controls and Geopolitics

    Beijing's crackdown on illicit cross-border stock trading — described as the most forceful in recent years — sent an unambiguous signal about the authorities' intent to arrest capital outflows that reached an estimated $1 trillion in 2025. Separately, travel restrictions on leading AI professionals represent an escalation in Beijing's effort to prevent talent diffusion to Western firms. The yuan is on course for a sixth consecutive quarterly gain against the dollar, its longest winning streak since 2013, with the People's Bank appearing comfortable allowing appreciation as a signal of economic confidence. Chinese Foreign Minister Wang Yi visited Ottawa — the first such visit in a decade — reflecting a deliberate diplomatic realignment as Canada diversifies away from US dependency. In the South China Sea, China expelled a Dutch naval frigate near the Paracel Islands on Wednesday, and ship-tracking data revealed intensifying coast guard activity around the Spratly Islands, maintaining a persistent Indo-Pacific friction point even as geopolitical attention remains concentrated on the Gulf.

    Japan, South Korea Policy, and Regional Divergence

    Japan's net external asset position reached a record ¥561.8 trillion at end-2025, though the country slipped to third-largest creditor nation as China's position grew faster. Japan's two-year bond yield rose Friday after a lukewarm auction, extending pressure on JGB markets that has drawn renewed intervention warnings from Finance Minister Katayama. The Bank of Korea's incoming governor signaled a hawkish pivot on Wednesday, pointing to rate increases ahead — a meaningful shift for a central bank that spent much of 2024-25 in an easing posture, with energy-driven inflation pressures appearing to drive the recalibration. Singapore's Ministry of Trade reported Q1 GDP growth of 1.0% quarter-on-quarter, well ahead of the 0.2% consensus, but forward guidance struck a notably cautious tone, identifying Middle East energy disruption, US tariff escalation, and a possible AI capex correction as meaningful downside scenarios. The contrast between strong first-quarter momentum and deteriorating forward visibility has become a recurring theme across the region. ---

    Europe

    • French and Spanish CPI accelerated Thursday to the fastest pace since 2024, above consensus, reinforcing the case for an ECB rate hike — even as France's economy simultaneously contracted in Q1, presenting the ECB with a direct stagflationary tension.
    • ECB Governing Council members including Isabel Schnabel called for a June rate hike, while Bund yields and inflation breakevens fell sharply Monday on Hormuz deal optimism before partially recovering on renewed US strikes; markets ended the week with three hikes priced for 2026.
    • A Romanian apartment building in Galați was struck by a Russian drone that entered NATO airspace on Thursday, the most serious such incident for the alliance's eastern flank since the Ukraine war began.
    • BP Chairman Albert Manifold disputed the company's account of his abrupt dismissal, deepening uncertainty around the UK oil major's strategic direction; a Bloomberg investigation found companies in at least nine European countries purchased fraudulent Chinese carbon credits.
    • The EU revived its Chips Act with a rebooted framework requiring an estimated €120 billion in public-private investment by 2035, a direct response to the semiconductor race being driven by US and Chinese capital.

    ECB Policy: A Genuine Dilemma

    The ECB's policy path crystallized into its most difficult configuration of the current cycle this week. Two Governing Council members, including Isabel Schnabel, called for a rate hike at the June meeting, and President Lagarde confirmed the bank will revise its inflation projections upward. The case for tightening was reinforced Thursday when CPI accelerated in France, Spain, and Italy to the fastest rates since 2024 — largely reflecting energy price pass-through from the Middle East conflict. Yet France's Q1 GDP contraction, reported the same day, presents the precise scenario that most complicates the ECB's task: inflation overshoot alongside weak growth. The conditionality in the Governing Council's language — a hike "unless a durable peace deal is reached" — introduces genuine two-way risk to the rate path. Bund yields and inflation expectations fell sharply Monday on thin holiday volumes as Hormuz deal optimism briefly dominated, before partially reversing as fresh US strikes on Iranian targets pushed energy prices back toward $97. Governing Council member Villeroy's observation that second-round inflation effects have not yet materialized sits in tension with Schnabel's more hawkish framing, and the ECB's semi-annual Financial Stability Review warned Wednesday that asset prices remain "stretched by historical standards," with geopolitical risks appearing underestimated by investors.

    UK Political Economy

    The UK's political situation continues to deteriorate in ways that carry medium-term economic significance. Commentary from Westminster suggests the Starmer administration is entering a period of effective policymaking paralysis as the leadership question remains unresolved, with Andy Burnham's potential candidacy drawing attention from fixed-income investors alert to any leftward fiscal shift during a leadership contest. The more immediate economic concern is consumer price pressure: over 80% of British food and drink manufacturers surveyed indicated they plan to raise prices, with energy cost pass-through from the Hormuz disruption the primary driver. A government-commissioned review warned of a "lost generation" of young unemployed workers, attributing the deterioration to surging employer national insurance contributions, worsening youth mental health, and AI-driven displacement of entry-level roles — structural drivers unlikely to self-correct through the cycle. Reform UK's position as Britain's most popular party by polling adds a longer-horizon policy risk: the party's deputy leader has outlined plans to dismantle net-zero commitments, which would create material discontinuity risk for UK renewable energy investments and carbon market instruments if translated into electoral power.

    Carbon Markets and EU Industrial Policy

    A Bloomberg investigation found that companies in at least nine European countries — including BP, Shell, TotalEnergies, EDF, ExxonMobil, and OMV — purchased carbon credits from Chinese projects in the EU's upstream emissions reduction scheme that appear to have been fraudulent or non-existent. German authorities have voided credits from 30 projects claiming 2.1 million tons of CO₂ savings. No criminal penalties are expected for the purchasing companies, as acquisitions were made in good faith, but the episode raises fundamental questions about the governance architecture of European carbon markets ahead of the EU's planned expansion of credit-based mechanisms toward its 2040 emissions target. The European Commission's rebooted Chips Act, targeting €120 billion in investment by 2035 in public-private partnership, reflects a strategic response to the semiconductor race — and specifically to Huawei's LogicFolding disclosure — that Brussels regards as a structural competitive threat requiring industrial policy intervention rather than market-driven adjustment alone. Oxford Economics published analysis quantifying a further upside risk to ECB inflation forecasts: severe climate-driven food supply disruptions could raise eurozone headline inflation by up to 0.6 percentage points annually, a supply-side risk that conventional monetary tools are poorly designed to address. ---

    LatAm

    • The US announced it will designate Brazil's two largest criminal organizations — Primeiro Comando da Capital and Comando Vermelho — as terrorist entities, a move likely to reignite bilateral tensions with President Lula and carries indirect implications for Brazilian sovereign spreads and the real.
    • Industrial metals posted their best monthly performance since January, providing terms-of-trade improvement for Chile, Peru, and Colombia at a moment of tighter global financial conditions.
    • Brazil's President Lula is actively stimulating the economy ahead of elections, according to Bloomberg Economics, raising questions about fiscal sustainability and medium-term inflation dynamics.
    • Venezuela opposition leader María Corina Machado signaled willingness to negotiate a path to presidential elections, a potential opening backed by Washington; more than half of Venezuelans support full dollarization as inflation runs near 600%.

    Brazil: Bilateral Friction and Fiscal Risk

    The US designation of Brazil's two largest criminal organizations as terrorist entities is the most consequential LatAm development of the period. The move gives US law enforcement expanded tools to pursue international financial flows linked to PCC and CV, but carries significant diplomatic cost: Lula has consistently resisted framing Brazilian organized crime through a counterterrorism lens, and any perception of US interference in domestic security matters is politically toxic in Brasília. The relationship between Trump and Lula was already strained by trade policy and ideological divergence; this designation adds a new source of bilateral friction at a moment when Brazil is deepening commodity export relationships with China. The macro implications are indirect but real — political turbulence in Brazil historically widens sovereign spreads and weakens the real, though the magnitude of any market reaction depends on whether the designation produces measurable policy escalation from either side. The fiscal backdrop complicates the picture further: Bloomberg Economics characterizes Lula's pre-election stimulus as a deliberate effort to boost activity ahead of the vote. While the near-term demand impulse may support growth data, the strategy risks widening Brazil's already elevated fiscal deficit and adding to inflationary pressures at a point when the Banco Central do Brasil has been working to anchor expectations. The tension between electoral fiscal expansion and monetary credibility is a recurring dynamic in Brazilian political economy, and markets will likely price additional risk premium into Brazilian rates if the stimulus trajectory extends beyond what primary balance targets can accommodate.

    Venezuela and Regional Political Economy

    Machado's signal of willingness to negotiate on a path to presidential elections represents a meaningful shift in tone, suggesting Washington's pressure campaign may be producing marginal movement. The context is an economy in severe distress: inflation running near 600% has produced majority public support for full dollarization — a measure that would eliminate the inflation tax but remove the government's ability to monetize deficits, a constraint Venezuela's fiscal structure has historically been unable to absorb without substantial external support. The broader regional picture is shaped by the commodity cycle: industrial metals' best monthly performance since January provides genuine terms-of-trade relief for Chile, Peru, and Colombia, offering some offset to the tighter global financial conditions implied by higher US rates and the Hormuz-driven energy shock. China's commercial outreach in the Western Hemisphere is simultaneously broadening — Beijing's rapprochement with Canada, including reduced EV tariffs exchanged for lower agricultural levies, is a reminder that Latin American producers of agricultural commodities and resources face evolving competition dynamics in key export markets. Colombia's approaching presidential election, with a leftist candidate polling as frontrunner, adds to a cluster of EM political risk events that Oxford Economics' quarterly survey identified as contributing to the sharpest deterioration in emerging-market conditions since late 2022. ---

    Global

    • A tentative 60-day US-Iran ceasefire extension was reached Thursday, pending Trump's approval; Brent crude fell approximately 19% in May — its steepest monthly decline since 2020 — though fresh US strikes on Iranian targets mid-week nearly reversed Monday's 7% single-session drop before the truce language stabilized markets.
    • Anthropic raised $65 billion at a $965 billion valuation Thursday, surpassing OpenAI; Apollo and Blackstone are assembling a $36 billion private credit facility to finance Google TPUs leased to Anthropic, effectively transforming advanced semiconductors into a financeable asset class.
    • Russia's GDP contracted in the most recent quarter for the first time in three years, with Bloomberg Economics cutting the 2026 growth forecast to 0.4% from 1.3%; Russia launched a major assault on Ukraine mid-week involving a hypersonic Oreshnik ballistic missile and approximately 90 missiles and 600 drones.
    • Gold shed nearly 20% from its late-January peak and traded around $4,490 on Wednesday, with UBS trimming its year-end forecast to $5,500; the metal's inability to sustain a safe-haven bid despite ongoing geopolitical risk reflects rising real yields and unwind of 2025 speculative positioning.
    • The Pentagon's Indo-Pacific command submitted a 121-page report to Congress warning that the PLA is targeting Taiwan readiness by 2027 and requesting billions for asymmetric sea-denial and hypersonic capabilities.

    Strait of Hormuz: Costly Stasis, Tentative Resolution

    The trajectory of US-Iran negotiations remained the single most consequential variable for global markets across the week. The sequence illustrated the gap between headline optimism and operational reality: Monday's reports of a potential deal drove Brent crude down more than 7% in thin holiday trading; fresh US Central Command strikes on Iranian missile launch sites and mine-laying vessels Wednesday pushed prices back toward $97; and Thursday's tentative agreement on a 60-day ceasefire extension produced a further leg down that left Brent on course for its largest monthly decline since 2020. Trump has not yet formally approved the terms, and Iran's Foreign Ministry earlier in the week explicitly stated that "no one can claim that the signing of an agreement is imminent." Secretary Rubio acknowledged talks could take several more days. Republican hawks including Senator Graham have argued the emerging terms concede too much to Tehran, while Iran's Supreme Leader Khamenei declared that US military bases in the region will no longer be safe — language that complicates any clean resolution. The longer the stasis continues, the greater the risk that second-order economic effects — food and energy inflation, shipping cost pass-through — accumulate to a degree that becomes politically difficult to manage even without a market-based forcing event. China appears to be playing a quiet but consequential mediation role, with Pakistani Army Chief Munir shuttling between Beijing and Doha; the reported possibility of Iran's highly enriched uranium being transferred to China would represent an unusual expansion of Beijing's direct involvement in Middle Eastern security arrangements.

    AI Capital Flows and the Infrastructure Supercycle

    The scale of capital mobilizing around artificial intelligence infrastructure approached macro-relevant magnitudes this week. Anthropic's $65 billion fundraise at a $965 billion valuation — eclipsing OpenAI for the first time — was accompanied by a $36 billion private credit facility assembled by Apollo and Blackstone to finance AI chip procurement, effectively transforming advanced semiconductors into a financeable asset class with characteristics more akin to infrastructure than venture capital. ByteDance is in discussions around $70 billion in capital expenditure for 2026. Taiwan's tech firms are borrowing a record $14.5 billion. The Quad — US, Japan, India, Australia — committed up to $20 billion in support for critical-minerals supply chain development. These flows are compressing risk premia in private credit, concentrating equity gains in AI-adjacent equities globally, and creating feedback loops between chip demand, energy infrastructure requirements, and sovereign growth forecasts that are still being incorporated into official projections.

    Russia: War Economy Under Strain

    Russia's economy presents an unusual configuration: a wartime state running relatively orthodox macroeconomic policy. The Bank of Russia's benchmark rate at 14.5% implies real rates above 9% given sub-6% consumer price inflation — among the highest real rates globally. The Economy Ministry cut its 2026 growth forecast to 0.4% from 1.3%, with the non-oil private sector — roughly three-quarters of the economy — stalling under tight monetary conditions and a labor shortfall estimated at approximately 1.5 million workers. Russia's mid-week use of a hypersonic Oreshnik ballistic missile in a mass strike on Ukrainian cities represents a continued escalation in the quality of munitions deployed even as front lines remain relatively static. Foreign Minister Lavrov's call to Secretary Rubio advising evacuation of US personnel from Kyiv signals Moscow's intent to maintain pressure on Ukrainian decision-making infrastructure. Russia's consideration of restricting diesel and jet fuel exports — a potential response to Ukrainian drone strikes on refinery infrastructure — would add a further layer of complexity to already strained global energy markets.

    Geopolitical Architecture in Realignment

    Beyond the primary theater of the Iran conflict, several developments this week signal a global order in active reconfiguration. The Pentagon's Indo-Pacific command report frames Taiwan preparedness through the same maritime chokepoint lens sharpened by the Hormuz experience, requesting billions for asymmetric sea-denial capabilities with a 2027 urgency benchmark. China expelled a Dutch naval frigate near the Paracel Islands Wednesday while coast guard activity around the Spratlys intensified, maintaining persistent Indo-Pacific friction beneath the headlines. The China-Canada diplomatic rapprochement — Wang Yi's first Ottawa visit in a decade — reflects Beijing's deliberate effort to widen commercial relationships in the Western Hemisphere as the US-China strategic competition intensifies. Russia's GDP contraction and the Romanian drone incident on Thursday, which Bucharest called a "serious and irresponsible escalation," collectively underscore that the global geopolitical risk premium, while partly priced through energy markets, carries dimensions in equity and credit valuations that appear underweighted relative to the breadth of active stress points.

  • US

    • The 30-year Treasury yield touched 5.19% on Tuesday — the highest since June 2007 — as Hormuz-driven inflation pressures overwhelmed the prior easing narrative, with futures markets by week's end pricing roughly a 70–75% probability of a Fed hike by December.
    • Kevin Warsh was sworn in as Federal Reserve Chair on Friday, inheriting an FOMC that had already shifted hawkish: minutes released Wednesday showed "almost all" participants expected persistent energy-driven inflation and "many" preferred dropping the easing bias entirely.
    • Nvidia reported 85% revenue growth and an $80 billion buyback authorization on Wednesday, though shares dipped after hours on underwhelming guidance; SpaceX filed its S-1 targeting up to $75 billion in proceeds, disclosing $18.7 billion in 2025 revenue.
    • NextEra announced a $67 billion acquisition of Dominion Energy on Monday — the largest US utility deal on record — driven by data-center power demand across Florida and Virginia.
    • Senate Republicans rebuffed Trump's $1.8 billion "weaponization" account request on Friday, a notable intra-party setback, while Moody's downgraded Mexico to one notch above junk, introducing North American credit and trade exposure concerns.

    Bond Markets and the Rate Outlook

    The week's defining story in US markets was the continued repricing of the long end. The 30-year Treasury yield reached 5.19% on Tuesday, a level last seen in June 2007, while 10-year yields touched 4.63% on Monday — the highest since February. The drivers were mutually reinforcing: war-driven inflation, with April headline CPI at 3.8% year-on-year, the firmest since 2023; persistent fiscal supply; AI-related capital expenditure; and continued labor market resilience. Citigroup's Jim McCormick flagged 5.5% as the next technical focal point. Barclays' Ajay Rajadhyaksha argued that only weaker macro data was likely to arrest the move absent a Hormuz reopening, and HSBC characterized a 2026 Fed hike as a tail risk that "can't be ruled out." The effect on equity markets was bifurcated: large-cap AI names remained broadly bid, while options activity in the Russell 2000 showed the first meaningful pickup in hedging demand in six weeks. BofA's fund manager survey, released mid-week, showed a record monthly jump in equity allocations and the sharpest cash drawdown since February 2024 — readings strategist Michael Hartnett characterized as near-complete "bull capitulation," with early June flagged as a potential inflection.

    The New Fed Chair

    Kevin Warsh's swearing-in on Friday transferred the chairmanship at an awkward moment. Wednesday's FOMC minutes — Powell's final meeting — showed a committee whose consensus had shifted materially beneath the incoming chair, with a majority signaling some firming may be appropriate if inflation runs persistently above 2%. Futures now price fed funds above 4% next summer, against 2.8% pricing in late February. UBS's Jonathan Pingle suggested Warsh may lean on trimmed-mean inflation and unit labor costs to frame a delay in hikes, and ABN Amro analysts floated the possibility of changes to the SEP cadence and press conference schedule within six to twelve months. Jamie Dimon, speaking to Bloomberg Television during the week, warned rates "could be much higher than they are today." The realistic ceiling on Warsh's dovishness appears to be holding, rather than cutting.

    Corporate Developments

    Nvidia's Wednesday print — 85% revenue growth, 62% gross margins, $78.9 billion in revenue — met but did not exceed elevated expectations, and the stock's after-hours dip illustrated the challenge of sustaining momentum at a Shiller CAPE of 39.6. Hyperscaler capex projections for 2026 and 2027 were revised to $812 billion and $968 billion respectively, up 89% year-to-date.
