SpaceX's filing, reportedly led by Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley, disclosed a $4.94 billion net loss alongside the revenue figure, a super-voting structure, and performance milestones tied to a Mars colony. NextEra's $67 billion Dominion acquisition — announced Monday — positions the combined entity to capture data-center load growth in Florida and Virginia with battery storage central to the strategy. Google and Blackstone announced a $5 billion initial equity commitment for an AI cloud venture, scaling to roughly $25 billion including leverage, which weighed on CoreWeave, Nebius and Applied Digital. Meta reassigned 7,000 employees to AI roles ahead of broader cuts. Separately, Brookings data showed 82% of Los Angeles County businesses reported negative impacts from ICE enforcement operations, with concentrated damage to small firms a potential early indicator of broader policy-driven disruption.

    Fiscal and Trade

    The EU finalized the text of its US trade deal on Tuesday, capping EU export tariffs at 15% in exchange for removal of EU duties on US industrial goods — averting a threatened rise to 25% on European automobiles ahead of the July 4 deadline. The post-summit US-China framework remains thin: $17 billion in annual agricultural purchases through 2028 is the most concrete commitment, with Section 232-style investigations queued to replace tariffs struck down in February. A fresh round of duties this summer could collide with gasoline prices near $4.50 and affordability concerns ahead of the midterms. Trump reversed course and announced 5,000 additional troops for Poland on Friday, while the Pentagon confirmed a separate permanent reduction of roughly 4,000 troops from Europe announced earlier in the week. ---

    Asia

    • Nvidia's earnings and SpaceX's IPO filing triggered a historic Asian tech rally on Thursday: South Korea's Kospi surged 8.4% and Taiwan's Taiex rose 3.4%, with Korean chip exports up 202% year-on-year in the first twenty days of May.
    • Samsung averted a general strike on Wednesday after reaching a deal with its largest union — potentially distributing roughly ₩40 trillion ($26.6 billion) in AI-linked bonuses averaging $340,000 per chip employee — while SK Hynix's market capitalization reached $910 billion.
    • China's April data, released Monday, delivered a broad miss: retail sales rose 0.2% year-on-year, industrial production grew 4.1% (the weakest in nearly three years), and fixed-asset investment contracted 1.6% year-to-date, reigniting the stimulus debate.
    • Bank Indonesia surprised with a 50 basis point rate hike to 5.25% on Wednesday, while President Prabowo announced state centralization of palm oil, coal, and nickel exports — a move that blindsided senior officials and extended the Jakarta Composite's year-to-date decline to roughly 26%, the worst among major global benchmarks.
    • Vladimir Putin arrived in Beijing on Monday for a state visit focused on Power of Siberia 2 pricing and energy cooperation, though the structural leverage favors Xi given Russia's isolation and dependence on Chinese demand.

    China: Growth Disappointment and Post-Summit Ambiguity

    The week opened with April activity data that fell short across every major indicator. Retail sales rose just 0.2% year-on-year — the softest print since the post-Covid reopening of December 2022 — with auto sales down 15% and jewelry off 21%. Industrial production at 4.1% was the weakest in nearly three years, and fixed-asset investment unexpectedly contracted 1.6% year-to-date, a result no Bloomberg-surveyed economist had anticipated. The figures arrived against the backdrop of the post-Trump-Xi summit narrative, which produced a commitment to at least $17 billion in annual US agricultural purchases through 2028 but little operational detail on the broader trade framework. The "placeholder summit" characterization increasingly resonates: Beijing's reluctance to add domestic stimulus is now harder to sustain given the breadth of weakness, and the trade board established by the summit appears to cover only roughly $30 billion in flows. Capital flows nonetheless continue to favor China, with domestic banks receiving $61.9 billion in net inflows on behalf of clients in April — the sixth month of inflows in seven — though exchanges are reportedly scrutinizing AI-driven rallies and the so-called "national team" is cutting ETF holdings by roughly 90% in the first half of the year, suggesting officials are managing froth rather than endorsing the advance.

    The AI Rally and Korea's Concentration Risk

    Thursday's Nvidia-driven surge crystallized Asia's structural position in the AI supply chain. Samsung and TSMC have together added approximately $1.2 trillion in market value year-to-date, and South Korean export data for May 1–20 showed chip shipments up 202% year-on-year, lifting the broader trade balance into surplus. Japan posted a third consecutive monthly trade surplus as semiconductor exports rose 44%, partly offsetting a 64% collapse in crude import volumes reflecting Hormuz disruptions. The gains, however, sit alongside notable concentration risk: Korea's Kospi year-to-date gain of roughly 73% has been driven almost entirely by Samsung and SK Hynix, and retail margin debt reached a record ₩36.5 trillion ($24 billion) earlier in the week before Samsung's labor settlement provided some relief. Oxford Economics frames the resulting external surpluses as a narrower echo of the 2000s Asian savings glut, with capital recycling indirectly supporting US asset prices even as Treasury yields press higher.

    Indonesia's Policy Turn and Regional Stress

    Bank Indonesia's 50 basis point hike — double consensus expectations — and President Prabowo's sweeping reassertion of state control over commodity exports together represent the week's most consequential policy shift in emerging Asia. The export centralization through Danantara echoes earlier authoritarian-era practices and is likely to unsettle foreign buyers of Indonesian coal and palm oil, even as Jakarta frames the move as targeting under-invoicing. A Moody's warning over centralization risks followed. India faces analogous pressures: the rupee touched nearly 97 per dollar during the week and the Reserve Bank of India is reportedly weighing its 2013 taper-tantrum toolkit, including rate hikes and expanded FX swaps. The Philippines, Thailand, and Malaysia each face secondary effects from elevated energy costs — Thai and Philippine fishing fleets are idling on diesel prices, and Thai authorities have prepared a 200 billion baht ($6.1 billion) cost-of-living package.

    Japan and the BOJ Dilemma

    Japan's Q1 GDP expanded an annualized 2.1%, accelerating from a revised 0.8%, on stronger consumption and trade — a positive surprise that nonetheless sat alongside mounting fiscal and monetary tensions. PM Takaichi, after weeks of insisting a supplementary budget was unnecessary, reversed course and instructed the finance ministry to compile one, targeting roughly $19 billion including revived energy subsidies. The move pushed 30-year JGB yields to fresh post-1999 highs, with Finance Minister Katayama publicly flagging markets as a policy risk. Core inflation, meanwhile, slowed to a four-year low — ostensibly providing the BOJ room to pause — though a BOJ official signaled support for a near-term hike, placing June increasingly in play. Pimco has turned constructive on the Japanese long end, though the combination of fiscal expansion and tightening monetary bias remains an uncomfortable juxtaposition for JGB markets. ---

    Europe

    • The EU finalized the text of its US trade deal on Tuesday, averting an increase in auto tariffs from 15% to 25% ahead of Trump's July 4 deadline, though the pact formalizes a less favorable arrangement than the pre-tariff status quo; eurozone exports to the US fell 37% year-on-year in March.
    • The eurozone composite PMI fell to 47.5 in May from 48.8, marking the sharpest contraction in two and a half years, with France's reading plunging to its lowest since 2020 while Germany was broadly stable.
    • UK M&A activity has risen more than 250% year-to-date to roughly $150 billion — the strongest pace since 2015 — as overseas acquirers exploit depressed sterling valuations, with Unilever, Schroders, Beazley, Intertek and Tate & Lyle among the names in play.
    • ECB Governor Pierre Wunsch signaled a likely June rate hike as Iran-driven energy costs reshape the inflation trajectory, while Emmanuel Moulin was confirmed as Banque de France governor, removing one source of policy uncertainty.
    • Standard Chartered announced plans to eliminate roughly 8,000 support roles over four years through AI integration, while Germany formally launched the privatization of Uniper, potentially Europe's largest utility transaction this year.

    ECB Policy and the Inflation Dilemma

    The week's most consequential European policy development was the crystallization of an ECB tightening bias. Belgian Governor Wunsch indicated a June hike was likely, Emmanuel Moulin reinforced that the ECB must be "ready to act," and at least one additional Governing Council member kept the door open to further moves. ECB President Lagarde maintained that medium-term inflation expectations remain anchored, though her messaging sat uneasily against G7 long-bond yields touching two-decade highs and the EU's economic affairs chief stating the ECB "will have to respond." The shift marks a notable repricing from the easing trajectory priced earlier in the cycle, and leaves the ECB navigating a deteriorating growth backdrop simultaneously — the composite PMI's fall to 47.5 on Thursday confirmed the bloc is contracting at its fastest pace in two and a half years. France's reading was particularly weak, falling to levels last seen in 2020, while Germany showed more resilience. Rapidan Energy's assessment that a Hormuz disruption persisting through August could push Brent toward $130 and deliver a stagflationary impulse comparable in scale to past oil downturns represents the tail scenario the ECB appears least equipped to handle.

    UK Politics and Gilts

    UK political dynamics remained a persistent source of noise throughout the week. Health secretary Wes Streeting's resignation and subsequent platform-building for a Labour leadership bid, combined with Greater Manchester Mayor Andy Burnham's announcement of a by-election route to challenging Keir Starmer, prompted hedge funds and asset managers to build bearish sterling option positions early in the week. Burnham's mid-week commitment to preserve existing fiscal borrowing limits offered some reassurance to gilt markets, and UK CPI data released on Wednesday fell to its lowest reading in more than a year — a divergence from euro-area inflation dynamics that prompted traders to pare Bank of England hike expectations. Chancellor Rachel Reeves moved separately on multiple fronts: abandoning a planned fuel-duty increase (complicating the fiscal arithmetic at a sensitive moment for gilts), preparing cost-of-living measures including free summer bus travel for children, concluding a free-trade agreement with the Gulf Cooperation Council, and closing a corporate tax loophole used by energy producers projected to yield roughly £300 million annually. The Foreign Office simultaneously announced job cuts that would roughly halve its Iran-related team — a curious resource decision given the conflict's third month of disruption. Reform UK's overtaking of the Conservatives as the principal party of the British Right, with deputy leader Richard Tice warning investors against participating in the offshore wind AR7 auction, introduces a meaningful policy risk premium for UK green infrastructure assets that warrants reassessment.

    EU-US Trade and Corporate News

    EU lawmakers and member states reached agreement on the US trade deal text on Tuesday, clearing a procedural hurdle that averts auto tariffs rising to 25%. The outcome offers near-term relief but formalizes a less favorable arrangement than the pre-tariff baseline — eurozone export data underscoring the stakes, with March shipments to the US down 37% year-on-year. Separately, the EU is weighing defensive measures against Chinese import volumes ahead of a June leaders' summit, with Beijing signaling retaliation against the pending Nuctech subsidy probe. In corporate news, Publicis rose 2.4% on Monday after agreeing to acquire LiveRamp for approximately $2.5 billion; Marks & Spencer guided full-year profit above pre-cyberattack levels and gained 4.7% on Wednesday; Ubisoft fell 14% on Thursday after guiding to declining sales; and the Estée Lauder–Puig tie-up around Charlotte Tilbury collapsed over compensation terms on Friday, sending Puig shares sharply lower. Germany's climate advisory council warned the country is likely to miss its 2030 emissions targets by up to 100 million tons of CO2, while Germany formally launched Uniper's privatization. Chancellor Merz proposed associate EU membership for Ukraine, a formulation Kyiv has previously received with limited enthusiasm. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude traded in a wide range through the week — from above $111 on Monday to near $105 by Friday — as US-Iran diplomatic signals alternated between cautious optimism and renewed threat, with the Strait of Hormuz remaining largely closed for a third consecutive month.
    • G7 long-bond yields reached two-decade highs, with the 30-year US Treasury touching 5.19% on Tuesday and Japan's super-long yields hitting post-1999 records, as the energy shock overwhelmed the disinflationary forces central banks had been counting on.
    • Vladimir Putin arrived in Beijing on Monday seeking better terms on Power of Siberia 2, but structural leverage favors Xi: Russia's economy is heavily dependent on Chinese demand, Sunday's record Ukrainian drone strike on Moscow underscored vulnerabilities, and Xi had just completed his own summit with Trump.
    • Gulf sovereign wealth funds are turning materially inward — Saudi Arabia's PIF cut its targeted foreign asset allocation to 18% from near 50%, and war-related costs equivalent to roughly 1.5% of Saudi GDP in the first month are compressing the petrodollar recycling pool that has historically supported global asset prices.
    • Scientists formally retired the RCP8.5 worst-case warming scenario as implausible; BloombergNEF's New Energy Outlook projects 2.4°C warming by 2050 under current trajectories, with solar set to become the largest single power source by 2032.

    The Hormuz Stalemate and Its Macro Consequences

    The US-Iran standoff entered its third month without diplomatic resolution, though the week produced more movement than most. President Trump called off planned strikes Monday after Saudi Arabia and Gulf allies requested additional time for diplomacy, sending Brent 1.8% lower to around $110. By Wednesday, Iran's Foreign Ministry confirmed it was reviewing a US response to Tehran's 14-point proposal — which envisions a short-term arrangement reopening the strait in exchange for lifting the port blockade, followed by deeper nuclear negotiations — and Trump told reporters Washington was in the "final stages." Brent fell 5.6% on Wednesday on those signals. The relief proved partial: Supreme Leader comments insisting Tehran retain its uranium stockpile and a dispute over a proposed Iran-Oman toll regime in the strait complicated the picture, and by Friday Brent had recovered to above $105 with Trump reiterating the threat of renewed strikes. Goldman Sachs estimates visible global crude and product inventories drew down at a record 8.7 million barrels per day in May, nearly double the conflict's running average — a rate that, if sustained, points to demand destruction rather than supply restoration as the rebalancing mechanism. Rapidan Energy's assessment that a disruption persisting through August could push Brent toward $130 with macroeconomic consequences comparable to 2008 represents a tail scenario the diplomatic track has not yet foreclosed. Physical workarounds are advancing: the UAE is accelerating pipeline construction to Fujairah to double bypass capacity, Singapore's state LNG buyer has secured non-Middle East cargoes through year-end, and NATO is reportedly discussing vessel escort options if the strait remains closed into July.

    The Bond Market as Policy Signal

    The global sovereign bond selloff deepened through the week before partially stabilizing on Iran diplomatic signals. Bloomberg's gauge of average sovereign yields reached its highest level since July 2008, with US 30-year yields touching 5.19% on Tuesday, Japanese super-long yields hitting post-1999 records following PM Takaichi's fiscal reversal, and UK gilts pricing political risk around a potential Burnham premiership. The drivers extend beyond oil: sticky underlying inflation, AI-related capital expenditure, deteriorating fiscal trajectories, and demographic pressures are reinforcing one another in a way that distinguishes this episode from a simple energy shock. The FOMC minutes released Wednesday showed "almost all" participants expected persistent energy-driven inflation and "many" preferred dropping the easing bias. Bank Indonesia delivered a surprise 50 basis point hike on Wednesday to defend the rupiah. The ECB signaled a likely June hike. The RBA has already delivered three hikes this year. TS Lombard's Dario Perkins characterized the environment as potentially heralding "rate hikes everywhere," with 2% targets functioning increasingly as a floor rather than a ceiling. The tension between that tightening bias and Pimco's observation that prior energy shocks have historically culminated in recession remains the defining macro ambiguity heading into summer.

    Gulf Capital Flows and Petrodollar Recycling

    A slower-moving but potentially consequential structural shift emerged more clearly through the week's analysis: the contraction of the petrodollar recycling mechanism. Saudi Arabia's first-quarter budget reflected roughly $17 billion in war-related costs, equivalent to 1.5% of GDP. Qatar faces potential losses of $20 billion from damaged gas infrastructure that could take five years to restore. The PIF had already cut its targeted foreign asset allocation to 18% from a prior ambition near 50% before the conflict began; the war is accelerating the inward turn, with LIV Golf preparing for possible bankruptcy after PIF withdrew funding and a $200 million Metropolitan Opera commitment abandoned. Saudi Arabia is now pursuing a $35 billion Aramco privatization to court domestic capital rather than export it. The multi-trillion-dollar investment pledges made to the Trump administration in 2025 look progressively less deliverable. For US markets, the implication is incremental at present: a structurally smaller Gulf bid for Treasuries and US equities at the margin, at a moment when Treasury supply is already elevated. Sovereigns historically supported by Gulf bilateral financing — Egypt, Pakistan, Jordan — face a tighter external environment, and Asian recipients of GCC foreign direct investment, including Indian infrastructure platforms and Southeast Asian data-center projects, may face delayed commitments depending on the conflict's duration.

    Geopolitics and the Russia-China Axis

    Putin's Beijing visit clarified the asymmetry of the relationship. Russia arrived weakened by Sunday's record Ukrainian drone strike on Moscow, with its economy heavily dependent on Chinese demand for hydrocarbons and on Beijing for over 90% of sanctioned technology imports. Xi, fresh from his Trump summit and able to offer or withhold leverage on Power of Siberia 2 pricing, retains the structural upper hand. Both sides will emphasize their "no-limits" partnership publicly; the substantive question of gas pricing terms — the core of Russia's leverage pitch, premised on Middle East supply disruption — remains unconfirmed. The September expiry of the US-China trade truce, China's dominance over rare earth separation underpinning roughly $1.2 trillion of US GDP-linked activity, and Trump's July 24 tariff deadline represent the principal near-term flashpoints for the bilateral relationship. Whether the Beijing détente forged at last week's Trump-Xi summit proves durable against those pressures remains to be seen.

  • US

    • April CPI rose to 3.8% year-on-year on Tuesday — the highest reading in three years — as Hormuz-driven energy costs generated second-order pass-through into core services.
    • Kevin Warsh was confirmed as Federal Reserve chair on Wednesday by the narrowest margin on record, inheriting a policy framework where rate hikes have moved from tail risk to plausible base case.
    • The S&P 500 and Nasdaq 100 reached successive all-time highs through midweek before retreating Friday, with 10-year Treasury yields touching 4.54% and futures markets pricing roughly two-thirds probability of a December rate hike.
    • Cerebras Systems debuted Friday as the year's largest IPO, surging 68% to a $67 billion market cap, while Nvidia approached a $6 trillion valuation — both reflecting continued AI-driven momentum even as macro headwinds intensified.
    • The administration sought a pause on a federal court ruling that declared the president's 10% global tariffs unlawful, while processing more than $35.5 billion in tariff refunds following an earlier Supreme Court decision.

    Monetary Policy and Inflation

    The week's most consequential domestic development was Tuesday's CPI print, which showed headline inflation at 3.8% year-on-year — the highest reading since 2023 and above the Federal Reserve's upper tolerance band. The Strait of Hormuz closure remains the primary driver through elevated energy costs, but the composition of the report was more troubling than the headline: core inflation ex-energy ran at 2.8%, sticky prices re-crossed 3%, and the Fed's supercore services measure rose sharply, suggesting the oil shock is generating second-order wage and price effects rather than operating as a purely relative-price disturbance. Consumer prices rose faster than average hourly earnings in April, the first such occurrence since 2022. Wednesday's PPI report reinforced the picture, rising 6% year-on-year — its highest reading since 2022 on an ex-pandemic basis — led by energy and freight, with electronics components inflating 27% year-on-year as AI-driven memory shortages ended decades of technology-sector disinflation. Oxford Economics now projects the PCE deflator reaching 3.8%, effectively foreclosing near-term rate cuts. The Fed's policy horizon shifted accordingly: Bank of America explicitly recommended short positions in two-year Treasuries, characterizing rate-hike risk as underpriced, while Fed Governor Barr described inflation as "the overwhelming risk facing the economy."

    Federal Reserve Leadership Transition

    Kevin Warsh's Senate confirmation on Wednesday — by the narrowest margin on record — represents one of the more consequential central bank leadership changes in decades, delivered at a particularly difficult moment. Rather than the rate-cutting cycle that markets initially priced around his appointment, Warsh inherits a policy environment where the probability distribution for the fed funds rate has shifted decisively toward tightening. Whether he will defend institutional independence against an administration that has historically expressed preferences on monetary policy remains to be seen.

    Equity Markets and AI Concentration

    US equity markets spent most of the week threading a narrow path between AI-driven earnings momentum and rising rate anxiety. The S&P 500 and Nasdaq 100 set fresh records through Thursday before retreating Friday on PPI data and oil price pressure. The concentration dynamic sharpened further: the top ten S&P 500 constituents account for approximately 40% of index market capitalization, an all-time high, while the SOX semiconductor index has outperformed the Magnificent Seven since the ChatGPT launch. Alphabet closed the week with a market cap of $4.8 trillion against Nvidia's near-$6 trillion, having gained 34% in April alone. The simultaneous rise in the VIX alongside equity prices — a historically uncommon configuration — may signal that investors are hedging aggressively into the rally rather than expressing unqualified conviction. Critics including Jeremy Grantham and Michael Burry publicly flagged valuation concerns, though earnings revisions at semiconductor names continue to outpace price appreciation, with Sandisk and Micron trading at roughly nine times forward earnings despite gains of 482% and 172% respectively year-to-date.

    Fiscal and Policy Developments

    The administration's legal exposure on trade policy intensified this week. A federal trade court declared the president's 10% global tariffs unlawful, and the government responded by requesting a pause pending appeals while processing over $35.5 billion in tariff refunds from a related Supreme Court ruling. The FEMA leadership situation drew scrutiny ahead of the June 1 hurricane season: newly nominated director Cameron Hamilton previously authored a memo advocating the agency's abolition, and roughly 5,000 staffers have departed since January. Trump's financial disclosures separately revealed personal equity purchases in Q1 including Nvidia, Oracle, Microsoft, Boeing, and Costco — a detail unlikely to recede from political circulation with midterms less than six months away. ---

    Asia

    • The Trump-Xi summit in Beijing on Thursday and Friday produced a Chinese commitment to purchase 200 Boeing aircraft and a renewed trade truce framework, though no actionable Iran mechanism emerged and Xi issued his bluntest Taiwan warning to date.
    • South Korea's Kospi fell 6.1% on Friday — its steepest single-session decline in months — as elevated rate expectations repriced a market that had risen roughly 86% year-to-date.
    • China's April CPI rose to 1.2% and PPI to 2.8% year-on-year, its fastest pace since July 2022, with analysts declaring an exit from deflation though partly attributable to imported energy inflation.
    • The Bank of Japan signaled a possible June rate increase as 20-year JGB yields touched their highest level since 1997, while a board member called for tightening "as early as possible."
    • China's new supply chain coercion regulations — characterized by Rhodium Group as a shift from reactive to proactive economic statecraft — represent a structural escalation in Beijing's industrial policy posture.

    Trump-Xi Summit

    The week's geopolitical centrepiece was the Trump-Xi summit in Beijing on Thursday and Friday, the first US presidential visit to China in nearly a decade. The delegation's composition was itself a signal: Jensen Huang of Nvidia joined Tim Cook, Elon Musk, Kelly Ortberg, and Goldman Sachs' David Solomon, thrusting AI chip access into the heart of the agenda alongside trade, Taiwan, and Iran. Xi greeted Trump with a full state welcome at the Great Hall of the People, and both leaders struck cooperative public tones. China renewed import licenses for hundreds of US beef plants as an early gesture, and Treasury Secretary Bessent outlined a potential board of investment allowing Chinese capital into non-sensitive US industries, alongside possible tariff relief on roughly $30 billion of non-critical trade. Pre-summit working-level talks between Bessent and Vice Premier He Lifeng had begun in Seoul on Wednesday. The most pointed moment came when Xi warned that mishandling Taiwan could push the bilateral relationship into a "highly dangerous situation" — language published by Chinese state media while the two leaders were still in the room. On Iran, both sides expressed shared interest in reopening Hormuz but produced no operational mechanism. Trump subsequently confirmed that China has not approved purchases of Nvidia H200 chips and intends to develop domestic alternatives — a reminder that the semiconductor competition underlying the summit cordiality remains unresolved.

    South Korean Market Dislocation

    South Korean equities experienced two distinct episodes of volatility this week. On Monday, the Kospi shed more than $300 billion in market capitalization within 97 minutes — falling as much as 5.1% — after presidential policy chief Kim Yong-beom floated a "citizen dividend" funded by AI-profit taxes via a Facebook post, before recovering partially following a clarification. By Friday, the index fell a further 6.1% in its steepest single-day decline since March. The underlying tension is genuine: Samsung Electronics posted a 755% profit increase in Q1 and its labor union is threatening an 18-day strike from May 21 demanding 15% of operating profit for chip-division employees. The convergence of redistribution politics, labor unrest, and speculative positioning — retail borrowing is at record levels — around AI-driven windfall profits appears likely to persist. Goldman Sachs economists noted that AI-chip export booms in South Korea and Taiwan are on track to push both economies' current-account surpluses to record levels, likely pressuring their central banks toward rate increases later this year.

    China: Inflation, Credit Quality, and Strategic Posture

    China's April inflation data marked a meaningful inflection: PPI rising to 2.8% year-on-year — its fastest since July 2022 — alongside a 1.2% CPI reading prompted analysts to declare a formal exit from the deflationary spiral that had weighed on domestic pricing for the better part of two years. The drivers are partly imported, however, with elevated energy and raw material costs from the Hormuz closure contributing materially; Chinese crude imports fell 20% year-on-year in April as state refiners drew on commercial inventories. More structurally concerning, independent analysts estimate true non-performing loan ratios in the Chinese banking system at approximately 10% of total credit — against an official reading of 1.5% — implying roughly $3 trillion in under-classified bad debt. Regulatory forbearance has so far contained systemic risk but is visibly slowing loan growth and trapping capital in unproductive enterprises. China's April exports rose 14% year-on-year, well above the 8% consensus, with technology the primary driver — even as 10-year domestic bond yields remain below 2%, now trading below Japanese government bond yields, a striking inversion that reflects the dichotomy between external competitiveness and subdued domestic demand.

    Japan, India, and Regional Currency Stress

    Japan's 20-year JGB yields touched their highest level since 1997 this week, driven by energy-import inflation feeding domestic price pressures. A Bank of Japan board member called for tightening "as early as possible," and Finance Minister Katayama confirmed close coordination with Bessent on foreign exchange policy, with Bessent characterizing excess FX volatility as undesirable — a tacit endorsement of recent intervention. Japanese household spending fell for a fourth consecutive month, illustrating the demand compression accompanying the cost-of-living shock. Corporate goods prices rose at the fastest pace in 12 years. In India, Prime Minister Modi doubled gold and silver import tariffs to approximately 15%, pairing earlier appeals for citizens to curb bullion purchases with formal policy tightening to defend the rupee and shore up foreign exchange reserves. The Philippines, India, and Indonesia are all burning FX reserves to defend weakening currencies as oil import costs mount, with shrinking import cover pushing policymakers toward rate hikes. Thailand convened an emergency meeting between the prime minister and leading business figures to address converging crises of inflation, unemployment, and energy costs. ---

    Europe

    • UK 30-year gilt yields reached their highest level since 1998 and the 10-year touched 5.14% on Friday, as political uncertainty around Prime Minister Starmer's tenure compounded oil-driven inflation pressures.
    • Four junior ministers resigned in an attempt to force Starmer's departure, with Greater Manchester Mayor Andy Burnham emerging as frontrunner to challenge him for the Labour leadership by Friday.
    • The ECB's policy stance shifted materially, with Bundesbank President Nagel indicating rate hikes are "increasingly likely" and Governing Council member Kazaks warning of stagflation risks.
    • UK Q1 GDP rose 0.6%, its strongest quarter in a year, though virtually all forecasters expect near-stagnant growth over the remainder of 2026.
    • German industrial production fell unexpectedly for a second consecutive month in March, and France's unemployment rose to its highest level in five years.

    UK Political Crisis

    The week opened with Prime Minister Starmer delivering a reset speech following Labour's sweeping local election defeat, only to face an accelerating internal revolt. By Tuesday, he was publicly defying cabinet-level calls to set a departure timetable, telling ministers that "the country expects us to get on with governing" — a response that failed to stabilize investor sentiment. Home Secretary Mahmood and Health Secretary Streeting were among those privately or publicly pressing for transition; four junior ministers resigned in an attempt to force the issue. By Friday, Greater Manchester Mayor Andy Burnham had secured a credible path to mount a leadership challenge, with the MP for Makerfield standing aside in his favour, making Burnham the frontrunner in what markets now price as a greater-than-50% probability of a change in leadership before year-end. The pound fell on the news, gilt yields surged, and Schroders disclosed it is actively avoiding UK bonds, citing the risk that a successor could pursue a more expansionary fiscal stance. Jamie Dimon added an external dimension, warning that any post-Starmer government raising taxes on banks would cause JPMorgan to abandon plans for a multi-billion-pound Canary Wharf headquarters. The King's Speech, meanwhile, represents a near-term test of whether Starmer can project governing authority through the noise.

    UK Economy and Gilt Markets

    Beneath the political turbulence, the UK's economic data produced a genuine positive surprise: Q1 GDP rose 0.6%, the strongest quarterly growth in a year, with the IT sector contributing meaningfully. The reading suggests the economy absorbed the early weeks of the Iran conflict without contracting. However, the sustainability of that pace is widely questioned — Bloomberg Economics projects near-stagnant growth over the remainder of 2026 as higher energy costs, tighter borrowing conditions, and political uncertainty compound. The gilt market has increasingly reflected this tension: 30-year yields reached levels last seen in 1998, and the spread between gilts and Treasuries has tracked the oil price since the outbreak of hostilities, suggesting Iran may be a more proximate driver of UK funding costs than domestic politics alone, even if the two reinforce one another. The UK simultaneously halved its pledge to the Green Climate Fund, reducing its 2024–2027 contribution from £1.6 billion to £815 million, a further withdrawal from multilateral climate commitments. British employers continued shifting toward temporary hiring in April, with permanent-role hiring falling at its fastest pace in three months.

    Eurozone: ECB Pivot and German Fragility

    The ECB's policy outlook underwent a meaningful shift this week. Bundesbank President Nagel told Handelsblatt that rate hikes are increasingly likely — a notable pivot given that the dominant expectation at the start of the year was for continued easing. Governing Council member Kazaks went further, warning that data are pointing toward stagflation risks and that a hike would be warranted if oil price increases feed through to inflation expectations. A Bloomberg survey now shows the ECB expected to hike twice in 2026, though Kazaks also described a June move as "less obvious" than imminent — reflecting the genuine tension between above-target inflation and softening growth that Oxford Economics estimates could add 0.6 to 0.7 percentage points of additional inflation pass-through given elevated public inflation awareness. Germany's underlying position remains fragile: industrial production fell unexpectedly for a second consecutive month in March, Chancellor Merz's approval ratings have fallen to historic lows, the AfD has extended its polling lead, and a six-hour coalition working session produced few concrete agreements. France compounded the eurozone picture, with unemployment rising unexpectedly to its highest level in five years.

    European Political and Structural Themes

    European political fragility extended well beyond the UK. Romania's government fell, Latvia's prime minister resigned over a drone-related controversy, and trade union protests greeted the German chancellor. Italy under Meloni stands as a notable exception to this pattern of leadership attrition. At the EU level, the Commission reached unanimous political agreement to sanction violent West Bank settlers — its first such consensus since the Gaza crisis — while reporting suggests von der Leyen's centralized management style is generating internal friction and diluting focus on the single market agenda. An analysis of London commercial property found that up to 78% of Westminster offices and 71% of City offices will fail to meet Minimum Energy Efficiency Standards expected in the early 2030s, raising stranded-asset risk across the sector. In eastern European bond markets, Hungary's borrowing costs fell below Poland's for the first time since 2020, reflecting investor optimism around new Prime Minister Magyar's EU alignment agenda. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude rose from approximately $103 to around $109 across the week as the Strait of Hormuz remained largely closed, entering its eleventh week of disruption, with TD Securities warning of a potential surge toward $150 and BloombergNEF projecting $160 if closure persists through July.
    • The IEA reported global oil inventories falling at a record pace and projected outright demand contraction of 420,000 barrels per day in 2026 — a reading historically associated with severe economic stress.
    • US, UK, eurozone, and Japanese sovereign bond markets sold off in concert, reflecting a common energy-inflation driver layered over country-specific fiscal and political factors, with US 10-year yields at 4.54% and UK 10-year gilts at 5.14% by Friday.
    • The Trump-Xi summit produced shared rhetoric on Hormuz but no operational mechanism, while Trump's comment that the US does not need the strait open "at all" complicated the diplomatic signal.
    • China's new supply chain coercion regulations, characterized by Rhodium Group as a shift to proactive economic statecraft, represent a structural escalation with implications for Western industrial policy.

    The Iran Conflict and Hormuz

    The Strait of Hormuz closure — now in its eleventh week — remains the dominant cross-cutting variable in global markets. Monday opened with Trump rejecting Iran's latest peace proposal, sending Brent crude up as much as 4.6% toward $106 before settling above $103. By Tuesday, with WTI near $98.93 and Trump characterizing the ceasefire as on "massive life support," the diplomatic outlook deteriorated further. Satellite imagery confirmed that Iranian crude exports from the Kharg Island terminal had halted for several days — the first sign of a prolonged stoppage since the conflict began. BloombergNEF estimates disruption across 27 Middle Eastern oil fields has reached 9 million barrels per day. A Japanese supertanker appeared to make a covert Hormuz transit, two India-bound LNG cargoes crossed under elevated-risk conditions, and Qatar directed vessels near Ras Laffan to disable their AIS transponders — at least nine tankers went dark from Monday. The asymmetric warfare dimension remains central: Iran's deployment of midget submarines has sustained the stalemate despite significant US naval superiority. Friday's Brent price of approximately $109 per barrel represents a rise of more than 60% since the conflict began in late February. The UAE announced plans to double its Fujairah pipeline bypass capacity by 2027, a structural adaptation to what some planners are now treating as a prolonged-disruption scenario.

    Oil Market Buffers and Their Limits

    Morgan Stanley and Mercuria both characterized the short-term buffers preventing a more severe price spike as unsustainable. US crude and product exports are running above 14 million barrels per day with emergency stockpile drawdowns at record rates; simultaneously, Chinese state refiners have reduced crude purchases by drawing on commercial inventories. Neither dynamic can persist indefinitely. The IEA's assessment that global inventories are falling at a record pace and will continue declining for months implies continued upward price pressure absent resolution. The US EIA now projects global oil demand growth of only 200,000 barrels per day in 2026, against a pre-war forecast of 1.2 million; the IEA projects outright demand contraction of 420,000 barrels per day. Hapag-Lloyd reported approximately $250 million in additional monthly operating costs since February 28, though its CEO noted that Asian export volumes outside the Persian Gulf have been "surprisingly" resilient. The jet fuel market is under particular strain, with US jet fuel exports surging to a record 455,000 barrels per day as airlines in Europe and Asia scrambled for supply. OPEC+ is targeting completion of quota restoration by end-September, though the relief that provides depends substantially on Hormuz conditions.

    Trade Flows and Energy Infrastructure Adaptation

    The Hormuz disruption is reshaping physical trade routes in ways that may prove semi-permanent. Iran has boosted rail freight to China via Kazakhstan and Turkmenistan, with train frequency rising from roughly one per week to one every three to four days and container rates along the route climbing 40% above typical levels. China's clean technology manufacturers are explicitly leveraging the energy shock as an export opportunity: EV exports rose 112% year-on-year in April, solar panel shipments doubled month-on-month in March to a record, and battery exports climbed over a third, with Chinese firms actively targeting Europe, Africa, and Southeast Asia. The commercial logic is clear — sustained high fossil fuel prices improve the economics of electrification across every geography simultaneously. At the same time, Beijing's new supply chain coercion regulations — allowing China to retaliate against foreign governments or firms that assist in actions deemed to harm Chinese industrial security — are calibrated to deter the sourcing diversification that Washington and Brussels have been encouraging. Rhodium Group warns of a self-reinforcing dynamic in which China's rising manufacturing share erodes competitor margins, reducing Western firms' capacity to reinvest in R&D, while private capital increasingly avoids sectors with entrenched state-backed Chinese competition.

    Global Bond Markets and Inflation Propagation

    The synchronized selloff in sovereign bond markets across the US, UK, eurozone, and Japan this week reflects a common inflation driver propagating through different institutional contexts. US 10-year yields closed Friday at 4.54%, UK 10-year gilts at 5.14%, and Japan's 20-year rate at its highest level since 1996. Producer price indices in the US, China, and Japan all surprised to the upside this week, suggesting pipeline inflation pressure has not peaked. Investors captured 5% yields on 30-year US Treasuries for the first time in nearly two decades — a notable level for long-duration pricing globally. The broader consequences of a world in which all major central banks face simultaneous inflationary pressure — limiting their ability to buffer growth slowdowns with easing — remain to be fully absorbed in equity valuations, credit spreads, and emerging market capital flows. Asian central banks are already responding: the Philippines, India, and Indonesia are burning FX reserves to defend weakening currencies, while the Bank of Japan is signaling an accelerated tightening path. The coherence of losses across global bond indices this year represents a meaningful change from the post-GFC environment of divergent monetary cycles, and its full implications for asset allocation have not yet resolved.

  • US

    • S&P 500 sustained record highs throughout the week, with the index approaching 7,400 by Wednesday, even as WTI crude gyrated between $100 and $112 per barrel.
    • AI capital expenditure — information-processing equipment up 64% in real terms in Q1 — remained the dominant force in US business investment, with S&P 500 buyback authorizations at a record $665 billion for the first four months of the year.
    • The K-shaped energy shock deepened: gas prices exceeded $4.50 per gallon by Thursday, with New York Fed research showing households earning under $40,000 cut real gasoline consumption 7% even as nominal spending rose 12%.
    • A federal trade court declared Trump's 10% global tariffs unlawful on Thursday, compounding earlier Supreme Court setbacks, while the administration gave the EU until July 4 to ratify a trade deal or face higher duties.
    • The April nonfarm payrolls report, expected to show approximately 62,000–65,000 new jobs, was due Friday — the labor market not yet registering the energy shock's full pass-through.

    Equity Markets

    US equity markets sustained record highs across the week in a manner that continued to unsettle strategists even as it rewarded momentum investors. The S&P 500 approached 7,400 by Wednesday, and Goldman Sachs strategists flagged "yellow flags" in the advance's composition: factor analysis showed quality stocks — characterized by stable earnings and strong balance sheets — were consistent underperformers since Liberation Day, while momentum and liquidity factors dominated. The rally appeared driven more by abundant liquidity and AI-related animal spirits than fundamental re-rating, leaving the index potentially vulnerable to any deterioration in the macro or geopolitical backdrop. Roughly 80% of S&P 500 companies reporting thus far had beaten estimates, providing genuine earnings support, but the index's resilience in the face of renewed Iran-related tensions and oil price volatility was notable. Corporate buybacks provided a structural floor, with full-year authorized repurchases projected at $1.55 trillion — potentially eclipsing last year's record.

    AI and Corporate Earnings

    The AI investment cycle remained the week's dominant corporate theme. AMD surged approximately 17% in premarket trading on Wednesday after raising its sales forecast, and Super Micro Computer gained 19% on a strong report. Alphabet raised nearly $17 billion in debt on Wednesday to fund data-center buildout. Nvidia, by contrast, fell 9% over six sessions and was the Philadelphia semiconductor index's worst performer year-to-date, as customers including Alphabet, Amazon, and Intel developed credible competing chip architectures — though evidence of material market share loss remained limited. Amazon's launch of Amazon Supply Chain Services on Tuesday, packaging its freight, trucking, air, ocean, and last-mile capabilities into a single commercial offering, sent FedEx, UPS, and Expeditors shares lower, though analysts characterized the near-term competitive threat as incremental. Fortinet surged 15% in premarket trading on Thursday after raising its revenue outlook, while Snap fell sharply on advertising revenue weakness attributed directly to the Middle East conflict.

    Fiscal and Consumer Stress

    The fiscal backdrop grew more complex. Jeffrey Gundlach disclosed on Thursday he was repositioning portfolios for an extreme sovereign-debt scenario — placing the probability of a US debt restructuring below 30% but rising — joining Apollo's Marc Rowan and Bridgewater's Ray Dalio in issuing unusually direct warnings about fiscal trajectories. The CBO projects federal debt reaching 108% of GDP by 2030. Former Treasury Secretary Mnuchin stated plainly there is no "break-the-glass" solution to the US debt problem. The Trump administration began processing refunds for $166 billion in tariffs declared unlawful by the Supreme Court — a development with meaningful fiscal implications that received limited market attention. At the household level, Spirit Airlines ceased operations during the week, illustrating the energy shock's asymmetric impact on businesses serving lower-income consumers. Kraft Heinz's CEO warned that lower-income shoppers are "literally running out of money at the end of the month." Bank of America estimates the full-year energy "gas tax" amounts to roughly 0.5% of GDP, approximately equal to the projected boost from either the One Big Beautiful Bill or AI capital expenditure — a structural offset that complicates the administration's growth narrative.

    Monetary Policy and the Fed

    The Federal Reserve's path toward rate cuts appeared increasingly constrained throughout the week. Two-year inflation swaps crossed 3% on Tuesday — their highest since 2022 — driven by Middle East-linked energy inflation. Fed Governor Collins aligned herself with last week's policy dissent on easing language, suggesting internal divisions at the FOMC had not fully resolved. The 30-year Treasury yield briefly breached 5%, though analysts at Evercore ISI argued a durable Hormuz deal bringing oil lower would render rate cuts delayed rather than derailed. The self-reinforcing dynamic between the administration's military engagement in the Gulf and its stated desire for lower interest rates represents a tension that deserves close attention. New research published during the week suggested the politically motivated dismissal of Bureau of Labor Statistics chief Erika McEntarfer correlated with approximately $20 billion in foregone industrial investment — a figure 25 times the BLS annual budget — as executives grew less willing to commit capital amid eroding confidence in government data. ---

    Asia

    • South Korea's Kospi gained 14% for the week, with Samsung Electronics crossing a $1 trillion market capitalization on Wednesday and SK Hynix rising 30%, as AI-driven semiconductor demand re-rated the country's equity market to what Goldman Sachs described as the strongest forecast earnings growth for any Asian market since 1999's post-crisis recovery.
    • Japan's Ministry of Finance deployed an estimated $30 billion in foreign exchange intervention during the week to defend the yen near ¥160, producing the currency's largest single-day gain since 2022 — though analysts remained skeptical of durability absent a meaningful shift in Bank of Japan policy.
    • China directed its largest banks to halt new lending to five US-sanctioned refiners while simultaneously ordering private companies to disregard US secondary sanctions, a calculated attempt to signal defiance while protecting dollar-system access ahead of the Trump-Xi Beijing summit scheduled for May 14–15.
    • Indonesia's economy expanded 5.61% year-on-year in Q1 — a three-year high — but its currency set a record low as oil-dependent emerging markets bore the heaviest burden from the Hormuz disruption.
    • The Reserve Bank of Australia delivered a third consecutive rate hike in an 8-1 vote, unwinding all of last year's easing, leading developed-market peers in the hawkish response to the energy shock.

    Semiconductors and the AI Super-Cycle

    Asian equity markets opened the week at fresh record highs, with South Korea's Kospi and Taiwan's benchmark surging 5.1% and 4.6% respectively on Monday as AI-linked semiconductor names led the advance. The dynamic accelerated materially on Wednesday when Samsung Electronics crossed a $1 trillion market capitalization — only the second Asian company to achieve that threshold — with the stock rallying 14–16% as markets repriced memory's structural role in AI infrastructure. SK Hynix rose 30% over the week. Goldman Sachs raised its Kospi target for the fourth time this year, projecting an additional 20% gain and citing forecast earnings growth of 300% for 2026. Morgan Stanley's chief Asia economist characterized the broader dynamic as the beginning of a multi-year industrial super-cycle, arguing that AI infrastructure, energy transition, and defense spending will broaden from semiconductors into wider capital expenditure and ultimately consumption across the region, with Taiwan, South Korea, China, and Japan identified as primary beneficiaries.

    China: Sanctions, Energy, and Summit Positioning

    China's behavior on US sanctions represented one of the week's most consequential geopolitical-financial developments. Beijing's weekend directive ordering private companies to disregard US secondary sanctions targeting Iranian crude purchases was an explicit act of defiance — a meaningful departure from its historically cautious approach of quiet compliance. By Thursday, however, regulators had separately directed the country's largest banks to halt new lending to five sanctioned refiners while leaving existing loans intact. The resulting posture is deliberately calibrated: project defiance in the policy domain, protect dollar-system access at the institutional level. Wang Yi hosted Iran's foreign minister on Wednesday and urged Tehran to reopen the Strait of Hormuz, reflecting Beijing's dependence on Middle Eastern oil — approximately two-fifths of its imports — and its desire to position Xi Jinping as a peacemaker ahead of the May 14–15 Trump summit. JPMorgan and Citigroup being sued in Chinese courts over frozen payments tied to a sanctioned firm illustrates the narrowing legal corridor available to global institutions navigating competing jurisdictions.

    Japan and Southeast Asia

    Japan's monetary and diplomatic postures both evolved significantly during the week. The Ministry of Finance deployed an estimated $30 billion in foreign exchange intervention, pulling the yen from above ¥160 toward ¥155–157 per dollar, but analysts broadly characterized the effort as increasingly ineffective level-targeting absent a Bank of Japan commitment to near-term rate hikes. Vanguard remained bearish on the yen, arguing that elevated oil prices are structurally eroding Japan's terms of trade. On the geopolitical front, Prime Minister Takaichi visited Vietnam and Australia, and Defense Minister Koizumi deployed to the Philippines on Tuesday to observe Japanese troops in joint Balikatan exercises — including a ship-sinking drill in the South China Sea — marking the first such deployment since World War II. Toyota on Thursday issued a notably cautious earnings outlook, projecting ¥3 trillion in operating income for fiscal 2027 against a consensus of ¥4.6 trillion, citing elevated raw material costs from the Iran conflict, and fell as much as 3.5%. In Southeast Asia, the Philippines faced its most severe economic stress since the pandemic, with growth slowing and consumer spending at its weakest in over a decade. Indonesia tightened foreign currency purchase limits to $25,000 from $50,000 to support the rupiah. ---

    Europe

    • UK 30-year gilt yields reached 5.78% on Wednesday — the highest since 1998 — reflecting a combination of the UK's twin fiscal and current account deficits, persistent inflation, and political uncertainty.
    • Reform UK secured a net gain of 275 seats in Thursday's English local elections, while Labour lost approximately 204 — Labour losing roughly half the seats it was defending — raising questions about Prime Minister Starmer's authority without triggering an immediate leadership challenge.
    • UniCredit reported a record quarterly profit of €3.22 billion on Tuesday, up 16% year-on-year and well ahead of consensus, while HSBC fell 5.9% in London after missing estimates due to an unexpected UK fraud charge and Middle East provisions.
    • Chancellor Merz's government faces questions about coalition longevity one year into office, and Trump's announcement of additional US troop withdrawals from Germany in response to Merz's public criticism of US Iran policy underscored how transatlantic tensions constrain European policy space.
    • The EU reiterated its demand that Washington adopt the main elements of their trade deal by July and respect a 15% tariff ceiling, as US Ambassador Puzder warned 25% tariffs on European vehicles could arrive "relatively soon."

    Political Fragmentation and UK Gilt Stress

    The week's defining political development in Europe was the scale of Reform UK's gains in Thursday's English local elections. With counts approaching completion, Reform had secured a net gain of 275 seats while Labour's tally fell by 204 — Labour losing roughly half the seats it was defending — and Nigel Farage's party simultaneously displaced Conservative support in traditional strongholds and Green support among dissatisfied progressives. Prime Minister Starmer ruled out stepping aside despite the results, but the outcome suggests meaningful fragmentation of Britain's traditional two-party structure. Market attention focused specifically on whether pressure on Starmer could push potential successor candidates toward looser fiscal commitments — a scenario gilt markets would view unfavorably. UK 30-year gilt yields reached 5.78% on Wednesday, the highest since 1998, with the spread over US Treasuries having widened beyond levels seen at the peak of the Liz Truss episode in 2022. Bank of England officials joined private forecasters in questioning the reliability of recent UK economic data, adding further complexity to the rate-setting path. Overnight index swaps implied significantly more BoE tightening than was priced before the Iran conflict.

    Transatlantic Friction and Continental Politics

    Europe's relationship with Washington deteriorated on multiple fronts. Trump's announcement of a 25% tariff on European automobiles and the disclosure of US troop withdrawals from Germany beyond the initially disclosed 5,000 — both appearing connected to Merz's public criticism of US Iran policy — illustrated the linkage between geopolitical alignment and economic leverage that European governments are struggling to navigate. The EU reiterated retaliatory options should auto tariffs proceed, while US Ambassador Puzder warned tariffs could arrive "relatively soon." France's Macron nominated Emmanuel Moulin to head the Banque de France and take a seat on the ECB's Governing Council on Wednesday — a personnel decision with implications for future ECB deliberations. Romanian politics added continental uncertainty, with a no-confidence vote against Prime Minister Bolojan backed by an unusual far-right and Social Democrat coalition, potentially deepening a fiscal crisis around the EU's widest budget deficit. Meanwhile, China's five-year plan, emphasizing quality upgrading and frontier technologies, generated what Germany's machinery association VDMA described as unprecedented corporate anxiety about European export competitiveness in sectors including industrial machinery and precision equipment.

    Corporate Earnings and Energy Exposure

    European corporate results this week illustrated the Hormuz disruption's uneven sectoral impact. Shell reported stronger-than-expected first-quarter earnings as elevated energy prices and increased trading volatility more than offset production declines. UniCredit's record quarterly result of €3.22 billion contrasted with HSBC's miss, the latter weighed by an unexpected UK fraud charge and Middle East conflict provisions — shares fell 5.9% in London on Tuesday. Novo Nordisk surged approximately 9% on Wednesday after its new Wegovy pill drove quarterly sales growth. Infineon's revenue outlook beat expectations on AI-driven power chip demand. BMW's earnings declined, reflecting intense pricing competition in China. The European Commission's stagflationary characterization of the continent's predicament — higher energy costs meeting sluggish growth — accurately captures the ECB's constrained position, where the inflation impulse from oil prices argues against the accommodation that weak growth would otherwise warrant. In Switzerland, lawmakers advanced a legislative process that could impose up to $20 billion in additional capital requirements on UBS, with significant implications for the institution's capital allocation and broader European banking sentiment. ---

    Global

    • WTI crude fell nearly 10% on Wednesday after Trump suspended "Project Freedom" and Axios reported a one-page US-Iran memorandum of understanding framework, though the commodity remained above $99 per barrel and December 2026 futures stayed near $77 — suggesting markets do not yet price a near-term, sustained reopening of the Strait.
    • US forces struck Iranian missile and drone launch sites near the Strait of Hormuz on Thursday after Iran attacked three American warships, introducing fresh uncertainty about whether the diplomatic framework reported earlier in the week remained viable.
    • China's defiance of US secondary sanctions, Iran's collection of Hormuz transit tolls in Bitcoin, and Gulf sovereign wealth funds reportedly reassessing US asset allocations collectively represent observable — if still modest — stress points in dollar-denominated financial architecture.
    • The New York Fed's global supply chain pressure index reached its highest reading since July 2022 in April, and global LNG exports fell to their lowest level in nearly two years during the same month.
    • The Trump-Xi Beijing summit, scheduled for May 14–15 with trade, Taiwan, and Iran all on the agenda, will be the first visit by a sitting US president to China since 2017.

    The Hormuz Crisis: Escalation, Diplomacy, and Oil Markets

    The Strait of Hormuz remained the central fault line in global markets for the week's entirety, with the diplomatic and military situation oscillating in ways that produced sharp intraday moves across oil, equities, and fixed income. The week opened with President Trump's "Project Freedom" initiative — a plan to guide neutral vessels through the waterway — generating confusion among shipping executives given an absence of operational detail and the US Navy's stated non-involvement. On Wednesday, Trump suspended the operation after Iran struck an oil terminal at the UAE's Fujairah port and US forces repelled multiple drone, missile, and fast-attack boat strikes — Axios subsequently reported the two sides were working toward a one-page memorandum of understanding involving a Hormuz reopening in exchange for a moratorium on uranium enrichment and lifting of some sanctions. WTI fell nearly 10% on Wednesday. By Thursday, however, US forces struck Iranian launch sites after Iran attacked three American warships, reintroducing genuine uncertainty about the framework's durability. Brent remained above $99 per barrel at week's end, and prediction markets assigned less than 50% odds to a return to normal Hormuz traffic by end of June, down from roughly 90% two weeks earlier. The cumulative supply loss since the conflict began is estimated to be approaching one billion barrels.

    Energy Markets: Supply Response and Structural Adaptation

    The US has emerged as the world's largest crude exporter, shipping over 250 million barrels in nine weeks, with domestic inventories drawing down rapidly in ways that raise questions about the sustainability of that role. Diamondback Energy's CEO signaled an "inflection point" for the Permian Basin on Wednesday, with expectations of up to 30 additional rigs; Exxon announced plans for a 12% production increase. OPEC+ agreed on a modest symbolic increase of 188,000 barrels per day for June, largely a signaling exercise following the UAE's surprise exit from the bloc. Abu Dhabi's ADNOC simultaneously announced $55 billion in accelerated project awards. US gasoline prices topped $4.50 per gallon for the first time since July 2022. In parallel, structural adaptation is accelerating: Chinese truck operators are reportedly advancing EV adoption timelines in response to surging diesel costs; green bond issuance in emerging markets rose 53% year-on-year to $24 billion in Q1 as energy-import-dependent economies seek to accelerate renewables deployment; and DP World introduced end-to-end war risk insurance for cargo traversing the region, an acknowledgment that perceived risk will persist even if a deal materializes.

    Dollar Architecture and Geopolitical Realignment

    The accumulation of de-dollarization stress points became harder to dismiss as episodic by week's end. Iran's collection of Hormuz transit tolls in Bitcoin represents a de minimis but symbolically notable bypass of dollar payment infrastructure. China's instruction to private refiners to disregard US secondary sanctions directly challenges the enforcement architecture that gives dollar primacy its coercive utility. Gulf sovereign wealth funds are reportedly reassessing US asset allocation as American security guarantees prove less reliable. Central banks across emerging markets are increasing gold holdings while reducing dollar reserves. Project mBridge — the BIS-coordinated cross-border settlement system developed with China, Hong Kong, the UAE, Saudi Arabia, and Thailand — remains nascent in transaction volume relative to SWIFT-based flows, but elevated geopolitical tensions create conditions under which adoption could accelerate. At the Milken Institute conference in Los Angeles, EQT's Per Franzén, overseeing roughly €270 billion in private-market assets, noted clients are actively reassessing dollar-denominated overweights. The more salient risk, as multiple conference participants noted, is not displacement by a rival currency but erosion through sustained US policy inconsistency — a self-inflicted vulnerability rather than an externally imposed one. Whether these strains prove transitory or structural remains genuinely uncertain, and the dollar retains substantial network advantages that competing systems have not yet overcome.

    Climate, AI, and the Energy Transition

    A widening tension between AI infrastructure expansion and corporate climate commitments crystallized more visibly this week. Microsoft was reported to be weighing abandonment of its 2030 hourly clean-energy matching target — a significant retreat from pre-ChatGPT commitments — as data center power demand and natural gas's appeal grow. Across major hyperscalers, reported carbon emissions have risen 23–64% versus pre-ChatGPT baselines. BloombergNEF projects US data center power consumption will exceed 106 gigawatts by 2035, more than double current levels. New York Governor Hochul moved on Thursday to soften the state's landmark climate law, pushing the emissions-enforcement deadline to 2028, citing energy affordability concerns — reflecting a broader pattern across US states where Iran war-induced fuel price increases are weakening political support for near-term climate mandates. The Hormuz disruption is simultaneously reinforcing the strategic rationale for domestic clean energy: energy-import-dependent economies from Australia to India to Southeast Asia are accelerating renewables investment, illustrating how supply security and decarbonization incentives have — at least temporarily — realigned.

  • US

    • The Fed's April meeting — Powell's last as chair — produced four dissents, the most fractious vote since October 1992, with futures markets now pricing no cuts in 2026 and roughly 50% odds of a hike by April 2027.
    • Mega-cap earnings delivered a split verdict: Alphabet surged more than 7% on Wednesday after beating estimates, while Meta fell roughly 7% in after-hours trade despite strong ad revenue, after raising 2026 capex guidance by $10 billion to potentially above $145 billion.
    • Apple delivered a stronger-than-expected third-quarter revenue forecast on Thursday, gaining approximately 2.8%, capping what appears to have been the best monthly performance for US stocks since late 2020.
    • The Philadelphia Semiconductor index extended its record winning streak to 18 consecutive positive sessions, trading at roughly 60 times trailing earnings — a valuation that prices in substantial margin expansion whose durability remains to be tested.
    • Kevin Warsh's path to the Fed chair cleared after Senator Tillis dropped his blockade of the nomination, following the Justice Department's decision to end its criminal investigation of Jerome Powell.

    Fed Leadership and Policy

    The Federal Reserve's April meeting will be remembered primarily for its institutional texture rather than its rate decision, which was, as widely anticipated, a hold. Four FOMC members dissented — three regional presidents (Beth Hammack, Neel Kashkari, and Lorie Logan) objecting to any residual easing bias in the forward guidance, and Stephen Miran on separate grounds — marking the most divided vote since October 1992. Two-year Treasury yields jumped 11 basis points on Wednesday, the largest Fed-day move since January 2022, while 30-year yields reached 5% for the first time this year. Futures markets have fully removed any 2026 easing from their base case, with roughly 50% odds now assigned to a hike by April 2027. The hawkish tilt reflects a committee increasingly focused on the inflationary consequences of sustained elevated energy prices, a dynamic that sits in direct tension with the rate cuts the White House has sought. The leadership transition adds a further layer of complexity. Powell announced he will remain on the board as a governor after Warsh assumes the chair role, until the Justice Department's building-renovation investigation is resolved. Treasury Secretary Bessent characterized this as a violation of Fed norms, though the precedent set by Marriner Eccles somewhat undercuts the framing. Warsh will inherit a deeply divided committee, an unresolved inflation picture, and the unusual presence of his predecessor as a sitting board member — circumstances that may constrain his ability to shift policy direction quickly, whatever his inclinations.

    Equity Markets and Earnings

    The week's earnings slate served as a meaningful stress test for the AI-driven valuation narrative. Alphabet's Wednesday results were received as a genuine positive, with shares gaining more than 7% as its custom TPU chip architecture was interpreted as a credible competitive challenge to Nvidia in AI inference workloads — adding $421 billion to market capitalization in a single session, the second-largest single-day market cap gain on record for any company. Amazon's AWS unit posted 28% revenue growth, its fastest in over three years. Meta's reaction, by contrast, was negative: despite advertising revenue of $56.3 billion, the company's decision to raise 2026 capex guidance by $10 billion to potentially above $145 billion was read by investors as prioritizing long-run AI buildout over near-term returns. The combined 2026 capex envelope for the four hyperscalers now approaches $725 billion. Microsoft's Azure growth was viewed as underwhelming, with shares slipping modestly. Apple's above-consensus third-quarter revenue forecast on Thursday provided a constructive bookend to the week. NXP Semiconductors surged 16% on Wednesday premarket on an upbeat revenue forecast, and Seagate rallied 18% on strong AI-related storage demand. The Philadelphia Semiconductor index extended to 18 consecutive positive sessions — the longest winning streak in the index's history. That streak reflects genuine earnings momentum: with slightly over a third of S&P 500 companies having reported, the beat rate stands at approximately 80% against a historical average of 77%, while the aggregate earnings surprise of roughly 12% materially exceeds the typical 7% premium. Societe Generale estimates 12-month forward profits are now approximately $600 billion higher than four months ago, though semiconductors alone account for roughly 43% of that revision — a concentration that amplifies both the upside and the tail risk.

    Monetary Policy and Fiscal Dynamics

    The Fed's hawkish hold creates an environment in which the bond market may increasingly act as the economy's primary tightening mechanism. Thirty-year Treasury yields pressing toward and briefly touching 5% on Thursday carry direct consequences for mortgage rates, corporate refinancing costs, and the fiscal burden on a government running structural deficits. Fitch separately warned that the US debt burden remains far above other AA-rated sovereigns, a reminder that fiscal sustainability concerns have not been resolved by strong nominal growth. Congressional Republicans are advancing proposals to increase Pentagon spending by 44% — a rare act of intra-party friction with the White House — and to lower capital gains taxes, a combination that complicates the budget arithmetic further. In credit markets, investors showed early signs of fatigue after a $300 billion AI-related debt binge, with demands emerging for better pricing and stronger covenant protections. University of Michigan consumer sentiment fell to 49.8 in April, its lowest reading since data collection began in 1978, a reminder that the macro backdrop outside equity markets remains more fragile than headline index levels suggest. ---

    Asia

    • The Bank of Japan held rates at 0.75% in its most divided vote under Governor Ueda's tenure — 6-3 — with a June hike now the base case; Japan subsequently intervened in currency markets on Thursday after the yen weakened toward 160 per dollar.
    • South Korea's Kospi has risen approximately 60% year-to-date, with Samsung Electronics reporting a 755% year-on-year profit increase in the March quarter, though a threatened 18-day strike beginning May 21 introduces meaningful labor risk.
    • China's Politburo pledged to counter external shocks amid better-than-expected first-quarter growth, while regulators took assertive action on multiple fronts: blocking the Meta-Manus AI deal, cracking down on metals-sector invoice fraud, and suspending autonomous vehicle licensing.
    • India faces compounding pressures: every one of the world's 50 hottest cities this week was located in India, with temperatures exceeding 40°C, while airlines warned of potential service suspensions as the Hormuz closure drives jet fuel costs to unsustainable levels.
    • India's trade policy underwent a meaningful shift, with the signing of a free trade agreement with New Zealand following earlier deals with the EU and UK, marking a departure from the protectionist posture maintained between roughly 2017 and 2020.

    Bank of Japan: Hawkish Hold and Currency Pressure

    The Bank of Japan's April meeting produced a 6-3 vote to hold its benchmark rate at 0.75% — the most divided outcome under Governor Ueda's tenure — with three board members, including the consensus-leaning Junko Nakagawa, voting for an immediate hike. Bloomberg Economics now assigns high probability to a move at the June meeting. Despite the explicitly hawkish signal, the yen remained stubbornly weak near 160 per dollar through much of the week, reflecting investor skepticism about how far normalization can proceed under Prime Minister Sanae Takaichi, who has appointed two dovish board members and publicly criticized a trade minister for suggesting rate hikes could strengthen the currency. Spring Shunto wage negotiations produced a third consecutive year of approximately 5% wage increases, and five-year inflation breakevens have reached 2.7%, suggesting bond markets believe the deflationary era is genuinely over — yet the currency's weakness persists, creating a tension between fundamentals and market pricing that is likely to require resolution. Japan intervened in currency markets on Thursday for the first time since 2024, purchasing yen and selling dollars after issuing a "final" warning to investors. The move triggered intraday strengthening of as much as 0.7%, with dollar-yen reaching 155.5 before partially retracing to around 156.5. Analysts cautioned that intervention can stabilize markets only briefly: Japan's structural dependence on imported energy — now made substantially more expensive by the Strait of Hormuz disruption — continues to exert downward pressure on the currency through the current account. Japan's top currency official also indicated readiness to intervene in crude oil futures markets, an unusual extension of currency-management tools into commodity markets.

    South Korea: Record Performance and Concentration Risk

    South Korea's equity market has emerged as the year's strongest performer among major indices, with the Kospi rising approximately 60% and total market capitalization surpassing the UK's at $4.04 trillion. Samsung Electronics reported a 755% year-on-year profit increase in the March quarter, and Samsung and SK Hynix together now represent 42% of Kospi capitalization, up from 28% two years ago. That concentration amplifies both momentum and tail risk. Labor tensions are building: thousands of Samsung employees rallied over the weekend demanding higher wages, with the union threatening an 18-day strike beginning May 21. South Korea's April export data showed continued semiconductor strength, providing some offset to the regional drag from elevated energy costs.

    China: Growth, Governance, and Technology Control

    China's April PMI data presented a bifurcated picture. Manufacturing remained in expansion territory, supported by resilient export demand, while services and construction slipped back into contraction, with building activity registering its worst performance since the early stages of the pandemic. The divergence reflects a familiar structural tension: external demand is sustaining factory output while weak domestic consumption and a deepening property downturn weigh on the broader economy. The Politburo pledged to counter external shocks and enhance energy security, citing better-than-expected first-quarter growth, and Moody's upgraded China's credit outlook to stable. Beijing's handling of the Meta-Manus transaction represents a significant escalation in China's approach to technology governance. Authorities ordered the two companies to unwind the completed $2 billion acquisition within weeks, barred Manus co-founders from leaving the country, and instructed several private firms via the NDRC to reject US-origin capital unless explicitly approved. The stated rationale centers on preventing strategically important AI capabilities from flowing to the United States, but the broader signal appears directed at domestic entrepreneurs contemplating offshore restructuring to access US capital. The episode risks reinforcing perceptions of elevated political risk in China's technology sector. Separately, China's tax authorities launched a crackdown on circular invoicing in the metals trading sector, rattling copper, aluminum, and silver traders in Shanghai who rely on such invoices for bank financing. The State Taxation Administration framed the investigation as targeting fraudulent activity that has artificially inflated output measures.

    India: Heat, Energy, and Trade
