    India faces an unusual convergence of pressures. Every one of the world's 50 hottest cities this week was located in India, with temperatures exceeding 40°C across densely populated states. The McKinsey Global Institute estimates heat-related productivity losses could reach $250 billion, with monsoon relief not expected until June. The Federation of Indian Airlines warned that carriers are "on the verge of closing down" without government relief on jet fuel prices, with Brent crude trading near $111 a barrel. Against this backdrop, the trade policy liberalization underway is a structurally meaningful development. India signed a free trade agreement with New Zealand, following earlier deals with the EU and UK, representing a departure from the protectionist posture that saw average tariff rates rise from approximately 13% to 18% between 2017 and 2020. The pivot appears driven partly by urgency to secure alternative market access as the US turns more protectionist, and partly by an acknowledgment that manufacturing's share of GDP has declined from 16% to 13% over the past decade. The liberalization has practical limits — most-favored-nation tariff rates have not been broadly reduced, agriculture remains shielded, and structural regulatory constraints persist — but the directional shift is meaningful. Sun Pharmaceutical's agreement to acquire Organon for approximately $11.75 billion on an enterprise basis is separately notable as one of the largest outbound acquisitions in Indian corporate history. ---

    Europe

    • The eurozone's first-quarter GDP growth fell short of consensus estimates, while April PMI data slipped into contraction territory with delivery times and input price inflation rising at their fastest pace since 2022 — a configuration increasingly consistent with stagflation.
    • The ECB and Bank of England both held rates in April but signaled June hikes as the base case, representing a dramatic reversal from February's expectations of unchanged rates throughout 2026.
    • UK Prime Minister Keir Starmer survived a parliamentary vote over the Peter Mandelson appointment controversy but faces projected losses of approximately 1,850 seats in May 7 local elections, raising questions about his political durability.
    • European bank earnings were broadly constructive: UBS reported first-quarter net income of $3.04 billion beating all estimates, Deutsche Bank beat on profit and revenue, Santander exceeded expectations, and BP reported adjusted net income of $3.2 billion — more than double the prior year.
    • S&P cut credit ratings for Belgium and Slovakia and placed Finland on negative watch, reflecting fiscal deterioration partly linked to elevated energy costs.

    Growth and the Stagflationary Pressure

    The eurozone economy unexpectedly decelerated at the start of 2026, with first-quarter GDP growth falling short of consensus estimates as surging energy costs — a direct consequence of the Strait of Hormuz blockade — weigh on industrial output and household purchasing power. Germany's government halved its 2026 growth forecast to 0.5% earlier in the month, while the Ifo Institute's business expectations index fell to its lowest level since 2023. Germany, Italy, and Spain individually outperformed first-quarter expectations in isolation, but the aggregate trajectory into the second quarter depends heavily on whether oil prices stabilize or continue to rise. April PMI data slipped into contraction territory with delivery times and input price inflation rising at their fastest pace since 2022. Euro-area consumer inflation expectations jumped across the board in March, and S&P cut credit ratings for Belgium and Slovakia while placing Finland on negative watch, reflecting fiscal deterioration partly attributable to elevated energy costs. Germany's €500 billion infrastructure stimulus fund may be the primary buffer preventing outright contraction, per Ifo President Clemens Fuest, though the fund's disbursement timeline limits its near-term impact.

    Central Banks: The Pivot Toward Tightening

    Both the ECB and the Bank of England held rates at their April meetings, in line with consensus expectations, but the accompanying signals were unmistakably hawkish. ECB President Christine Lagarde disclosed that policymakers debated an immediate increase but concluded they had insufficient information to act — language interpreted by those familiar with the deliberations as implying only a narrow path to avoiding a June hike if Hormuz-related energy disruption persists. Bundesbank President Joachim Nagel separately indicated he favors a June move absent a material improvement in the outlook. At the Bank of England, Chief Economist Huw Pill voted for an immediate increase, and Governor Andrew Bailey warned that waiting for second-round inflation effects to materialize before acting would mean responding too late. Market pricing has shifted dramatically: traders now price a June hike as the base case for both institutions, with three ECB increases priced for the year — a dramatic reversal from February's expectations of unchanged rates throughout 2026. The White House's Kevin Hassett publicly characterized potential central bank rate increases as policy errors, an unusual intervention in the affairs of independent institutions. A UK research group warned that a deepening Middle East crisis could tip the UK into recession and force aggressive rate hikes — a tail risk that may be underpriced given the country's energy import exposure.

    UK Politics: Washington, Westminster, and Local Elections

    The week produced a notable split-screen moment in UK politics. King Charles III completed a four-day state visit to Washington that included an address to a joint session of Congress — remarkable for the pointed political content of a constitutional monarch's appeal to reject isolationism and support NATO and Ukraine commitments — alongside bilateral meetings with President Trump. Trump announced the removal of some Scotch whisky tariffs as a personal gesture to the monarch, a tangible if narrowly scoped trade concession. British officials privately expressed skepticism that the royal visit would meaningfully alter Trump's underlying disposition toward Prime Minister Starmer or accelerate a broader UK-US trade agreement. Simultaneously, Starmer survived a parliamentary vote over his assurances regarding Peter Mandelson's appointment as ambassador to Washington, though the prolonged controversy constituted a costly distraction. Labour is projected to lose approximately 1,850 seats in May 7 local elections, including potentially control of the Welsh Parliament for the first time since its formation in 1999, a performance that would raise credible questions about the Prime Minister's political durability. The UK also raised its national terror threat level to severe following a stabbing in London's Golders Green district.

    Corporate and Financial Developments

    European bank earnings offered a broadly constructive read. UBS reported first-quarter net income of $3.04 billion, beating all estimates, with revenue rising nearly 30% driven by equities and foreign exchange trading; shares jumped 4.3% in Zurich on Tuesday and management confirmed intentions to add to its $3 billion share buyback program. Deutsche Bank posted higher-than-expected profit and revenue, supported by trading income and stronger contributions from asset management and retail banking. Banco Santander exceeded expectations, with its US expansion anchored by the Webster Financial acquisition providing incremental support. Standard Chartered, BNP Paribas, and Société Générale also reported first-quarter results that exceeded expectations. BP reported adjusted net income of $3.2 billion, more than double the year-earlier figure and ahead of the $2.64 billion analyst consensus, as surging oil prices and market volatility drove strong trading results. Barclays' trading division, by contrast, failed to match the outsized revenues generated by Wall Street peers. Lufthansa cancelled approximately 20,000 flights over the next six months in response to the surge in jet fuel prices, an early and visible sign of the war's operational consequences for European aviation. Adidas gained 8.3% in Frankfurt on strong apparel momentum. Bayer sought a favorable Supreme Court ruling to limit the scope of Roundup litigation that has cost the company more than $10 billion. EssilorLuxottica heir Leonardo Maria Del Vecchio agreed to buy out two siblings' stakes in family holding company Delfin for approximately €10 billion. The Netherlands announced plans to impose a 36% annual tax on unrealized gains from stocks, bonds, and cryptocurrencies effective 2028, drawing immediate concern about capital flight. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude briefly surpassed $126 per barrel on Thursday after Axios reported President Trump is scheduled to receive a briefing on new military options against Iran; WTI posted a 12% weekly gain, while Trump confirmed the naval blockade will not be lifted without a nuclear agreement.
    • The UAE's shock exit from OPEC after 60 years removes meaningful spare production capacity from the cartel's toolkit and signals a durable reorientation of Abu Dhabi's strategic posture, with potential ripple effects across the Arab League and Gulf Cooperation Council.
    • Polymarket assigns only 52% odds to the Strait of Hormuz reopening by end-June, making a protracted blockade the probabilistic base case; Oxford Economics estimates global goods trade will expand just 2.5% in 2026, roughly half the pace recorded in 2025.
    • G-7 central banks are converging toward tightening as energy-driven inflation accumulates, with the notable potential exception of the United States — a divergence that carries significant implications for currency markets and capital flows.
    • The oil shock is accelerating structural energy transitions: European EV purchases rose 44% year-on-year in March, South Korean EV sales more than doubled, and Chinese EV and hybrid exports reached a record, rising 140% from the prior year.

    The Hormuz Standoff: Escalation Risk Rises

    The Strait of Hormuz remains the dominant variable in global markets, and the week's developments suggest the conflict may be approaching an inflection point rather than winding down. Brent crude briefly surpassed $126 per barrel on Thursday after Axios reported that President Trump is scheduled to receive a briefing on new military options against Iran, and US Central Command is preparing fresh strike plans. Trump separately confirmed to Axios he will not lift the naval blockade without a nuclear agreement, removing near-term ambiguity about the conditions for resolution. The US also escalated its financial campaign, sanctioning Hengli Petrochemical's Dalian refinery — the first time Washington has targeted a major Chinese private refiner — and threatening secondary sanctions on banks supporting Chinese private refiners purchasing Iranian oil. Kpler estimates Iran has 12 to 22 days of remaining storage capacity at current production rates before output curtailments begin; Treasury Secretary Bessent indicated some well shutdowns may have already commenced. Six to eight supertankers remain clustered near Chabahar port in the Gulf of Oman, just outside the blockade perimeter. A single LNG tanker appeared to traverse the strait on Tuesday, the first such transit since the conflict began, though it remains unclear whether this represents a durable development. Iran's Foreign Minister Araghchi traveled to Moscow to coordinate with Putin, and Tehran's latest proposal — conveyed through Pakistan — calls for reopening the strait in exchange for lifting the US port blockade, with nuclear negotiations deferred to a separate track. Washington's formal response to that proposal remains to be seen. Polymarket assigns only 52% odds to reopening by end-June, making a protracted closure the probabilistic base case.

    Energy Markets and OPEC's Architecture

    The UAE's decision to exit OPEC after 60 years adds a structural dimension to the energy market disruption that extends beyond near-term price dynamics. The departure reflects a strategic imperative to maximize production before the energy transition erodes long-run demand, and removes from the cartel's toolkit the spare production capacity that served as its primary price-defense mechanism in 2014 and 2020. The exit blindsided other members and raises questions about the cartel's long-term cohesion. Reports that the UAE is reconsidering participation in the Arab League, the Organization of Islamic Cooperation, and potentially the Gulf Cooperation Council suggest a durable strategic reorientation rather than a tactical maneuver. A Turkey-Saudi axis may emerge as a counterweight, with implications for the region's post-conflict order. Separately, US crude exports reached a record above 6 million barrels per day as importing nations seek alternatives to Middle Eastern supply. US shale producers are pumping near-record volumes of 13.6 million barrels per day, well-positioned at current prices but cautious about overextension given the forward curve's implied reversion toward $80 for later-year delivery. Goldman Sachs raised its fourth-quarter Brent forecast to $90 per barrel — up nearly $30 from pre-conflict projections — though spot prices remain elevated well above that level. Exxon Mobil and Chevron both exceeded profit expectations, underpinned by elevated realized prices.

    Macro Transmission: Trade, Inflation, and Supply Chains

    The economic ramifications of the Hormuz closure are broadening in ways that are becoming harder to dismiss as transient. Oxford Economics estimates global goods trade will expand just 2.5% in 2026, roughly half the 4.9% pace recorded in 2025, with the oil shock alone potentially subtracting 0.6% to 0.9% from world trade volumes. A Dallas Fed survey found 35% of Texas manufacturers are already feeling direct negative impact from the conflict. The Bloomberg agriculture spot index hit a two-year high, up 13% over three months, as fertilizer supply disruptions and harvest uncertainty compound food price press

  • US

    • S&P 500 closed above 7,000 for the first time last week, with the index up approximately 9% in April, but the rally showed signs of fragility: fewer than half of members traded above their 50-day moving averages at the record, and volume ran 11% below the six-month average.
    • Kevin Warsh's Senate Banking Committee confirmation hearing on Tuesday and Wednesday dominated monetary policy attention, with the nominee pledging Fed independence while signaling comfort with the administration's "economic statecraft" framework and interest in overhauling inflation measurement toward trimmed-mean gauges.
    • Tesla beat first-quarter earnings estimates on Wednesday but gave back after-hours gains after announcing a $25 billion capex plan; Intel surged approximately 20% on Friday on a blockbuster AI-driven revenue forecast.
    • Meta and Microsoft announced workforce reductions totaling as many as 23,000 positions, framed by markets as AI cost discipline; Alaska Air suspended profit guidance, citing Hormuz-driven fuel costs.
    • Consumer sentiment fell to an all-time low, with household concerns about war-driven inflation and gasoline prices approaching four-year highs diverging sharply from equity market performance.

    Equity Markets

    US equity markets delivered a week of broadly constructive performance against a backdrop of persistent geopolitical uncertainty, with the S&P 500 sustaining its April advance and the Nasdaq 100 logging a 13-session winning streak — its longest since 2013 — before pausing Monday. JPMorgan raised its year-end S&P 500 target to 7,600 following the index's breach of 7,000. The rally's technical characteristics, however, warrant scrutiny: the combination of below-average volume, narrow market breadth, and declining investor sentiment during an advance to all-time highs suggests the move is being driven by a relatively small cohort of large-cap names rather than broad-based conviction. Analysts at Barclays drew a parallel to the low-volatility conditions preceding the February 2018 "Volmageddon" correction, a precedent that merits monitoring even if it does not constitute a near-term forecast.

    Corporate Earnings

    First-quarter earnings provided genuine support to the market's constructive positioning, with nearly 80% of S&P 500 companies reporting beats — a rate above the historical average. Technology remained the dominant earnings driver. Intel's Friday surge of approximately 20% in premarket trading reflected a blockbuster AI infrastructure forecast and placed the stock on course to eclipse its year-2000 record. Texas Instruments surged 12% Thursday after a stronger-than-expected outlook driven by data-center and industrial demand. The contrast with ServiceNow, which fell 13% after cutting its margin outlook and citing deal delays attributable to the Middle East conflict, illustrates the differentiation within technology: companies with direct infrastructure exposure are outperforming those dependent on discretionary enterprise software spend. Tesla's Wednesday report — $0.41 per share against a $0.34 consensus — generated a muted response once the $25 billion capex commitment became clear, as investors signaled a preference for automotive clarity over unquantified robotics ambitions.

    Monetary Policy and the Warsh Hearing

    The Warsh confirmation hearing was the week's most consequential domestic policy event, though it generated more questions than answers. The nominee's preference for trimmed-mean inflation gauges — where the Dallas Fed's measure currently reads 2.33% against core PCE's 3.0% — carries material implications for perceived distance from the 2% target and, if adopted as the policy anchor, would shift the rate path considerably. Warsh's alignment with the Bessent-Rubio "economic statecraft" framework on dollar stability also drew attention, though he was careful not to commit to specifics. Senator Thom Tillis's hold on the nomination, tied to the Justice Department's probe of Jerome Powell over Fed renovation expenditures, creates a genuine possibility that Powell remains in post beyond his May 15 term expiry — an awkward institutional interregnum that bond markets have not yet fully priced.

    Fiscal and Energy Policy

    Trump invoked the Defense Production Act to direct federal funds toward energy infrastructure, including coal, LNG, petroleum, and grid projects, as the administration faces political pressure over elevated fuel prices. The internal divergence between Energy Secretary Wright — who said gasoline prices are unlikely to fall below $3 per gallon until next year — and Treasury Secretary Bessent on the energy price outlook is a notable crack in the administration's narrative. Goldman Sachs estimated US potential GDP growth at approximately 2.3% annually through 2028, driven by roughly 2% annual AI productivity gains, a constructive structural backdrop that sits in tension with the cyclical headwinds generated by sustained energy price elevation and consumer confidence at all-time lows. ---

    Asia

    • South Korean chip exports surged 183% year-on-year in the first 20 days of April, and the Kospi reached a record, up 52% year-to-date, led by Samsung and SK Hynix as AI-driven memory demand sustains a potential supercycle.
    • Japan's cabinet approved the country's first framework for overseas lethal weapons exports since World War II, a significant strategic shift under Prime Minister Takaichi reflecting heightened threat perceptions from China and North Korea.
    • DeepSeek unveiled V4 Flash and V4 Pro preview models on Friday, citing advances in coding, reasoning, and a 1 million-token context window, driving a rally in Chinese chipmaker stocks.
    • China's People's Bank injected additional liquidity as an unexpected labor market deterioration — partly attributed to AI adoption — weighed on early-career employment, while the "national team" appeared to reduce its dominant ETF stabilization positions.
    • TSMC shares rose as much as 5.1% to a record on Friday after Taiwan's Financial Supervisory Commission moved to relax single-stock fund concentration limits, with JPMorgan estimating potential inflows exceeding $6 billion.

    China: Growth Narrative and Policy Response

    China's economic narrative carried genuine complexity this week. Recent GDP data surprised to the upside, property indicators showed tentative improvement, and equity markets have recovered to near decade highs — developments that have revived debate about whether Beijing has navigated beyond its "Japanification" moment. Two influential academic contributions framed the dispute: Rogoff and Yang at the NBER acknowledged that state-directed policy has prevented financial breakdown but warned that without structural rebalancing, managed adjustment could eventually evolve into stagnation. Eurizon SLJ's Jen and Freire argued more constructively, citing fierce domestic competition and US-China technological rivalry as productivity-sustaining forces that distinguish China's trajectory from Japan's lost decades. The debate remains genuinely unresolved, though the PBOC's mid-week liquidity injection — responding to an unexpected deterioration in youth unemployment as AI adoption accelerates — illustrated that the adjustment is not without cost. Chinese regulators also moved to restrict technology companies from accepting US capital without government approval, a measure framed as a response to Meta's acquisition of startup Manus, reinforcing Beijing's tightening control over strategic technology flows in both directions.

    Semiconductors and Technology Exports

    The AI-driven semiconductor boom continued to define the week's regional equity narrative. South Korean chip exports surged 183% year-on-year in April's first 20 days; Taiwan's export orders rose 66% in March, the fastest pace in 16 years; and SK Hynix reported a five-fold jump in quarterly profit while reaffirming plans to raise capital expenditure. Samsung shares have tripled and SK Hynix has quadrupled since late August. DeepSeek's Friday unveiling of V4 Flash and V4 Pro — with advances in agentic capabilities and a 1 million-token context window — added a Chinese dimension to the AI infrastructure theme, lifting domestic chipmaker stocks on expectations of sustained domestic semiconductor demand. Victory Giant, a Chinese Nvidia supplier, surged as much as 60% in its Hong Kong debut after raising $2.6 billion, while TSMC's record close on Friday reflected both structural demand and a technical catalyst from Taiwan's fund concentration rule relaxation.

    Japan: Strategic Realignment

    Japan's cabinet approval of a legal framework for lethal weapons exports — covering missiles, fighter jets, and warships — represents one of the more consequential strategic shifts in the region this week. Prime Minister Takaichi has absorbed the domestic political cost, with polls showing 67% opposition, in pursuit of a defense self-sufficiency agenda driven by threat perceptions from Beijing and Pyongyang. Japan's first major naval exercises with the US and Philippines — conducted simultaneously with China's western Pacific drills — underscored the widening military competition west of the First Island Chain. The Bank of Japan is meanwhile leaning toward holding rates at its next meeting, citing conflict-related uncertainty, even as March export data showed faster-than-expected growth on rebounding Chinese demand.

    Regional Geopolitics and Markets

    North Korea's five tactical ballistic missile tests early in the week were a reminder of Pyongyang's ongoing capability development, compounded by concerns about combat experience accumulating through deployments in Ukraine. Indonesia's equities declined after MSCI extended its market status review to June. Hyundai missed earnings estimates, citing US tariff disruption and Iran war-related supply chain strain. Danantara, Indonesia's sovereign wealth fund, confirmed early-stage talks to acquire Eramet's 38.7% stake in PT Weda Bay Nickel — the world's largest nickel mine — as President Prabowo moves to consolidate natural resource assets. China's IMF spring meeting defense of its trade surplus, which drew skepticism from Japan's finance minister, signals a more assertive Beijing posture on external criticism of its economic model. ---

    Europe

    • The eurozone economy unexpectedly contracted in April for the first time since late 2024, with PMI data showing a steep services sector decline as war-driven energy costs weigh on confidence and activity.
    • Germany's ZEW investor expectations index collapsed to -17.2 in April from -0.5 in March — its lowest in more than three years — against a consensus estimate of -5.8, reflecting rapid deterioration in business confidence.
    • UK Prime Minister Starmer faces mounting political pressure as former senior official Olly Robbins testified that Downing Street was "dismissive" of vetting concerns surrounding Peter Mandelson's appointment as US ambassador, with local elections next month expected to be damaging for Labour.
    • Europe's airline sector faces an acute jet fuel shortage, with Lufthansa cancelling 20,000 short-haul flights, KLM eliminating 80 return services, and EasyJet warning of a potential first-half loss.
    • The EU finalized disbursement of its €90 billion loan to Ukraine after Hungary lifted its veto, and European leaders signaled preliminary conditions for Ukraine's formal membership process, though a committed timeline is absent.

    Eurozone Macro and ECB

    The week's most significant European data point was the April flash PMI reading showing the first eurozone contraction since late 2024, driven by a steep decline in services activity. The figure crystallizes the ECB's dilemma: war-driven energy inflation is pushing headline consumer prices higher — UK CPI accelerated to 3.3% in March on a 9% monthly surge in motor fuel costs, the largest since Russia's 2022 invasion of Ukraine — while the growth picture is deteriorating simultaneously. ECB President Lagarde described this as a "double uncertainty," effectively arguing for a data-dependent approach that delays further action. Analysts at TS Lombard have raised the risk of a "Trichet 2.0" scenario, in which the ECB tightens into a weakening economy, though the base case remains a hold through June. Germany's Bundesbank estimated slight first-quarter expansion, but the ZEW collapse to -17.2 in April signals that forward-looking confidence has deteriorated sharply and rapidly, leaving the eurozone's industrial core increasingly exposed to a sustained energy shock.

    UK Politics and Fiscal Outlook

    Keir Starmer's political position eroded incrementally but persistently across the week. The central controversy — whether Downing Street was aware of Peter Mandelson's vetting failure and pressured the Foreign Office to proceed with his appointment as US ambassador anyway — gained evidentiary weight when former senior civil servant Olly Robbins testified on Tuesday that Downing Street was "dismissive" of concerns raised during the process. The episode has triggered what amounts to an open revolt among senior civil servants, a distracting and damaging conflict for a government that entered office promising institutional restoration. London Mayor Sadiq Khan offered qualified support, framing political stability as a UK competitive asset, but the calls for Starmer to resign ahead of next month's local elections signal the controversy is far from resolved. UK Chancellor Rachel Reeves received a near-term fiscal reprieve as government borrowing fell to its lowest in three years, though analysts caution that the Iran war's inflationary pass-through may erode that buffer materially.

    German Politics and Corporate News

    Chancellor Friedrich Merz faces a deeply negative approval environment, with 80% of respondents expressing disapproval in a recent poll. His measures to cushion energy price impacts — including a €1,000 worker payment intended to be voluntarily funded by employers, in which the federal government itself declined to participate — were widely criticized as inadequate. The AfD continues to gain political oxygen from coalition infighting ahead of September's eastern German state elections. On the corporate front, UniCredit CEO Andrea Orcel pressed his case for acquiring Commerzbank, arguing deep structural change is required at the German lender, even as their most recent talks failed to produce agreement. SAP reported strong cloud revenue growth, gaining 7.3% in Frankfurt, while Siemens Energy rose 4.9% after raising its annual outlook on grid and gas turbine demand.

    Aviation and Energy Stress

    Europe's aviation sector faces what may be the most acute near-term commercial challenge on the continent. Persian Gulf refineries supplying roughly 20% of EU jet fuel are effectively cut off, Asian refineries face their own crude shortfalls, and India's Reliance has prioritized domestic LPG output over jet fuel exports. Shell's Pernis refinery is operating at maximum throughput and Nigeria's Dangote refinery has raised European shipments to record levels, but these measures appear insufficient to meet summer demand. Europe's ban on Russian LNG imports took effect Saturday — poorly timed given current energy market stress — and the EU is preparing jet fuel distribution optimization measures among member states. The structural implication is that Europe is entering the summer travel season with a genuine supply constraint that pricing alone cannot resolve quickly. ---

    Global

    • The Strait of Hormuz remains effectively closed, with both the US and Iranian blockades nominally in place; Goldman Sachs estimated Persian Gulf oil output running approximately 14.5 million barrels per day below pre-war levels in April, roughly 57% below pre-conflict volumes.
    • Brent crude rose above $104 per barrel on Thursday, its fourth consecutive daily gain, while WTI traded near $94; oil markets slipped briefly mid-week on ceasefire extension news before resuming their advance.
    • Clean power surpassed coal's share of global electricity generation in 2025 for the first time since 1919, with renewables at 34% against coal's 33%, according to Ember, sharpening policy focus on energy independence.
    • Commodity trading houses — Vitol, Trafigura, and Gunvor — raised $7.5 billion in contingency liquidity at the war's outset, with preliminary first-quarter profit estimates comparable in magnitude to the $30 billion-plus earned collectively in 2022.
    • Approximately $300 billion in goods subject to US tariffs are estimated to be circumventing levies annually via transshipment, according to Altana data showing a 76% year-on-year surge in suspect transactions in 2025.

    Strait of Hormuz: Diplomacy and Market Implications

    The Hormuz impasse remained the week's defining global risk, and its evolution was characteristically volatile. Iranian Foreign Minister declared the strait "completely open" on Friday of the prior week, only for Tehran to reverse course within 24 hours after the US refused to lift its naval blockade. The US then boarded an Iranian-flagged cargo ship in the Gulf of Oman — the first such seizure since the blockade began — and separately intercepted two Iranian oil supertankers attempting to evade its blockade later in the week. President Trump's announcement of an indefinite ceasefire extension on Wednesday — delivered within 30 minutes of news that Vice President Vance's Islamabad trip had been cancelled — encapsulated the dynamic markets have priced: escalation optionality consistently resolved toward the off-ramp. The subsequent Friday sighting of the US-sanctioned supertanker Yuri carrying 2 million barrels of Iranian crude toward the strait suggested that enforcement resolve will continue to be tested. Iran's UN envoy indicated Wednesday that Tehran had received "some sign" Washington was ready to lift the blockade, suggesting backchannel communication persists even as formal talks remain stalled. Polymarket assigned only approximately a one-in-five probability to tanker traffic normalizing by end of April.

    Oil, Commodities, and Supply Chains

    The commodity dimensions of the conflict extend well beyond crude oil. The Bloomberg Agriculture sub-index has risen meaningfully since hostilities began, with wheat and rice moving higher; Bank of America flags corn as carrying the largest upside risk given mounting nitrogen fertilizer market pressure from Gulf supply disruption. Industrial metals have also rallied sharply, with copper and aluminum each up more than 11% since mid-March on a combination of supply chain disruption and structural demand from AI data center buildout and grid electrification. The copper-to-gold ratio recently touched four-decade lows, a level historically associated with commodity supercycle onset. Approximately 800 vessels remain stranded in the Persian Gulf, tankers face 3.5-day delays at the Panama Canal as diverted traffic surges, and DHL's CEO warned the global economy risks a "tipping point" if the supply shortfall persists. US shale producers, despite oil above $90 per barrel for nearly a month, have largely declined to increase drilling — citing policy uncertainty and price volatility linked to administration social media posts — with Continental Resources the only major producer to confirm expanded drilling plans.

    Energy Transition Acceleration

    The crisis appears to be functioning as an accelerant for structural energy transition. At the Petersberg Climate Dialogue in Berlin — the opening event on the road to COP31 in Turkey — delegates from nearly 40 countries framed the Hormuz disruption as a catalyst for reducing fossil fuel dependence. Battery installations are expected to jump roughly a third this year; China's solar cell exports rose 80% year-on-year in March, and EV exports surged 53%. China holds approximately 72% of cumulative long-duration energy storage installed capacity, though its dominance in that sub-segment appears less entrenched than in conventional lithium-ion, offering competitive openings for US and European developers. Brazil's program to electrify isolated Amazon communities via solar and battery storage illustrates how elevated diesel prices — a direct consequence of the Hormuz conflict — have improved the relative economics of clean alternatives, with the government projecting $171 million in subsidy savings and 800,000 metric tons of avoided greenhouse gas emissions by 2036.

    Geopolitics: Defense, Trade, and Dollar Dynamics

    Japan lifted most restrictions on lethal weapons exports for the first time since World War II, a development that drew formal warnings from Beijing about revived militarism. Chinese defense contractors made their largest-ever showing at the Defence Services Asia exhibition in Kuala Lumpur, with 192 firms and drone technologies prominently featured — a direct competitive response to the Western-aligned defense industry. The US unveiled measures targeting AI model distillation by Chinese developers, and Beijing moved to exclude Taiwan's president from African airspace by pressuring island nations to revoke overflight permits. On trade, the approximately $300 billion in tariff circumvention via Southeast Asian and Mexican transshipment is sharpening USMCA renegotiation tensions, with USTR Greer signaling potential enforcement action against Canada. Persian Gulf and Asian sovereigns seeking dollar swap lines with the US Treasury reflect the dollar's reinforced role in global trade under stress — a dynamic the IMF flagged as partly reflecting a relative deterioration in advanced-economy risk perception, with US Treasuries losing some of their traditional safe-haven premium amid fiscal trajectory concerns and questions about Federal Reserve institutional independence.

  • US

    • University of Michigan consumer sentiment fell to 47.6 on Friday — the weakest reading in the survey's 1978 history — as year-ahead inflation expectations rose a full percentage point to 4.8%.
    • The S&P 500 and Nasdaq 100 reached all-time highs on Wednesday, April 16, completing rallies of roughly 10% and 14% respectively from their March 30 lows — among the fastest recoveries of that magnitude since 1950.
    • Federal Reserve independence remains politically contested, with Trump reiterating threats to remove Jerome Powell and Senator Tillis blocking Kevin Warsh's confirmation pending resolution of a Justice Department building-renovation probe.
    • First-quarter earnings season opened with mixed results: Goldman Sachs and Fastenal disappointed, while JPMorgan, Citigroup, and Wells Fargo results were due mid-week; Netflix fell approximately 10% in premarket trading after missing earnings forecasts on Thursday.
    • Rising utility bills are emerging as the defining issue ahead of November midterm elections, with electricity costs having nearly doubled for many households over several years.

    Consumer Sentiment and the Macro Backdrop

    The week's most striking domestic data point arrived on Friday, when the University of Michigan's preliminary April consumer sentiment index printed at 47.6 — below every prior trough in the survey's history, including readings during the 1980 hostage crisis, the 1990 Gulf War, and the Covid pandemic. Year-ahead inflation expectations rose to 4.8%, their highest in decades. The political polarization embedded in sentiment surveys provides some analytical comfort — Republican respondents have historically skewed results — but even that cohort reported conditions worse than at any point during the Biden administration. March CPI had already printed at its highest in more than two years ahead of the week, briefly pushing the headline rate back above 3%, and while core and trimmed-mean measures remained below that threshold, all four main CPI components were rising simultaneously — a configuration that appears to preclude near-term Fed rate cuts. Mortgage applications fell more than 10% through March, and existing home sales appear anchored near 4.1 million annualized. The pent-up housing demand that might have driven recovery remains sidelined by financing costs.

    Equity Markets: Speed Without Precedent

    Against that macro backdrop, the equity market's behavior warrants scrutiny. The S&P 500 and Nasdaq 100 closed at all-time highs on Wednesday, April 16, having recovered all their war-driven losses in a move that several analysts described as one of the fastest recoveries from a comparable drawdown since 1950. The Nasdaq extended its winning streak to twelve consecutive sessions. By Thursday, April 17, the S&P 500 closed above 7,000 for the first time since January, on track for a third consecutive week of gains exceeding 3%. The closest historical parallel by earnings multiple and timing, as several strategists noted, appears to be March 2000. The 10-year Treasury yield sits near 4.3%, and the Shiller excess CAPE yield implies below-average prospective decade returns. First-quarter earnings estimates have declined during the rally rather than risen, making the multiple expansion difficult to justify on fundamental grounds. The Bank of America fund manager survey showed the proportion viewing US equities as overvalued at a seven-year low — a finding that reflects valuation compression from war-related selling but does not resolve the underlying tension. A basket of retail-favored shares is on track for its best month relative to mutual fund favorites since 2020, and the Roundhill Meme Stock ETF gained 54% from late March.

    Monetary Policy and Political Uncertainty

    The Federal Reserve remains firmly on hold, a posture now implicitly endorsed by Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent, who signaled twice in the week that he understands the case for waiting while the war's inflationary impact becomes clearer — a meaningful shift from his prior advocacy for cuts. That framing provides political cover for incoming chairman Kevin Warsh to avoid an embarrassing early dissent, should his confirmation proceed. The Fed's Beige Book described an economy in suspension with no near-term catalyst for movement in either direction. Markets are pricing near-zero probability of a June rate change. Separately, the Justice Department made an unannounced visit to the Federal Reserve in Washington, adding to institutional uncertainty. The White House's Economic Report of the President projected 3.5% growth in 2026 and 10-year yields falling to 3.7% — against current rates of approximately 4.28% and a CBO forecast of 4.4% for the early 2030s. The gap between administration projections and independent forecasts remains substantial.

    Earnings and Corporate News

    Beyond the headline index moves, corporate results were uneven. Goldman Sachs and Fastenal both disappointed at the open of reporting season on Monday. Netflix shares fell approximately 10% in premarket trading on Friday after the streamer's earnings forecast missed estimates, with co-founder Reed Hastings simultaneously announcing his departure from the board after 29 years. Alcoa disappointed despite elevated metal prices, with operational disruptions and higher costs weighing on results. QVC filed for bankruptcy to restructure more than $5 billion in debt — a casualty of secular retail shifts rather than the current macro cycle. A federal antitrust jury found Live Nation illegally monopolized the live events industry, setting the stage for a potential structural breakup. On BNPL, Klarna has lost more than 60% of its market value since its September IPO, with nearly half of users reporting at least one late installment payment in the past year and usage increasingly concentrated in essentials such as groceries — a sign of financial stress rather than discretionary adoption.

    Fiscal, Energy, and Political Dynamics

    Treasury Secretary Bessent confirmed that tariff rates may be restored to their pre-Supreme Court levels by July, after the court struck down emergency-powers authority for their original imposition. Atlanta Fed survey evidence suggests that the One Big Beautiful Bill's capex provisions have had limited uptake — only 20% of firms incorporated the legislation into planning, echoing the limited pass-through observed after the 2017 tax cuts. States including California, Colorado, New York, and New Jersey are accelerating large-scale renewable energy procurement ahead of federal investment tax credits expiring on July 4, with Southern California Edison alone targeting 6,000 megawatts of additional capacity. The Trump administration rejected CenterPoint Energy's request to retire a 60-year-old Indiana coal plant. On defense infrastructure, the Pentagon continues to advance large-scale hardening at military installations — rebranded as "operational readiness" rather than climate resilience — with Tyndall Air Force Base being rebuilt with flood-resistant structures following nearly $5 billion in hurricane damage. Former Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson warned on Bloomberg Television that a collapse in Treasury demand remains a tail risk, urging authorities to develop contingency plans. ---

    Asia

    • Taiwan Semiconductor's first-quarter profit surged 58% on Wednesday, April 16, with full-year revenue growth guidance raised to above 30%; Taiwan's equity market value at $4.14 trillion has overtaken the UK's to become the seventh largest globally.
    • China's first-quarter GDP expanded 5.0% year-on-year, beating consensus, though retail sales grew only 1.7% in March — the persistent demand-supply imbalance remains the central structural challenge.
    • Japan's 10-year government bond yield climbed to 2.49% — its highest since 1997 — as BOJ Governor Ueda signaled a more cautious stance on rate hikes, a notable pullback from prior market expectations.
    • Singapore became the first Asian central bank to tighten monetary policy settings in response to energy-driven inflation, a move that may signal broader regional divergence if crude remains elevated.
    • Xi Jinping had his most diplomatically active week in nearly two years, receiving delegations from Spain, Abu Dhabi, Vietnam, and Russia, while framing China as a stabilizing force against a "crumbling" global order.

    China: Growth Beats, but Structural Imbalances Persist

    China's first-quarter GDP print of 5.0% year-on-year, released on Wednesday, April 16, beat consensus expectations and offered policymakers a measure of reassurance. The headline, however, conceals a pronounced divergence: industrial output grew 5.7% in March while retail sales rose only 1.7%, falling short of forecasts. A significant portion of the export surge reflected manufacturers front-running US tariff escalation, a boost that is by definition temporary. The Canton Fair in Guangzhou provided a more granular signal of the headwinds ahead — Middle Eastern buyers, who had increasingly substituted for absent American customers since the pandemic, were conspicuously absent this year due to the conflict and flight disruption, with exporters describing conditions as potentially the worst in memory. March trade data showed export growth slowing sharply while imports surged at the fastest pace since late 2021, as elevated energy commodity purchases reflected the war's disruption to Gulf supply. The two-speed economy — robust supply side, fragile demand side — remains a central policy challenge, and the tariff environment that incentivized front-loading may itself constrain external demand in coming quarters.

    Energy Squeeze and the Teapot Refiners

    China's independent oil refiners — the so-called teapots, accounting for roughly a fifth of national refining capacity — remain under acute pressure as the primary transmission channel of Hormuz disruption into the domestic economy. Heavily reliant on discounted Iranian crude, the sector is now exposed to both US sanctions enforcement and blockade risk. Floating stockpiles of approximately 38 million barrels of Iranian crude in Asian waters provide a buffer of perhaps two months, and Beijing has released strategic reserves and granted additional import quotas. Saudi Aramco's official selling prices have risen to a record, and Riyadh's crude shipments to Chinese customers are set to approximately halve in May — to around 20 million barrels from roughly 40 million in April. China's overall oil and gas imports declined in March. Russia's potential to fill part of the gap creates a structural incentive for Beijing to reinforce that relationship, as Xi's meeting with Foreign Minister Sergei Lavrov on Wednesday made clear, with Xi emphasizing that "the stability and certainty of China-Russia relations are particularly valuable." Yuan-denominated crude oil transactions reached a single-day record of 1.22 trillion yuan through CIPS during the week.

    Geopolitical Positioning and the Xi Diplomatic Offensive

    Xi's most diplomatically active week in nearly two years — receiving Spain's Prime Minister Sánchez, Abu Dhabi's Crown Prince, Vietnam's President, and Russia's Foreign Minister — served a deliberate purpose: framing Beijing as the responsible stakeholder in a disordered world. Xi's description of the international order as "crumbling into disarray," delivered in the context of his Sánchez meeting on Tuesday, was notably stronger than his customary characterizations of global turbulence. He proposed a four-point Middle East peace plan and vowed to deepen ties with Moscow. The direct question of whether US warships will interdict vessels carrying Iranian oil to China — a scenario that would constitute a significant escalation in bilateral tensions — remains unresolved, as illustrated by the Rich Starry incident on Tuesday, when a US-sanctioned tanker linked to China transited the Strait without interdiction, challenging the blockade's credibility. A Trump-Xi summit in approximately a month's time looms as the next significant test. China has condemned the US-Israeli strikes on Iran as "unacceptable," but Xi has not publicly commented on the blockade.

    Taiwan, Japan, and Korea: AI Tailwinds Meet Macro Headwinds

    Taiwan Semiconductor's first-quarter results on Wednesday provided the clearest evidence yet that AI infrastructure investment is sustaining its own momentum largely independent of the broader geopolitical disruption. The 58% profit surge and guidance raise to above 30% full-year revenue growth drove Taiwan's Taiex to a record high on Tuesday, April 14, posting a year-to-date gain of 25%. South Korea's Kospi has rallied approximately 23%, recovering sharply from an 18% two-day decline at the conflict's onset; BlackRock flagged Korean equities as a preferred position. In Japan, the more cautious backdrop is evident: BOJ Governor Ueda's pullback on rate hike signaling on Monday, combined with the 10-year JGB yield reaching 2.49% on the same day — its highest since 1997 — reflects the difficulty of navigating tightening global financial conditions while domestic supply chains absorb naphtha shortages. Toilet manufacturer Toto suspended new prefabricated bathroom orders citing feedstock constraints, illustrative of a growing list of Japanese manufacturers cutting production or raising prices. Japan's Finance Minister Katayama warned of "bold action" to support the yen following bilateral discussions with Bessent in Washington, a statement widely interpreted as an intervention threat that received no pushback from the US side. Macro hedge funds in China suffered notable losses in March, with Bridgewater's All Weather Plus strategy declining 5.6% from late February through early April, and the peer-group average loss of 6.3% described as the worst on record. ---

    Europe

    • Viktor Orbán's 16-year tenure ended on Sunday, April 13, when Péter Magyar's Tisza party secured a supermajority in Hungary's parliamentary election; the forint surged to a four-year high on the result.
    • The ECB signaled it will likely hold rates unchanged at its April 30 meeting, with President Lagarde declining at the IMF Spring Meetings to resolve the tension between inflation vigilance and growth concerns.
    • Hermès shares fell as much as 14% on Wednesday — its largest single-day decline on record — after first-quarter sales disappointed, with the war's confidence effects cited alongside broader consumer caution.
    • ASML raised its full-year sales forecast to €36–40 billion from €34–39 billion on Wednesday, driven by AI-related semiconductor demand, with its CEO noting supply cannot meet demand "for the foreseeable future."
    • UK Prime Minister Starmer faces a deepening political crisis after it emerged that Ambassador Mandelson received security clearance despite vetting objections, leading to the dismissal of the Foreign Office's top civil servant.

    Hungary: A Structural Shift in European Politics

    Viktor Orbán's defeat on Sunday, April 13, represents the most consequential shift in European politics in several years. Péter Magyar's Tisza party secured what appears to be a parliamentary supermajority, reducing Fidesz to a rump opposition after 16 years of what one Macquarie strategist characterized as "unchecked executive power through erosion of institutional pillars." The immediate European dividend is substantial: Orbán's Hungary had repeatedly exercised veto power to block EU measures requiring unanimity, holding up approximately €90 billion in support for Ukraine and repeatedly fracturing EU sanctions consensus. Magyar has pledged to dismantle the illiberal governance structures, reorient toward Brussels and NATO, and supports eventual euro adoption. German Chancellor Merz moved quickly to press for rapid disbursement of the Ukraine loan package, meeting with President Zelensky in Berlin on Wednesday. The forint surged to a four-year high on Monday, April 14, triggering what markets have described as a "euro convergence trade." Orbán called a Fidesz party congress for April 28 to review the defeat. Complications remain: Magyar inherits an economy that has consistently underperformed regional peers Poland and Czechia, is dependent on Russian energy imports, and carries commercial ties to Asian battery manufacturers that will constrain the pace of reorientation. Veto rights over EU decisions may also prove too useful a tool to abandon entirely even under new leadership.

    ECB Policy: Genuine Uncertainty

    ECB President Lagarde's comments at the IMF Spring Meetings on Tuesday and Wednesday placed the eurozone explicitly between two scenarios without resolving them. The March baseline incorporated two rate increases in 2026; the adverse scenario projects 3.5% inflation this year. Lagarde stated the ECB has no tightening bias while simultaneously acknowledging that "looking through" the energy shock risks undermining price stability credibility — positions in tension that she did not reconcile. ECB policymaker Madis Müller separately called for vigilance on inflation risks. A Bloomberg survey published later in the week pointed to a June hike as increasingly plausible given inflation tracking above earlier projections. Bank of England Governor Bailey struck a similar note of cautious ambiguity, signaling no urgency to move while acknowledging the difficulty of assessing the war's full economic impact. Pimco indicated it sees value in European bonds following the war-driven selloff, suggesting institutional buyers view current yield levels as attractive entry points.

    Luxury Shock and Industrial Stress

    The week delivered a sharp earnings reality check for European luxury. Hermès shares fell as much as 14% on Wednesday, April 15 — its largest single-day decline on record — after first-quarter sales growth disappointed, with management attributing weakness to the confidence effects of the war alongside broader consumer caution. Kering also reported disappointing figures. LVMH's fashion and leather goods division, home to Louis Vuitton, reported sales declining in the first quarter when it reported on Monday, April 14. The contrast with ASML could hardly be more pronounced: Europe's most valuable company raised its full-year guidance to €36–40 billion on Wednesday, with its CEO noting AI demand means supply cannot meet orders "for the foreseeable future." European chemical producers face acute input cost pressures from surging fossil-fuel feedstock prices, with SKW Stickstoffwerke Piesteritz's COO warning that loss of basic chemical supply chains could trigger broader economic damage. The Port of Antwerp-Bruges, home to major operations for TotalEnergies, ExxonMobil, and BASF, is managing additional disruption from a fuel spill. Alstom fell 36% in Paris on Thursday, April 17 — its steepest single-day decline since 2023 — after withdrawing full-year free cash flow guidance. Lufthansa shares fell 4.4% on Monday following a two-day pilot strike over pensions.

    UK Political Turbulence

    Prime Minister Starmer's position deteriorated materially on Thursday, April 17, when it emerged that Washington Ambassador Peter Mandelson had received security clearance despite objections from vetting officials — a decision complicated by Mandelson's past associations with Jeffrey Epstein. Starmer's response, dismissing Foreign Office permanent secretary Olly Robbins, has been widely characterized as managing the political optics rather than addressing the underlying judgment question. Calls for resignation are growing. The episode arrives as Starmer was attempting to position the UK as a constructive international actor, co-hosting with France a summit on establishing a naval force to ensure freedom of navigation in the Strait of Hormuz — a project whose European support base remains fractured. Separately, UK household energy costs are tracking roughly £500 higher for a typical household, the RICS homebuyer appetite gauge fell to its lowest since August 2023, and Moody's warned that French elections risk stalling budget consolidation. In French telecoms, three major operators entered exclusive talks to acquire Patrick Drahi's SFR at approximately $24 billion on Thursday, a transaction that would reshape the country's mobile landscape. Italy's Meloni called Trump's remarks about Pope Leo XIV "unacceptable," a signal of growing distance from Washington that reflects wider European discomfort with the conflict's management. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude rose as much as 9.1% to approach $104 on Monday's Asian open, before falling toward $98 by Thursday as diplomacy dominated sentiment; WTI traded near $92–93 by week's end.
    • The IMF cut its global growth forecast at its Spring Meetings, with Bloomberg Economics' base case at 2.9% for 2026 — the weakest since 2020 — and a downside scenario of 2.2% if oil averages $170.
    • The London Metals Index reached a record high during the week, driven by aluminum, as JPMorgan forecast LME aluminum prices could reach $4,000 per ton — approaching the 2022 all-time high — amid the largest supply deficit in over 25 years.
    • The Strait of Hormuz remains effectively closed under formal US blockade, with only a single Pakistan-flagged tanker completing a crude cargo exit since the blockade came into force; Panama Canal congestion has reached its worst since the 2023–2024 drought.
    • Anthropic's Mythos AI model emerged as an unexpected focal point at the IMF-World Bank Spring Meetings, with central bank governors from the ECB, Bank of England, and New York Fed all raising cybersecurity concerns publicly within days of each other.

    The Hormuz Blockade: Strategy, Execution Risk, and Market Pricing

    The formal US naval blockade of the Strait of Hormuz, announced after the collapse of US-Iran talks in Islamabad over the weekend, represents a material escalation in a conflict that has disrupted global energy markets since the February 28 strikes. The strategic logic, as articulated by advisers close to the situation, is to close Iran's revenue mechanism while simultaneously working to reopen the mined half of the Strait through demining, escorts, and insurance backstops — thereby pressuring Tehran to concede politically. The approach carries substantial execution risk: US warships operating in closer proximity to Iranian drone and missile capabilities face a rapid escalation cycle should any vessel be struck. Iran responded on Monday by threatening that "no port in the Persian Gulf and Sea of Oman will be safe" if its own facilities are targeted. The UK confirmed it will not participate in the blockade. The mechanism's credibility was directly challenged on Tuesday when the US-sanctioned tanker Rich Starry — linked to China — transited the Strait without apparent interdiction, and two Iran-linked vessels were later observed testing alternative corridors between Larak and Qeshm islands. By Thursday, only a single Pakistan-flagged tanker had completed a crude cargo exit since the blockade came into force, illustrating the gap between diplomatic tone and operational reality. Trump expressed optimism about a permanent ceasefire on Thursday, claiming Iran had conceded on nuclear ambitions and Hormuz access — assertions Tehran has not publicly confirmed. Gulf Arab and European officials believe a durable agreement is more likely six months away.

    Macro Consequences: Growth, Trade, and Supply Chains

    The IMF's Spring Meetings in Washington provided a sobering backdrop, with Bloomberg Economics' base case projecting global growth of 2.9% in 2026 — the weakest since 2020 — and a downside scenario of 2.2% if oil averages $170 a barrel. Global trade volume growth is now projected at 2.8% for 2026, below global GDP growth of 3.1%. Qatar's Finance Minister described current conditions as "the tip of the iceberg," and economist Carmen Reinhart argued the world is less prepared to respond to a crisis than at any point in the past two decades, constrained by a $348 trillion global debt burden, central banks still contending with inflation, and a fragmented multilateral order. Global Trade Alert has tracked more than 250 distinct government policy responses since hostilities began — the large majority liberalizing import measures, energy subsidies, or demand-reduction programs. Panama Canal congestion has reached its worst since the 2023–2024 drought, with vessels facing 3.5-day waits and one ship paying $4 million to jump the queue. The Port of Los Angeles chief estimates that even after the Strait reopens, full shipping normalization will take approximately four and a half months. The IEA issued a striking forecast: global oil demand is expected to decline in 2026 for the first time since the pandemic, as the price surge offsets underlying demand growth.

  • US

    • US equity markets posted a seven-session winning streak by Friday, the longest since October, as the Iran ceasefire supported a broad risk-on recovery, with high-yield spreads returning to pre-war levels.
    • March CPI is expected to show the sharpest monthly headline increase since 2022, driven by a roughly $1-per-gallon gasoline surge, complicating Federal Reserve policy at a moment when labor markets are softening.
    • Fed funds futures remain priced for zero cuts in 2026, a stark reversal from two cuts fully priced before hostilities began, with swaps implying inflation above 3% over the next twelve months.
    • The Supreme Court's invalidation of emergency-powers tariffs and a Court of International Trade order mandating $160 billion in refunds to importers represent a significant fiscal and legal development that received less attention than geopolitical headlines.
    • An unusual joint action by Fed Chair Powell and Treasury Secretary Bessent summoned Wall Street CEOs to an emergency meeting over concerns that Anthropic's Mythos AI model could enable a new generation of cyberattacks, signaling that regulators are treating advanced AI as an emerging systemic risk.

    Labor Market and Inflation Dynamics

    Friday's March non-farm payrolls, released during the Good Friday holiday, showed a gain of 178,000 — the strongest print since 2024 — and the Sahm Rule currently shows no recessionary signal. The underlying picture is more nuanced, however. Wage growth is slowing across multiple measures, JOLTS hiring is near post-GFC lows, quit rates are declining, and average unemployment duration has re-extended above six months. The composite portrait is one of a labor market softening gradually rather than collapsing, consistent with a pre-recessionary plateau rather than durable resilience. Against that backdrop, March CPI — due Friday — is expected to show a roughly 0.9–1.0% month-on-month headline gain, the sharpest reading since June 2022, driven principally by gasoline's move above $4 per gallon for the first time since 2022. Retail diesel has risen nearly 50% to $5.62 per gallon, and the Logistics Manager's Index climbed to 65.7 in March, its highest since May 2022. The ISM non-manufacturing survey reinforced the concern, with more than 70% of purchasing managers reporting rising input prices — the largest monthly increase in 13 years.

    Federal Reserve and Monetary Policy

    The Fed's March meeting minutes, released Wednesday, offered insight into how policymakers are weighing the energy shock against mixed labor signals, though the core policy dilemma remains unresolved. Fed Vice Chair Philip Jefferson acknowledged the Iran conflict will push near-term inflation higher while characterizing current policy as appropriate. Bloomberg Economics now projects only two cuts in 2026, down from four forecast in January, and Fed funds futures have effectively priced out easing entirely. The five-year/five-year inflation breakeven remains below 2.2%, suggesting markets tentatively treat the energy shock as transitory, but that assessment is fragile and contingent on whether the Strait of Hormuz reopens in practice. Kevin Warsh's confirmation as incoming Fed chair remains pending in the Senate. The February core PCE data point — due Thursday and expected to show a 0.4% monthly gain keeping the year-on-year rate above 3% — will provide an additional read on whether the inflation impulse is broadening beyond energy.

    Equity Markets and Corporate Developments

    US equities navigated a turbulent week with notable resilience. The S&P 500 oscillated sharply around the ceasefire announcement — futures gained 2.6% on Wednesday and the index recorded its longest winning streak since October — while sector rotation was pronounced. Health insurers rallied sharply Monday after CMS proposed a 2.5% Medicare Advantage rate increase for 2027, with Humana gaining 11% and UnitedHealth rising 7%. Semiconductor and technology names benefited disproportionately from the ceasefire, with Micron rising nearly 10% and Western Digital gaining 8.3% on expectations of restored helium supply chains. Energy names moved in the opposite direction, with Exxon Mobil down 5.8% and Occidental falling 8.1% on ceasefire day. An index of SaaS stocks fell 4.8% on Thursday to its lowest in over three years, extending a roughly 40% decline in 2026, as Anthropic's Claude Managed Agents release and a new Meta model renewed fears that AI agents will erode incumbent software competitive moats. FactSet projects S&P 500 first-quarter earnings growth of approximately 12.5% year-on-year, with Goldman Sachs and JPMorgan Chase results due next week. SpaceX has begun investor meetings for a potential IPO that could value the company above $2 trillion, and Morgan Stanley launched a Bitcoin-tracking ETF under the ticker MSBT, underscoring the asset class's institutional entrenchment even as Bitcoin trades near $71,000, roughly 40% below its October peak. ---

    Asia

    • Samsung Electronics reported an eightfold increase in quarterly profit driven by AI memory chip demand, with shares rising as much as 4.9% in Seoul on Tuesday, underscoring how semiconductor demand has become a stabilizing force for the Korean technology complex amid broader regional stress.
    • China's CSI 300 advanced 3.5% on Wednesday following the ceasefire announcement, though optimism faded quickly, with the index giving back 0.6% on Thursday as oil prices partially rebounded and the structural energy import burden reasserted itself.
    • Xi Jinping met Friday with Kuomintang Chairwoman Cheng Li-wun — the first KMT leader to visit Beijing in a decade — in a move carrying explicit geopolitical signaling ahead of the anticipated Trump-Xi summit in mid-May.
    • India's foreign investors sold $17.8 billion in equities over 23 consecutive sessions — a record outflow pace — as elevated crude prices amplified inflation fears in an economy sourcing roughly half its crude from the Middle East.
    • China's factory-gate prices exited deflation in March, with PPI rising 0.5% year-on-year after a 0.9% decline in February, though Capital Economics cautioned the turn may not persist.

    China: Policy Reorientation and Trade Dynamics

    China's economic policy framework appears to be undergoing a meaningful structural shift. Rather than deploying demand-side stimulus in response to the energy cost surge triggered by the Iran conflict, Beijing is firmly committed to a supply-side reorientation articulated in its latest Five-Year Plan. State support is being directed toward technology firms and "new productive forces" rather than infrastructure or consumer transfer payments — a posture that Premier Li Qiang reinforced at last month's China Development Forum by rejecting external criticism of China's trade surpluses and signaling no course correction. Urban private-sector wage growth fell below 1% by end-2025, against a pre-pandemic rate of approximately 8%, and the absence of numerical consumption targets in the Five-Year Plan reinforces the view that domestic demand will remain structurally subdued. The implication — that China's already-record trade surplus is likely to persist or widen — sits in direct tension with rebalancing calls from Washington and Paris. On a more constructive note, EV and hybrid exports surged 140% year-on-year in March to a record 349,000 units, with BYD, Geely, and Chery leading shipments, though BYD's domestic sales fell more than 40%, illustrating the continued bifurcation between export competitiveness and subdued internal demand.

    China: Energy, Finance, and Geopolitics

    Beijing granted independent teapot refiners additional crude import quotas to sustain domestic fuel production, a practical measure revealing the extent to which China's energy security was stretched by the Hormuz closure. BloombergNEF projects Chinese LNG imports will fall to 62.3 million tons in 2026 from 68.4 million tons last year — a rare decline in nearly two decades of near-uninterrupted growth — with elevated prices and supply risks expected to persist regardless of ceasefire terms. China's central bank purchased its largest gold tranche in more than a year in March, consistent with continued reserve diversification. On the geopolitical front, Taiwan's KMT chair began a six-day Beijing visit Monday — later culminating in the Friday Xi meeting — with Xi calling for "cooperation and peace" while simultaneously reaffirming sovereignty claims, a combination calibrated to demonstrate to Washington that Taipei contains significant constituencies favoring engagement. The KMT has stalled a $40 billion Taiwanese defense expansion, and a $14 billion US arms deal has not received Trump's approval. Chinese Foreign Minister Wang Yi also traveled to North Korea for the first time in six years, reflecting Beijing's effort to consolidate relationships across shifting geopolitical alignments.

    Japan, India, and Regional Dynamics

    Japan's Prime Minister Sanae Takaichi marks six months in office this week, a tenure that has delivered a credible pivot away from deflation and a sustained rise in JGB yields, though the galvanizing equity effect of her election has been largely offset by the Iran war shock. Real wages rose at the fastest pace since 2021, supporting the case for continued Bank of Japan normalization, but consumer confidence fell in March by the most since the early stages of the Covid pandemic, and the yen continues to weaken despite higher domestic yields — a divergence that appears increasingly unstable. Seven & i Holdings delayed the planned US listing of its convenience store operations, sending shares down 4.6% on Thursday. In India, the Reserve Bank held rates unchanged Wednesday, with Governor Sanjay Malhotra acknowledging the ceasefire while cautioning that supply chain disruptions continue to pose risks. The rupee strengthened 0.5% following his remarks, partially reversing a roughly 7% decline over the past year. State Bank of India separately disclosed approximately $5 billion in speculator positions against the rupee caught by the regulator's crackdown, with estimated losses of around $32 million from forced unwinds. India's record equity outflow pace and visible fuel shortages on the state election campaign trail indicate the energy shock is transmitting broadly into domestic politics. ---

    Europe

    • The ECB's next policy move now appears likely to be a rate increase rather than a cut, potentially as early as this month, as energy-driven inflation reasserts itself — a significant reversal from the easing path anticipated at the start of the year.
    • ASML declined 4.7% in Amsterdam on Tuesday after US lawmakers introduced legislation to tighten chip tool export restrictions to China, with significant implications for one of Europe's most strategically important technology companies.
    • Hungary's Sunday election represents Viktor Orbán's most serious electoral test in sixteen years, with the outcome carrying direct implications for EU Ukraine policy, Russia sanctions architecture, and Central European political alignment.
    • UK Prime Minister Starmer traveled to the Gulf Wednesday to leverage Britain's diplomatic role on Hormuz, chairing a meeting of approximately 35 nations — notably in the absence of the US — in an effort to organize multilateral pressure on Iran.
    • Sodexo plunged 20% in Paris on Friday after issuing weaker-than-expected revenue and margin guidance, while Universal Music Group surged 11% in Amsterdam after Bill Ackman proposed a deal valuing the company at approximately $65 billion.

    ECB Policy and Eurozone Macro

    The European Central Bank's policy path has shifted materially over the course of this week. Economists now view the next move as a rate increase rather than a cut, as energy-driven inflation from the Middle East conflict reasserts itself across the eurozone. The euro area's five-year/five-year inflation breakeven has risen, though it remains below 2% and below comparable US readings — a differential that reflects Europe's somewhat more anchored medium-term expectations but does not eliminate the tightening pressure. German industrial production data due Thursday arrived in the context of pre-existing structural weakness, with declines recorded even before the onset of hostilities, suggesting the energy shock is compounding rather than causing a cyclical downturn. The EU's senior economy official explicitly warned of stagflation risk despite the ceasefire, a concern that eurozone services PMI data due Tuesday and retail sales and PPI figures due Wednesday will help calibrate. Earnings estimate revisions have turned negative for European equities even as US estimates hold in positive territory — a reversal from the earlier-year narrative in which European stocks were outperforming on fiscal stimulus and ECB easing expectations.

    UK: Politics, Markets, and Diplomacy

    Britain's political and economic week was unusually eventful. Prime Minister Starmer's Gulf trip Wednesday positioned the UK as an active multilateral diplomatic actor on Hormuz, chairing a 35-nation coordination meeting in the absence of the US — a deliberate differentiation from Washington's posture that Starmer has also deployed as a domestic political asset ahead of May local elections, criticizing both Reform UK's Nigel Farage and Conservative leader Kemi Badenoch for their initial support of the US-Israeli campaign. The political strategy faces headwinds, however: Labour has slipped to fourth place in national polling, a notable deterioration. The economic backdrop is challenging — real wages are stagnating, consumer prices remain elevated, and UK mortgage rates rose above 5% in March, with homebuyer demand falling to its lowest since August 2023. The Bank of England, previously expected to cut, is now seen holding. UK fuel exposure is structurally significant: BloombergNEF identified Britain and France as the European markets most exposed to diesel and kerosene supply disruption, given reliance on refined product imports from Kuwait and Saudi Arabia respectively. On a more constructive note, UK EV registrations hit a record 86,120 units in March, illustrating that the energy shock is simultaneously pressuring affordability and accelerating electrification.

    European Corporate and Political Developments

    Corporate news was active across the continent. Unilever's €44.8 billion sale of its food business to McCormick & Co. contributed to a record global M&A first quarter, though war-related deterioration in earnings multiples may weigh on second-quarter activity. H&M's CEO continues to face investor skepticism over a turnaround strategy squeezed between Inditex at the premium end and Shein and Primark at value — a credibility problem compounded by elevated fuel costs and weakening consumer confidence. In Italy, Prime Minister Meloni ousted the CEO of state-backed Leonardo while retaining the heads of Eni and Enel, a recalibration of executive leadership at state firms following a referendum defeat. Former Italian Prime Minister Conte called for deeper engagement with China following repeated US tariff threats. The US formally requested European NATO allies submit concrete plans within days to help secure Hormuz, a shift from rhetorical commitment to operational demand that has deepened transatlantic friction — with Trump publicly raising the Greenland issue again after NATO members declined to participate in the Iran operation or offer basing rights. UBS confirmed it will not hand over privileged documents to the Credit Suisse Nazi-linked accounts investigation. France moved to fast-track emergency agricultural legislation to address a deteriorating food trade balance. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude surged above $140 per barrel on Tuesday — its highest level since 2008 — before falling as much as 16% on Wednesday following the US-Iran ceasefire announcement, one of the largest single-day oil price declines in market history.
    • The Strait of Hormuz, effectively closed since US-Israeli strikes began February 27, remains functionally disrupted: only three vessels transited on Wednesday following the ceasefire, against a pre-war norm of roughly 135 per day.
    • Global M&A recorded its strongest first quarter on record at approximately $1.3 trillion, roughly 20% above the prior year, though deal values have declined around 15% since the war began, indicating a meaningful frontloading effect and growing caution.
    • Bloomberg Economics' aggregate measure of advanced-economy interest rates now sits approximately 35 basis points higher at end-2026 compared with projections from three months ago, reflecting widespread recognition that energy infrastructure damage may prove durable.
    • Pakistan's emergence as the key ceasefire mediating channel — leveraging ties with Saudi Arabia, Iran, the US, and China — reflects a meaningful shift in regional diplomatic influence, with Vice President Vance leading US-Iran talks in Islamabad on Saturday.

    Energy Markets and the Hormuz Crisis

    The defining macro variable of April 2026 is the Strait of Hormuz, which has been effectively closed since the US-Israeli offensive against Iran began on February 28. The waterway normally channels approximately one-fifth of global oil and LNG supply; the EIA estimates the conflict has removed approximately 314 million barrels from global supply to date — roughly 80% of the IEA's record emergency reserve release. Brent crude's prompt spread widened above $10 per barrel in backwardation early in the week, surpassing peaks reached after Russia's invasion of Ukraine, before the ceasefire announcement on Wednesday delivered Brent's sharpest single-day fall since April 2020. WTI tumbled nearly 19% on Wednesday before recovering approximately 4% on Thursday as Tehran alleged ceasefire violations and Israeli strikes in Lebanon intensified. By Friday, WTI held near $99 per barrel, on track for its steepest weekly loss in nine months but still commanding a material premium above pre-conflict levels. OPEC+ has warned that damage to Middle Eastern energy infrastructure will produce prolonged supply disruption extending well beyond any eventual ceasefire. Saudi Arabia separately disclosed that Iranian attacks have impaired its own production capacity, providing a partial floor to prices. Goldman Sachs has flagged Brent averaging above $100 through 2026 if the Strait remains closed for another month.
