Japan is reportedly considering releasing the equivalent of twenty days of consumption from strategic reserves.

    Ceasefire, Diplomacy, and Geopolitical Reconfiguration

    The US-Iran two-week ceasefire, brokered with Pakistan as intermediary and announced Tuesday evening, has provided intermittent relief to markets without resolving the underlying conflict. The historical parallel to the 1991 Kuwait crisis — in which oil more than doubled before returning to pre-crisis levels within twelve months — is instructive, though the current episode appears to be resolving more rapidly. Several Arab Gulf states reported continued Iranian missile attacks in the immediate aftermath of the ceasefire announcement, and both Kuwait and the UAE subsequently reported fresh strikes, underscoring the arrangement's fragility. Iran's stated intent to levy fees on vessels transiting the Strait introduces a novel dynamic that, if implemented, would effectively transform an international waterway into a controlled one. China's role in persuading Iran to accept a ceasefire — credited by Iranian officials to a last-minute Beijing push — illustrates the growing influence Beijing derives from its $270 billion financial footprint across the Gulf. Xi's position is nuanced: quietly facilitating de-escalation while avoiding overt alignment with either side, constrained by the same Gulf relationships that give him leverage. French President Macron publicly called on medium powers to assert greater independence from Washington, and not a single NATO member or Gulf ally directly joined the US-Israeli campaign — a structural erosion of the alliance architecture that predates this conflict but has been materially accelerated by it.

    Global Monetary Policy and Financial Conditions

    The conflict's most durable macroeconomic legacy may be the repricing of global monetary policy trajectories. The Fed, ECB, and Bank of England are all navigating an asymmetric pressure: energy-driven inflation constrains easing while decelerating growth limits tightening. The Fed Reserve Bank of New York's Global Supply Chain Pressure Index rose in March to its highest since January 2023. Foreign central bank holdings at the Federal Reserve Bank of New York fell approximately $82 billion since late February to $2.7 trillion — the lowest since 2012 — as Gulf producers liquidate Treasuries to defend currencies and fund wartime expenditures, representing a structural erosion of the petrodollar recycling mechanism. Emerging market capital flows, which had reached near two-decade highs earlier in 2026, suffered approximately $60 billion in equity outflows during the crisis, with the shock differentiated primarily by energy import dependence. Wednesday's ceasefire rally partially reversed those outflows. The dollar has risen roughly 2.2% since hostilities began, partially reversing an 8%-plus decline in 2025, though analysts characterize the move as reflecting safe-haven flows rather than a structural shift. The IMF is poised to cut its global growth forecasts, while the World Bank has urged governments to keep any fiscal response transparent, temporary, and targeted. The energy shock is simultaneously generating a coal renaissance in Asia — as LNG alternatives become unavailable — and accelerating EV adoption, illustrating the conflict's contradictory implications for the energy transition.

  • US

    • S&P 500 posted its worst monthly performance since September 2022, declining approximately 7.8% in March, with the index sitting roughly 8–9% below its pre-war peak entering April.
    • Average US gasoline prices crossed $4 per gallon for the first time since August 2022, prompting Georgia to implement a 60-day gas tax holiday and several other states to explore similar measures.
    • Iranian President Pezeshkian's overture toward negotiations on Tuesday triggered the S&P 500's best session in nearly a year, though the move reflected short-covering as much as fundamental reassessment.
    • Fed Chair Powell and NY Fed President Williams signaled policy is well-positioned, while bond managers at Pimco, JPMorgan Asset Management, and Columbia Threadneedle began shifting emphasis from inflation risk toward the growth-shock scenario.
    • The week marked the one-year anniversary of Liberation Day tariffs, with the administration preparing to announce a tiered steel and aluminum tariff restructuring and 100% tariffs on pharmaceutical imports reportedly imminent.

    Equity Markets

    US equity markets spent the week oscillating between geopolitically driven relief rallies and late-week risk reduction, a pattern that has defined trading since the Iran conflict began five weeks ago. The S&P 500 ended March down approximately 5% on the month — its worst since September 2022 — and the Nasdaq 100 sat roughly 11.5% below its October all-time high entering the week. Tuesday produced the index's best single session in nearly a year after Iranian President Pezeshkian indicated to EU officials that Tehran was open to ending hostilities, but trading desks were quick to note that the magnitude of the move owed more to a short squeeze against heavily bearish end-of-quarter positioning than to a durable shift in conviction. By Thursday, S&P 500 futures had given back roughly 1.1%, continuing the pattern of cumulative gains in the first half of each trading week followed by net losses on Thursdays and Fridays — a dynamic reflecting investor reluctance to carry risk through weekends when major geopolitical announcements have consistently occurred. Corporate earnings added modest color: Nike fell nearly 10% in premarket trading after issuing a weaker-than-expected full-year outlook, while aluminum producers Alcoa and Century Aluminum each rose approximately 8.5–8.7% in premarket Monday trade following Iran's weekend strikes on Gulf production facilities.

    Monetary Policy and Fixed Income

    The Federal Reserve's calibration challenge intensified through the week. Fed Chair Powell, speaking Monday, noted that longer-term inflation expectations appear anchored, and New York Fed President Williams described policy as well-positioned — language that markets read as signaling no imminent move in either direction. That posture is being tested: Treasury yields rose across the curve after Trump's Thursday address, with traders rapidly paring expectations for rate cuts as oil surged above $107. The competing pressures are genuine and unresolved — the US manufacturing ISM prices-paid subindex climbed to 78.3 in March, its highest since mid-2022, while the growth outlook is deteriorating. Bond managers at several major asset managers have begun shifting their analytical emphasis toward the demand-destruction scenario, arguing the surge in yields has run its course. The Friday nonfarm payrolls report — consensus expecting 60,000 jobs added after a 92,000 decline in February — was released when equity markets were closed, leaving the first clean read on the real economy's absorption of the shock to land into an asymmetric market open.

    Fiscal and Trade Policy

    This week's Liberation Day anniversary arrived against a backdrop that complicates the administration's industrial policy narrative. Foreign direct investment into the US totaled $288.4 billion in 2025, a modest decline from 2024 and the lowest of the Biden era, providing limited evidence that tariffs have catalyzed a manufacturing investment surge as claimed. Bloomberg Economics estimates that 43% of US imports — roughly $1.4 trillion in goods — currently remain tariff-free, a proportion frequently overlooked in broader commentary. The Supreme Court's February ruling that the original tariffs were illegal triggered a $170 billion refund process that adds fiscal complexity. Looking ahead, the administration's preparation of a tiered steel and aluminum tariff restructuring and reportedly imminent 100% pharmaceutical tariffs suggests the trade policy agenda remains active even as the Middle East crisis consumes political bandwidth. State-level fiscal responses to the oil shock — led by Georgia's 60-day gas tax holiday — are providing near-term consumer relief but carry revenue implications for already-stretched state budgets.

    Consumer Backdrop

    The household sector is entering the shock with a narrowed buffer. The savings rate declined to 4.5% from 6.4% in January 2024, and while fuel expenditure as a share of disposable income remains near historic lows at approximately 1.6%, the $4-per-gallon threshold carries significant political resonance in a midterm election year. Potentially more consequential than pump prices is the equity wealth channel: corporate equities now represent over a quarter of household net worth — a record in eight decades of Federal Reserve data — meaning that equity market performance may prove the more potent transmission mechanism to consumer spending. Public transit agencies are reporting early ridership upticks consistent with prior oil shocks, though the durability of any behavioral shift at current price levels remains uncertain. ---

    Asia

    • Asian benchmark indices fell approximately 13% in March — the steepest monthly decline since October 2008 — with South Korea, Japan, and India each recording drawdowns of roughly 10%, while Chinese equities declined a comparatively contained 5.5%.
    • China's official manufacturing PMI returned to expansionary territory in March for the first time this year, though input costs surged sharply and smaller export-oriented firms reported deteriorating conditions in the private Caixin survey.
    • India faced compounding pressures: the RBI capped lenders' daily FX positions at $100 million, global funds withdrew approximately $12 billion from Indian equities since the conflict began, and LPG shortages began disrupting the food service sector.
    • Japan's spring Shunto wage negotiations secured average increases exceeding 5% for a third consecutive year, while the Nikkei surged approximately 5% on Wednesday before partially reversing after Trump's Thursday address.
    • BYD reported overseas sales of 120,083 vehicles in March, a three-month high, and raised its 2026 export target to 1.5 million units, as elevated fuel prices appear to be accelerating EV adoption globally.

    China: Relative Resilience Under Pressure

    China entered the week as Asia's relative outperformer, a position it largely maintained. The official manufacturing PMI returned to expansion in March, supported by ramped-up fiscal spending and AI infrastructure demand. Chinese equities declined approximately 5.5% since hostilities began — meaningfully less than the roughly 10% drawdowns recorded in South Korea, Japan, and India — a divergence that appears to reflect structural investments in renewable energy, diversified export markets, and strategic oil reserves. Beijing's policy response revealed its priorities clearly: the People's Bank of China withdrew cash from the financial system for the first time in approximately a year — a cautious move that keeps options open without signaling a pivot — while authorities instructed independent "teapot" refiners to maintain 2025 output levels or risk losing future import quotas, effectively socializing margin losses among smaller processors to ensure downstream fuel availability. On the diplomatic front, Beijing and Pakistan issued a five-point joint peace proposal calling for the restoration of Hormuz shipping, signaling that China's calculus is shifting from passive beneficiary toward active mediator as economic costs accumulate. The China-Pakistan proposal coincided with the PBOC's unusual financial system tightening, illustrating the competing demands on Chinese policymakers navigating energy security, growth support, and diplomatic positioning simultaneously.

    Corporate and Technology Developments

    Several significant corporate developments emerged from the region. Alibaba released its Qwen3.5-Omni multimodal model on a closed-source basis — a departure from its usual open-source practice that may signal a shift toward commercialization. Zhongji Innolight, an Nvidia optical components supplier, confidentially filed for a Hong Kong IPO targeting roughly $3 billion, illustrating continued momentum in AI-adjacent hardware even as broader sentiment remains pressured. TCL Electronics announced a $475 million acquisition of a 51% stake in a joint venture absorbing Sony's home entertainment business — a transaction that would position the combined entity above Samsung in global TV market share at approximately 16.7%, exemplifying Chinese manufacturers' strategy of acquiring premium Western brand equity to penetrate higher-margin consumer segments. President Xi Jinping also invited Kuomintang Chairwoman Cheng Li-wun to visit China from April 7–12, the first such visit by a sitting KMT leader in a decade, providing Beijing a platform to discuss Taiwan's US weapons purchases with an interlocutor who has publicly questioned her island's defense spending levels, one month ahead of Trump's scheduled Beijing summit.

    Korea, Japan, and Southeast Asia

    South Korea experienced the most dramatic volatility of the week. Having entered the year as the world's best-performing market on AI optimism, Seoul approached bear market territory by end-March before surging to its strongest single session since 2001 on Wednesday following Trump's negotiation signals — a move led by Samsung and SK Hynix on revived semiconductor demand sentiment. The government announced a supplementary budget to cushion households and businesses while President Lee Jae Myung deployed a $17 billion support package, as inflation nudged above the 2% target. South Korea also signaled it is considering extending driving restrictions to the general public should Brent breach $120 per barrel. Japan's spring Shunto wage negotiations secured average increases exceeding 5% for a third consecutive year, with base pay recording its largest year-on-year gain in more than 33 years, while total employment is rising despite population contraction as female participation and immigration liberalization expand the labor pool. The Nikkei fell as much as 4.5% at Monday's open before Japan's top currency official delivered his strongest intervention warning yet. Across Southeast Asia, Vietnam is emerging as a structural beneficiary of trade diversion, surpassing China as the leading US supplier of laptops and gaming consoles last year, though its exposure to the tariff policies driving that shift represents a meaningful vulnerability. The broader ASEAN manufacturing bloc saw PMI readings fall to their weakest since September, with the Philippines declaring a national emergency over energy supply. India's rupee staged its largest single-day gain in over a decade on Thursday after the RBI banned key offshore derivatives and forced the unwind of speculative positions, though structural pressure from elevated energy import costs persists. ---

    Europe

    • Euro-area CPI rose to 2.5% in March — the steepest monthly acceleration since March 2022 — though the reading came in below the 2.6% consensus, and core and services inflation actually softened relative to February.
    • European equities fell 7.6% in March, the worst monthly performance in nearly four years, with LVMH, SAP, and Novo Nordisk collectively accounting for more than half of the €420 billion in market capitalization erased in Q1.
    • ICE Gasoil diesel futures broke above $200 per barrel on Thursday — the highest since 2022 — as tankers divert refined product flows toward higher-bidding Asian buyers.
    • Norway is emerging from its driest winter in decades, with snow reserves at two-decade lows and an estimated 25 terawatt-hour energy deficit, reducing electricity exports to the UK by approximately 50% year-on-year and compounding the imported energy shock.
    • Germany's leading economic institutes cut their 2026 growth forecast to less than half their prior projection, warning that potential growth may approach zero in the medium term due to demographic decline and declining hours worked.

    Inflation and Monetary Policy

    The March euro-area CPI print of 2.5% — below the 2.6% consensus — provided the ECB's doves with modest cover, given that the overshoot relative to February was concentrated in energy rather than core or services categories. The divergence between headline acceleration and underlying softness is the central analytical tension for the Governing Council. Bank of Italy Governor Fabio Panetta emphasized the need to prevent a wage-price spiral while keeping policy proportionate to the mandate; ECB Governing Council member François Villeroy de Galhau signaled readiness to act against unanchored inflation expectations but stopped short of indicating specific action; and ECB Executive Board member Cipollone spoke Wednesday without materially shifting the forward guidance calculus. Bloomberg Economics assessed April rate hike odds as low given the data undershoot, but the ECB's institutional memory of its slow response to the 2022 shock is clearly shaping communications toward vigilance regardless of near-term data. The Bank of England faces a structurally different exposure: regulated energy pricing and inflation-linked wages leave the UK comparatively vulnerable to energy shocks relative to euro-area peers, and a Bank of England survey showed businesses planning larger-than-normal price increases. UK house prices unexpectedly rose, suggesting consumer demand held up at the onset of the conflict, though the durability of that resilience against sustained energy cost pressure remains to be seen.

    Energy Stress and Supply Disruption

    The diesel market emerged as the week's most acute European stress point. ICE Gasoil futures breaking above $200 per barrel on Thursday reflected not just the Hormuz disruption but a global arbitrage dynamic: tankers loading diesel in UK waters are diverting to Australia and other high-bidding destinations rather than discharging locally, while Atlantic-crossing cargoes originally bound for Europe are rerouting to Africa. The market appears to be signaling that European buyers will need to pay more to attract supply. Norway's energy deficit compounds the picture — an estimated 25 terawatt-hour hydropower shortfall from the driest winter in two decades has reduced electricity exports to the UK by approximately 50% year-on-year and to Germany by around 40%, while Nordic prices have surged to more than four times 2025 levels in parts of the region. The dual squeeze — imported energy shock plus domestic hydropower deficit — is arriving precisely as European gas markets remain under Persian Gulf supply pressure. The EU is actively considering reactivating emergency energy measures last used after Russia's 2022 invasion, and the European Commission has proposed limited ETS adjustments to cushion industry competitiveness.

    Corporate and Political Developments

    LVMH shares fell 28% in the first quarter — the worst start to a year in the company's history — serving as a proxy for deteriorating global consumer confidence among high-net-worth cohorts. Unilever announced advanced talks to sell most of its food business to McCormick, with a final announcement potentially imminent. Meg O'Neill assumed the role of CEO at BP on Wednesday, becoming the first woman to lead a major oil company, inheriting a business that receives near-term revenue support from elevated prices but faces deepened long-term questions about capital allocation and transition strategy. Greece received a notable upgrade from MSCI to developed-market status, effective during the May 2027 index review — a milestone in its recovery from the sovereign debt crisis that saw it demoted in 2013. In the UK, London's youth unemployment reached approximately one in four 16-to-24 year-olds seeking work, with graduate job postings falling from roughly 13,000 in 2019 to just over 2,000 at the start of 2026 — a political risk for Keir Starmer's Labour government ahead of municipal elections. French authorities detained two additional suspects linked to a foiled bomb plot near a Bank of America office in Paris, which officials believe is connected to the Middle East conflict. Chinese brands' share of fully electric car sales in Europe rose approximately two percentage points to 8% of the EV market, nearly double their share from a year prior, as elevated fuel prices accelerate the adoption of alternatives to internal combustion vehicles. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude advanced above $107 per barrel by Thursday — a gain of more than 40% since the Iran conflict began approximately five weeks ago — with WTI closing above $100 for the first time since hostilities started on Monday.
    • The Strait of Hormuz, which under normal conditions sees approximately 135 vessel transits daily, was averaging fewer than six commercial transits per day; the IRGC is reported to be demanding payment in yuan or cryptocurrency for safe passage under re-flagged registries.
    • Fertilizer prices — particularly urea and ammonia, with nearly half of global urea supply originating in the Persian Gulf region — are rising sharply, raising concerns about food security in sub-Saharan Africa and parts of South Asia.
    • The WTO's 14th ministerial conference ended without agreement on renewing the 28-year e-commerce tariff moratorium, with the US Trade Representative declaring the organization's role "limited" and pivoting toward bilateral agreements.
    • Nuclear proliferation debates are intensifying across US allies, from Germany and Poland exploring deeper integration with France's nuclear umbrella to rising public support in South Korea and renewed discussion in Japan.

    The Hormuz Chokepoint and Oil Markets

    The Strait of Hormuz remains the defining macro variable for global markets. An Iranian drone struck a fully laden Kuwaiti oil tanker at anchor near Dubai on Tuesday, one of the most significant vessel attacks of the conflict, temporarily pushing WTI toward $107. WTI recorded its largest monthly gain since May 2020, rising more than 50% in March and closing above $100 for the first time since hostilities began. Brent, reflecting non-US supply dynamics, broke above $107 following Trump's Thursday address. The divergence between crude prices and refined products is notable: jet fuel in Europe surged to all-time highs equivalent to over $215 per barrel equivalent, while benchmark crude remains in the low triple digits — a gap explained by record IEA emergency reserve releases, a well-supplied market entering the conflict, and traders' reluctance to hold leveraged positions into weekends. Options markets span downside hedges to speculative bets on $450 per barrel oil, reflecting the absence of directional conviction rather than any particular scenario. Energy consultancy FGE NexantECA warns that if near-closure of Hormuz persists for six to eight weeks, Brent could reach $150–$200; Bloomberg Intelligence estimates an additional surge to $140 if Houthi strikes on Red Sea shipping resume. Approximately 20,000 seafarers remain stranded in and around the Strait, and Emirates Global Aluminium has halted operations at its Al Taweelah smelter following missile and drone strikes on Gulf production facilities.

    Strategic and Diplomatic Landscape

    Trump's Tuesday signals and Thursday address encapsulate the week's central tension: the president simultaneously claimed the war is "very close" to completion and pledged to hit Iran "extremely hard" over the next two to three weeks, while declining to define exit conditions. The Wall Street Journal reported Monday that the administration is considering ending the campaign without requiring the Strait to reopen — a significant departure from US policy documents published four months prior that listed Hormuz access as a core national interest. Iran's President Pezeshkian published an open letter addressed directly to the American public on Thursday, a move that appears calibrated to exploit divisions within Trump's political base rather than advance formal diplomatic channels; the IRGC, which retains effective strategic control, has shown no inclination toward ceasefire discussions. China and Pakistan issued a five-point joint peace proposal calling for Hormuz shipping restoration. Polymarket probabilities assign a 60% chance that the war concludes in April and only 20% that it extends beyond June — a distribution that has shifted sharply following Pezeshkian's remarks but rests on diplomatic conditions that may prove difficult to formalize. Secretary of State Rubio declared NATO's response "very disappointing," calling out allies for denying base access — a statement that may presage a formal reassessment of the US-NATO relationship once the conflict concludes.

    Second-Order Effects: Trade, Food Security, and Proliferation

    The conflict's cascading effects are spreading beyond energy markets into commodity supply chains, institutional architecture, and security postures. UNCTAD projects global merchandise trade growth of 1.5–2.5% for 2026, a sharp deceleration from 4.7% last year. US naphtha exports reached a single-month record of approximately 15 million barrels in March as Japanese buyers substituted US Gulf Coast supply for unavailable Middle Eastern cargoes. Fertilizer prices — urea and ammonia in particular — are rising sharply given the Persian Gulf's roughly 50% share of global urea supply, with fuel shortages already spreading to sub-Saharan Africa: Kenya, Ethiopia, and Zambia are reporting station outages. Nuclear proliferation debates are intensifying with a breadth and speed that may prove structurally significant regardless of how the current conflict resolves, with Germany and Poland exploring deeper integration with France's nuclear umbrella, and the Trump administration reportedly considering both resuming nuclear testing after more than three decades and sharing enrichment technology with Saudi Arabia. The Russia-Ukraine conflict is re-intensifying as spring conditions return to the battlefield, with diplomatic attention consumed by the Gulf crisis reducing pressure on Moscow. The voluntary carbon market suffered a setback as Koko Networks — a World Bank-backed clean cookstove operator serving approximately 1.3 million Kenyan households — filed for bankruptcy in January, depressing Corsia aviation offset prices from approximately $15 to $12.85 and reinforcing methodological credibility concerns ahead of mandatory airline compliance beginning in 2027.

  • US

    • S&P 500 futures whipsawed across the week, declining sharply on Monday before partially recovering midweek on ceasefire diplomacy, with the index now down roughly 5% since hostilities began — a relative resilience that reflects as much caution on the short side as genuine fundamental optimism.
    • Fed funds futures briefly priced a non-trivial probability of a rate hike at the April meeting, the first time since 2023 that outcome was live, as oil-driven inflation repricing combined with a weakening growth outlook to produce a textbook stagflationary configuration.
    • Goldman Sachs raised its 12-month US recession probability to 30%, projecting unemployment at 4.6% by year-end; the OECD put US inflation at 4.2% for 2026, 1.2 percentage points above its December forecast.
    • The Pentagon's $200 billion supplemental funding request for the Iran conflict threatens to push the 2026 federal deficit toward $2 trillion, roughly double Treasury Secretary Bessent's original 3%-of-GDP target.
    • Apollo Global Management capped redemptions from its $25 billion credit vehicle at 5% after clients sought to withdraw 11.2%, the most visible sign yet of mounting stress in private credit markets.

    Equity and Credit Markets

    US equity markets spent most of the week in defensive mode, with institutional desks accumulating cash rather than taking directional positions. Bank of America's latest fund manager survey recorded the largest jump in cash allocations since March 2020, with holdings reaching 4.3%. The S&P 500 has now logged five consecutive weeks of losses — its longest such streak since 2022 — though would-be bears remain reluctant to press the short side, given the memory of April 9, 2025, when the index surged 9.5% on tariff relief. Monday's intraday reversal, when markets briefly rallied on Trump's Truth Social post claiming "productive conversations" with Tehran before quickly fading as Iran denied any negotiations, illustrated the structural difficulty of pricing an adversarial, social media-dependent foreign policy process. Vanda Research data showed Monday marked the first net selling day for individual stock buyers since 2023, with retail risk appetite declining sharply from February highs.

    Monetary Policy and the Stagflation Bind

    The Federal Reserve's position deteriorated meaningfully across the week. Entering 2026, rate cuts were consensus; by week's end, futures were briefly assigning a non-trivial probability to a hike at the April meeting. Three Fed officials expressed growing concern over inflation risks, with at least one explicitly ranking the oil price spike above labor market deterioration in the hierarchy of policy concerns. Chair Powell has signaled the committee will not even fully engage the question of whether to "look through" energy-driven inflation until the separate tariff impact has been assessed — a sequencing that effectively extends the Fed's holding pattern. Fed Governor Barr indicated rates may remain steady "for some time." The anticipated arrival of Kevin Warsh as the next Fed chair is also beginning to influence market pricing, arguably reinforcing the hawkish margin and complicating the debasement narrative that had underpinned gold's January surge.

    Fiscal and Corporate Developments

    The fiscal picture darkened as the week progressed. The Pentagon's $200 billion supplemental request, the Supreme Court's earlier invalidation of most of the tariff regime reducing anticipated revenue, and oil-driven inflation suppressing the probability of rate relief have combined to put Goldman Sachs economists projecting the deficit will exceed 6% of GDP through 2029. US import costs rose in February by the most in nearly four years, suggesting inflationary pressure was building before the Middle East conflict began. On the corporate side, unusual oil futures trading in the two minutes before Trump's Monday Truth Social post — approximately 6 million barrels changing hands against a five-day average of 700,000 — drew regulatory scrutiny. Apollo's redemption cap and the jury verdict finding Meta and Alphabet liable in a social media addiction case added to a corporate news backdrop that, while not individually decisive, collectively reflects a tightening risk environment. Average US diesel prices surpassed $5 per gallon by week's end, and United Airlines' CEO warned of potential fare increases of up to 20%, with Bloomberg Intelligence estimating sustained elevated jet fuel prices would raise US carrier costs by 15–23%.

    Housing and Energy Policy Tensions

    The housing market entered the spring selling season under visible stress. Thirty-year fixed mortgage rates climbed back to 6.4%, a six-month high, reversing sub-6% readings seen as recently as late February. KB Home lowered full-year guidance, citing the conflict as a contributing factor, while industry contacts at a JPMorgan homebuilder conference described conditions as "fragile." The administration's "drill, baby, drill" posture meanwhile faces a practical contradiction: US E&P stocks have risen only around 30% against a 40% rise in Brent, and the consumer impact — retail gasoline up roughly a third from a month ago — complicates the political calculus in ways the administration appears not yet to have fully resolved. Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac's $200 billion mortgage-backed securities purchase directive sits in evident tension with the broader rate environment. ---

    Asia

    • Asian markets suffered their sharpest single-session decline since the conflict began on Monday, with MSCI Asia Pacific falling 3.7%, South Korea's Kospi tumbling 6.5%, and multiple regional currencies — the Korean won, Indian rupee, and Philippine peso — reaching multi-year or record lows.
    • China's relative insulation from the energy shock — rooted in renewables, EV penetration, and continued access to Russian and discounted Iranian crude — has positioned Beijing as a relative regional winner, with port throughput running more than 6% ahead of year-ago levels through mid-March.
    • South Korea, the Philippines, Vietnam, and Japan have all moved to emergency energy footing, with Manila declaring a national energy emergency and Tokyo executing its largest emergency oil stockpile release on record.
    • The Trump-Xi summit has been confirmed for May 14–15 in Beijing, providing a tentative diplomatic anchor for US-China relations, though Beijing has not formally confirmed the dates.
    • India faces a distinct BRICS-chair dilemma: unable to unify the bloc around a common position on the conflict while navigating its own acute energy vulnerability, with LPG delivery times stretching from five to twenty-five days in some regions.

    China's Strategic Positioning

    Xi Jinping's studied silence on the Iran conflict has crystallized into a coherent strategic posture. China's structural advantages — an accelerating renewables buildout, electric vehicles comprising roughly half of new car sales, and continued access to discounted Russian and Iranian crude following a limited US waiver on tanker-loaded Iranian oil — mean Beijing faces fewer immediate economic pressures than its regional peers. State-owned refiners have begun quietly exploring purchases of Iranian crude, with representatives of National Iranian Oil Company sounding out Asian buyers and China representing the most consequential potential customer. The development reflects Beijing's careful navigation between energy security and US diplomatic sensitivities. Simultaneously, China launched retaliatory trade investigations into US supply chain and renewable energy practices ahead of the May summit — staking out negotiating leverage rather than signaling premature accommodation. ChangXin Memory Technologies reportedly more than doubled revenue to approximately $8 billion in 2025, and Moonshot AI is exploring a Hong Kong IPO, reflecting continued momentum in China's AI and semiconductor sectors even amid the broader disruption.

    Energy Emergency Across the Region

    The contrast between China's relative resilience and the acute stress facing most of the rest of Asia is striking. South Korea stood up an emergency economic task force, implemented vehicle-usage restrictions in the public sector, and its prime minister invoked the need to prepare for "worst-case scenarios" — a signal of genuine institutional concern, compounded by reported redeployment of US air defense assets from the Korean Peninsula toward the Middle East. South Korean exports to China surged 69% in the first twenty days of March, with semiconductor shipments up 164%, illustrating how AI-driven trade flows are partially offsetting energy disruption. The Philippines declared a national energy emergency, with President Marcos warning grounded aircraft remain a "distinct possibility." Vietnam Airlines suspended select domestic routes. Japan executed its largest emergency oil stockpile release on record and its Finance Ministry made market inquiries about potential crude futures intervention. The IEA's executive director Fatih Birol, speaking in Canberra, characterized the supply disruption as equivalent in magnitude to the combined effect of the two 1970s oil crises and the 2022 European gas crisis — and emphasized that Asia bears disproportionate exposure given its structural dependence on Gulf crude.

    Geopolitical Realignment

    The energy crisis appears to be accelerating a cautious but meaningful reassessment of US alignment across the Indo-Pacific. Manila's exploration of cooperative oil and gas development with Beijing in disputed South China Sea waters — despite deepening US-Philippines security ties since 2022 — is the most striking illustration. President Marcos's approach reflects a wider pattern: the Iran war is forcing governments to weigh the costs of US alignment against immediate energy survival needs, particularly as Washington simultaneously applies trade pressure and reduces regional security commitments. India's dilemma as BRICS chair is similarly revealing — unable to unify a bloc spanning multiple sides of the dispute while managing its own acute vulnerability, with Prime Minister Modi discussing Hormuz directly with Trump. Wall Street banks including Bank of America, Citigroup, and Goldman Sachs have revised China's inflation forecasts upward for 2026, and BofA has withdrawn its call for two rate cuts. Bloomberg Economics analysis suggests China's property downturn is approximately 70% complete, with stabilization potentially achievable by 2027 — a considerably more benign trajectory than Japan's two-decade correction.

    Japan and the BOJ Path

    Japan's largest labor union secured average wage increases above 5% for a third consecutive year, maintaining the Bank of Japan's gradual tightening trajectory. However, rising oil prices combined with yen weakness are stoking stagflation concerns that complicate that path, prompting the Finance Minister to flag potential currency intervention and driving a rise in Japanese super-long bond yields. Sumitomo Mitsui Financial Group is reportedly developing contingency plans for a potential opportunistic takeover of Jefferies Financial, and Nintendo reduced Switch 2 production guidance by more than 30%, citing weaker-than-expected US holiday demand and rising memory chip costs. ---

    Europe

    • UK ten-year gilt yields briefly breached 5% this week, surpassing even the extremes of the Liz Truss episode in duration if not velocity, as markets pivoted from pricing at least two 25 basis-point cuts to pricing at least two hikes by year-end.
    • The S&P Global Eurozone Composite PMI fell to 50.5 in March from 51.9 in February, with the largest deterioration in future output expectations since Russia's invasion of Ukraine and input prices rising at their fastest pace since February 2023.
    • Italy is preparing to cut its 2026 GDP growth forecast to as low as 0.5%, and the OECD concluded the UK faces the largest output hit among G7 nations this year.
    • Goldman Sachs estimates euro area headline inflation could rise by nearly 3 percentage points over the next year in a severely adverse oil scenario — the largest projected impact among major economies.
    • Trump's US envoy to the EU warned that American LNG could be redirected to other buyers if Brussels fails to accelerate trade deal progress, making explicit the energy-as-leverage dimension of the transatlantic relationship.

    Sovereign Bond Stress and Monetary Policy

    UK gilt markets were the most acute developed-economy sovereign stress point of the week. Ten-year yields briefly breached 5% before partially retracing following Trump's temporary extension of his strike deadline, returning to levels last seen before the Global Financial Crisis. The driver this cycle is less directly fiscal than in 2022 and more about the Bank of England's constrained policy options: markets have pivoted sharply from cutting to hiking expectations as inflation break-even spreads over US equivalents — which had narrowed under the Labour government's stabilizing market credibility — reversed sharply. UK gilts remain on track for their worst monthly performance since the Truss-era crisis. Bank of England Governor Bailey attended an emergency cabinet meeting convened by Prime Minister Starmer, underscoring the severity of the dual fiscal-monetary stress the UK faces. British factory input costs rose in March at the fastest pace in more than three decades. The ECB, by contrast, is moving more deliberately. President Lagarde used the ECB Watchers Conference in Frankfurt to signal the bank will not act until it has "sufficient information on the size and persistence" of the energy shock — retaining more credibility cushion than the Bank of England, though ECB Vice President-designate Vujcic explicitly warned that stagflation risk is increasing and that the institution must remain "very agile and vigilant."

    Growth Deterioration and Corporate Developments

    The first comprehensive economic health check since the conflict began confirmed deterioration across the eurozone. Germany's composite PMI declined more than expected; France recorded a third consecutive month of contraction in services. Italy is revising its 2026 GDP forecast toward 0.5%, and UK retail sales declined for the first time in three months. Goldman Sachs Chief Economist Hatzius noted that his estimates "may underestimate the nonlinear impact" of a severe gas shock — an unusual acknowledgment of tail risk not captured in central forecasts. German economist Isabella Weber drew a pointed parallel to the early weeks of 2020, when catastrophe was evident but its full dimensions still unclear. On the corporate side, Italy's Poste Italiane made a €12.5 billion offer for full control of Telecom Italia, with Poste shares falling 7% on Monday and Telecom Italia rising 4.2%. Pernod Ricard entered preliminary discussions to acquire Brown-Forman, owner of Jack Daniel's, with Brown-Forman Class B shares rising as much as 21% and Pernod falling 5.7% in Paris. Henkel reached a deal to acquire hair-care brand Olaplex for $1.4 billion, with Olaplex shares surging nearly 50%. Novo Nordisk's market capitalization has declined by nearly $500 billion over twenty-one months as patent expirations in India and China enable generic GLP-1 drugs at prices as low as $14. Airfare disruption adds further consumer cost, with Asia-Europe route prices rising as much as 560% this month according to Alton Aviation.

    Political Developments

    European political news was active across multiple fronts. In France, Socialist Emmanuel Grégoire won the Paris mayoral race, extending the left's hold on the capital, while Marine Le Pen's National Rally fell short in its bid to take control of major cities — a result that may temper expectations ahead of next year's presidential election. In Germany, Chancellor Merz's CDU dislodged the Social Democrats from power in Rhineland-Palatinate after thirty-five years, adding complications for the SPD within the governing coalition. Slovenia's Prime Minister Golob secured a narrow victory over nationalist Janša in the country's closest election on record, with coalition negotiations expected to be protracted. Italian Prime Minister Meloni suffered a referendum defeat on judicial reform, a self-inflicted setback approximately a year before a general election. UK Prime Minister Starmer, whose prediction market probability of exiting office before year-end stands at 68%, signaled publicly he will not be pressured by Trump, while Trump's criticism of NATO's refusal to support US military operations — warning he would "remember" the alliance's response — added institutional tension across the Atlantic. Germany is separately drafting contingency plans to counter US pressure, including the potential targeting of White House-aligned tech firms in any future trade dispute. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude traded near $108–$113 per barrel across the week, on pace for its largest monthly gain since 1990, with BloombergNEF's base case at $106.70 for April and a mid-June Hormuz reopening scenario implying $154.40.
    • Gold posted its worst weekly decline since 1983 — down 10.5% — before recovering modestly on ceasefire headlines, with the metal now roughly 18% below its January peak above $5,000 per ounce.
    • Trump extended his military strike pause by ten days to April 6, citing diplomatic progress, though Iran publicly dismissed US overtures and continued missile and drone attacks on Gulf states and Israel through the week.
    • The OECD formally revised its 2026 global growth projection, leaving it unchanged at 2.9% while trimming 2027 by 0.1 percentage point to 3.0% and raising G20 average inflation to 4.0% from 2.8% in December.
    • Reports that Iran is permitting Hormuz passage for vessels paying for oil in yuan have prompted Deutsche Bank to flag the conflict as a potential catalyst for erosion in petrodollar dominance.

    The Strait and the Strategic Impasse

    The US-Iran conflict, now one month old, remains the organizing fact of global markets. Trump's weekend ultimatum — threatening to bomb Iranian power plants unless the Strait of Hormuz was reopened within forty-eight hours — was effectively rolled back Monday after markets sold off sharply, with the deadline extended by five days and subsequently by a further ten, to April 6, following what the administration described as diplomatic progress. Iran publicly rejected those characterizations and continued striking Gulf states and Israel throughout the week. Former MI6 chief Alex Young assessed that the US "lost the initiative to Iran about two weeks ago." The structural difficulty is that even a ceasefire would not immediately resolve the Strait question: a CBS report indicated approximately a dozen Iranian mines are now present in the waterway, raising questions about the timeline and logistics of any reopening. Iran's reported draft legislation to impose passage fees on commercial vessels transiting the strait — and its reported practice of permitting passage for vessels paying in yuan — represents a form of de facto toll extraction and monetary leverage that persists regardless of near-term political outcomes. Prediction markets place the probability of a ceasefire by end of April at no better than fifty-fifty.

    Energy Markets and the Commodity Transmission

    The supply disruption is, by the IEA's assessment, comparable in magnitude to the combined effect of the 1970s oil shocks and the 2022 European gas crisis. Dallas Fed modeling suggests a Strait shutdown lasting through June would reduce global GDP growth by 2.9 percentage points next quarter. Qatar's Ras Laffan LNG complex — the world's largest — has sustained damage estimated to require years to fully repair, affecting both near-term supply and long-run investment calculus. Iran stopped natural gas exports to Turkey following an Israeli strike on the South Pars gas field. Saudi Arabia and the UAE have reportedly taken steps toward joining the conflict on the US-Israeli side, including Riyadh granting US military access to King Fahd Air Base. Saudi tanker freight rates from Yanbu — the Red Sea alternative routing — have eased somewhat as capacity arrives but remain well above pre-conflict levels. The Brent-WTI spread has widened to levels exceeding those seen during the Ukraine invasion, reflecting the US's relative insulation as a major domestic producer. Urea, the world's most widely used nitrogen fertilizer, has reached its highest level in more than three years, with the risk of a feedback loop from energy into food prices real and precedented in the 2008 rice crisis. A suspected sea-drone attack on a crude tanker in the Black Sea added a further dimension to energy market risk. Iran appears to be a net financial beneficiary of the disruption — as the only major exporter currently able to utilize the strait freely, Tehran has reportedly earned hundreds of millions in incremental oil revenue since hostilities began.

    Gold, the Dollar, and Financial Markets

    Gold's behavior continues to challenge conventional safe-haven narratives. The metal fell 10.5% in its worst weekly decline since 1983 before recovering modestly on ceasefire headlines — the selloff appearing to reflect a confluence of overbought positioning entering the conflict, dollar strength as an alternative haven, and the shift in rate expectations undermining the debasement trade thesis. Turkey sold approximately $8 billion — around sixty tons — of gold reserves this month to defend its currency, a pace larger than concurrent ETF outflows; should other energy-importing central banks follow, the narrative of central banks as consistent net buyers that has underpinned gold's more-than-doubling since 2022 would face meaningful revision. The Bloomberg Dollar Spot Index is on track for its largest monthly gain since July, an approximately 2% advance that complicates the global inflation picture by raising local-currency oil and food costs for importing nations. JPMorgan strategists turned bullish on the dollar for the first time in a year, with speculative positioning flipping from the most net-short in five years to net-long. The correlation between gold and real yields that anchored its valuation framework through the post-GFC era appears to have broken down; whether this represents a structural regime change or a temporary dislocation remains genuinely uncertain.

    Geopolitical Realignment and the Energy Transition

    The conflict is functioning as a stress test for the post-Cold War architecture simultaneously across multiple dimensions. Nations that had pivoted away from China and Russia toward deeper US alignment are reassessing that strategy under the combined pressures of trade tariffs, energy disruption, and reduced security commitments — a dynamic visible from Manila to Ankara. European Commission Executive Vice-President Ribera argued explicitly this week that fossil fuel market disruption would prove "a very relevant driver of the green economy," and early data suggest the conflict is accelerating EV adoption, rooftop solar uptake, and electrification of cooking across several markets. Pakistan stands to save an estimated $6.3 billion this year by substituting solar for oil and gas at elevated prices — a structural response the war may be advancing beyond what the energy transition alone would have achieved. Satellite data show elevated flaring at impacted Gulf facilities, adding an underappreciated emissions dimension to the conflict's footprint. The EU-Australia free trade agreement, concluded this week in Sydney with European Commission President von der Leyen taking an implicit swipe at US tariff policy and Chinese critical minerals restrictions, reflects Europe's broader effort to diversify strategic partnerships amid simultaneous pressure from multiple directions. The honest assessment is that the range of outcomes remains wide, and current market pricing may not fully reflect the left tail of the distribution.

  • US

    • The Federal Reserve held rates at 3.5%–3.75% on Wednesday, with Chair Powell signaling cuts are increasingly distant and colleagues raising the possibility of a hike — generating the S&P 500's largest Fed-day decline since 2024.
    • US gasoline averaged $3.70 per gallon as of Sunday March 16, up roughly 27% since hostilities began February 28, with diesel surpassing $5 per gallon for the first time since December 2022.
    • Futures markets eliminated all expectations for 2026 rate cuts by Friday, a dramatic reversal from the two cuts priced at the week's open.
    • Super Micro tumbled 22% on Friday after federal prosecutors charged a co-founder with illegally diverting Nvidia-powered servers to China; FedEx climbed roughly 10% after raising its earnings forecast.
    • The Fed's institutional standing came under pressure on multiple fronts: a prolonged Powell succession dispute, a Justice Department challenge to his authority, and a Supreme Court-linked tariff refund process still unresolved.

    Monetary Policy

    The Federal Reserve's Wednesday meeting was, by some margin, the most consequential in years. The FOMC voted 11-1 to hold the benchmark rate at 3.5%–3.75%, but the surrounding communication was unmistakably hawkish. Powell acknowledged "meaningful" internal movement toward fewer cuts and explicitly noted that some colleagues had raised the possibility of a rate hike — a formulation that rattled markets. The Fed's median year-end headline inflation estimate was revised upward to 2.7% from 2.4%. Core PCE was running at an estimated 3% in February, a full percentage point above target, and February producer price data showed core PPI rising to a three-year high. As Carl Weinberg of High Frequency Economics observed, the pipeline was already accelerating before the Strait of Hormuz blockade added a separate inflationary layer. By Friday, futures markets had fully eliminated expectations for any rate reductions this year. The 10-year Treasury yield rose to approximately 4.30% by week's end.

    Institutional Uncertainty

    The Fed's policy challenge was compounded by an unusually fraught succession picture. Powell confirmed he will not vacate his board seat — which runs to January 2028 — until a Justice Department investigation into the Fed's building renovation project concludes. Nominee Kevin Warsh faces a Senate confirmation hold from Senator Thom Tillis on the same grounds. The result is a protracted, politically charged transition at the central bank at precisely the moment when clarity of purpose is most valuable. Separately, top Justice Department officials are rallying behind a US attorney's challenge to Powell's authority, with the White House declining to oppose the effort — a development whose implications for central bank independence remain to be seen but warrant close monitoring.

    Energy and the Consumer

    The transmission from oil prices to household budgets is now clearly visible in the data. SMBC Nikko Securities estimates the gasoline price increase could reduce the personal saving rate by 60 basis points to approximately 3.9%. University of Michigan consumer sentiment fell to a three-month low in the March preliminary reading. January consumer spending was essentially flat even before the energy spike, and households no longer hold pandemic-era savings buffers. Oxford Economics cautioned that higher energy costs threaten to suppress spending decisions materially in coming months. The political arithmetic is uncomfortable: gasoline at levels last seen in 2023, tariff-driven price pressures still in the pipeline, and a midterm election cycle approaching. President Trump called for an emergency rate cut — a move associated historically with acute crises — while Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent floated lifting sanctions on approximately 140 million barrels of Iranian crude already at sea, a measure analysts regard as operationally limited given virtually all such supply flows to Chinese refiners.

    Corporate Developments

    The week produced a notably mixed corporate tape. Nvidia's annual developer conference was constructive, with CEO Jensen Huang projecting $1 trillion in AI processor sales through 2027, though his preference for copper interconnects over fiber optics weighed on photonics names. Meta gained roughly 3% in premarket Monday on reports of a $27 billion cloud infrastructure deal and a planned workforce reduction exceeding 20%. Lululemon fell approximately 2% after forecasting a second consecutive year of profit declines. Micron Technology, whose market capitalization has risen from roughly $100 billion at end-2024 to approximately $520 billion, reported after Wednesday's close. On Friday, Goldman Sachs's trading desk noted clients who anticipated a quick resolution to the Iran conflict are increasingly doubtful, with some positioning for either a sharp correction or a prolonged 2022-style grind lower. ---

    Asia

    • China's January–February industrial production rose 6.3% year-on-year — the fastest pace since September — though analysts caution the Hormuz closure poses a material threat to sustaining that momentum.
    • Asian buyers scrambled for alternative crude supplies, with US oil purchases for April loading reaching approximately 60 million barrels — the highest single-month volume since April 2023.
    • South Korea's Kospi surged 5% on Wednesday after authorities restricted value-dilutive subsidiary listings; Samsung Electronics rose 7% on signals it is exploring multi-year memory chip contracts.
    • The Indian rupee weakened past 93 per dollar to a record low by Friday, with Nomura projecting it could reach 96 by end-June; Goldman Sachs projected 95 over the next year.
    • The Bank of Japan held rates unchanged at its Thursday meeting, with Governor Ueda citing Middle East uncertainty, while Japan's February exports to the US fell 8% year-on-year ahead of Prime Minister Takaichi's White House visit.

    China: Resilience Under Pressure

    China entered the week with the most encouraging domestic data of any major Asian economy. Industrial production expanded 6.3% year-on-year in January–February, retail sales exceeded forecasts, and fixed-asset investment turned positive after a period of contraction. Chinese equities regained some footing, and Jefferies' Christopher Wood argued that mainland equities are the most defensible in the current environment, citing robust domestic renewable capacity, battery storage, and reserve buffers as partial insulation from the energy shock. Beijing also began 2026 with its fastest pace of government spending growth since 2022, suggesting fiscal stimulus is being deployed to sustain momentum. The risk, as Gavekal Research flagged, is that rising commodity prices compress manufacturer margins — particularly where cost pass-through to consumers is limited — potentially triggering further investment pullback and necessitating additional easing. China's 2026 growth target of approximately 5% relies heavily on exports to non-US markets, making an oil-driven global slowdown a material threat.

    China: Geopolitical Positioning

    China remains the central geopolitical pivot of the Iran conflict. Chinese state media responded with undisguised scorn to US appeals for help reopening the Strait of Hormuz, and the structural leverage Washington once held through tariff threats has diminished, partly via Supreme Court rulings and partly due to China's commanding position in rare earth supply chains. Beijing appears likely to engage on its own terms and timetable, if at all. The Trump-Xi summit, originally planned for late March, was postponed to approximately mid-May — a delay Beijing reportedly views with quiet relief, given the diplomatic complications of hosting Trump while Iran war operations continue. A Paris meeting between senior officials appeared constructive, and the trade truce remains intact, though a pending $14 billion US arms package for Taiwan represents a significant latent irritant. Industry consultant FGE NexantECA indicated Beijing is close to drawing on its estimated 1.4 billion barrels of commercial oil reserves as the Hormuz disruption persists. Alibaba and Tencent combined lost approximately $66 billion in market value after results from both disappointed investors seeking a clearer path to AI monetization; Alibaba's quarterly net income fell 67%.

    Japan and South Korea

    Japan faces a specific supply chain vulnerability from tightening naphtha supplies, with multiple petrochemical firms announcing production cuts. The Bank of Japan held rates at its Thursday meeting but maintained a conditional tightening bias, with analysts noting a potential move as soon as April if inflation data cooperate. Prime Minister Takaichi characterized her White House meeting as "extremely difficult": Japan's pacifist constitution forecloses overseas military deployments, February exports to the US fell 8% year-on-year, and car exports by value declined 14.8%. Takaichi is expected to offer pledges of additional US investment, potentially in nuclear energy, as a diplomatic offset. South Korea's governance reform story was the week's most constructive equity narrative — the Kospi surged 5% on Wednesday and now trades above 5,900, with Samsung's multi-year contract signaling a potential structural improvement in memory market dynamics.

    Emerging Asia

    India occupies a particularly exposed position. The rupee weakened past 93 per dollar to a record low, Prime Minister Modi secured safe passage for LPG tankers through Hormuz — placing India alongside China and Russia — while managing US relations and preparing for April state elections that will test his handling of the energy crisis. Indonesia's central bank delivered a hawkish hold while tightening foreign-exchange regulations to defend the rupiah, which is down approximately 14% in March. The Philippine peso fell past 60 per dollar. Pakistan's airstrikes on Kabul, reported to have killed at least 400 people at a drug treatment facility, represent a separate and serious escalation risk that global markets have not yet fully priced. ---

    Europe

    • The ECB held its key rate at 2.0% on Thursday but dropped language describing policy as in a "good place"; money markets are now pricing two quarter-point hikes this year.
    • UK 10-year gilt yields rose to their highest level since 2008 following the Bank of England's Thursday statement that it "stands ready to act" against inflation, as a unanimous hold was accompanied by a sharp hawkish shift in tone.
    • European natural gas futures jumped as much as 35% on Thursday after Iran struck Qatar's Ras Laffan LNG complex, driving benchmark prices to more than double their pre-war levels.
    • UniCredit launched a $40 billion bid for Commerzbank, potentially clearing the path toward a full acquisition of Germany's second-largest lender.
    • Unilever confirmed it is evaluating a sale of its food division and received an offer from McCormick, valued by Barclays at approximately €28–31 billion.

    Central Banks: A Hawkish Pivot

    The week's most consequential European macro development was the unmistakable shift in central bank posture. The ECB held at 2.0% on Thursday — a rate in place since last June — but the surrounding communication had changed materially. The ECB dropped its description of policy as being in a "good place," and Bundesbank President Joachim Nagel told Bloomberg on Friday that a hike as early as April is conceivable if energy-driven inflation risks materialize. The Bank of England voted unanimously to hold, but Governor Andrew Bailey explicitly noted that the prior expectation of scope to cut has changed, stating the BoE "stands ready to act." Sweden's Riksbank held similarly. The direction of travel across European central banks is now unmistakably toward tighter policy. Bloomberg Economics estimated March inflation across advanced economies is running approximately 0.4 percentage points above pre-conflict projections, and analysts drew explicit parallels to the post-Ukraine 2022 inflation episode. UK gilt volatility subsequently became a focal point for global fixed income investors, compounded by February borrowing data that came in above forecast due to surging debt-interest costs.

    Energy Exposure and Partial Cushions

    Europe entered the crisis with more resilience than during the 2022 energy shock, though that resilience has limits. Rabobank estimated electricity prices would be approximately one-third higher absent the growth in solar and wind capacity added since 2022, with France's nuclear fleet also operating more reliably than during that earlier episode. European natural gas futures had already risen more than 60% since hostilities began before Thursday's jump of as much as 35% following the Ras Laffan strike, pushing benchmark prices beyond double their pre-war levels. Dubai crude contracts for Gulf-loaded oil are up over 80% in March. Evening power price spikes in several countries point to ongoing vulnerability, and analysts warn that seasonal solar support will fade heading into autumn. Germany's domestic response — restricting petrol stations to one price hike per day and tightening antitrust oversight — is a political gesture rather than a market-moving intervention.

    Politics and Defense

    At the EU Brussels summit, leaders called for a moratorium on strikes against energy infrastructure, with Italian Prime Minister Meloni described as particularly animated about the severity of the situation. Trump explicitly warned that the US "will remember" how NATO allies responded to the crisis, adding that insufficient support would be "very bad for the future of NATO." Most European governments — including the UK, Germany, and France — declined to participate militarily, citing both legal constraints and skepticism about escalation. European officials are simultaneously navigating fatigue from US-related crises, and there are nascent signs governments are reconsidering their posture toward China as a more predictable trade partner. Germany's €600 billion defense spending program is generating tangible economic stimulus, with defense suppliers reporting record order books, but Chancellor Merz's broader political standing remains precarious — fewer than a quarter of Germans hold a positive view of him. In the UK, Prime Minister Starmer faces mounting pressure from Labour's left flank ahead of anticipated May local election losses, with Reform UK continuing to lead in national polls despite emerging fragmentation from newer far-right parties backed by Elon Musk.

    Corporate Developments

    UniCredit's $40 billion bid for Commerzbank, announced Monday, would allow the Italian lender to increase its stake beyond the 30% threshold and represents the most significant cross-border European banking consolidation attempt in years. Unilever's exploration of a food division sale — with McCormick among the early bidders at a valuation of approximately €28–31 billion — would represent the largest restructuring in the company's near-century history. HSBC's reported review of approximately 20,000 roles as part of an AI-driven restructuring of its middle and back offices is among the first concrete signals of how the technology may reshape large financial institution workforces at scale. Logitech fell 5.7% in Zurich on a UBS downgrade; HelloFresh dropped 9.6% in Frankfurt on guidance materially below expectations. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude rose above $118 per barrel on Thursday after Iran struck Qatar's Ras Laffan LNG complex — the world's largest — before pulling back to approximately $108–110 on Friday following signals of potential restraint from both Israel and Iran.
    • The Strait of Hormuz remained effectively closed to commercial shipping throughout the week, with approximately 40,000 seafarers stranded on either side and vessel insurance premiums reaching roughly 5% of hull value.
    • Gold fell approximately 7% on the week — its largest weekly decline since March 2020 and on pace for its worst monthly performance since 2008 — as global monetary policy repricing eroded the appeal of non-yielding assets.
    • The Reserve Bank of Australia hiked rates, becoming the first of eighteen central banks to meet this week to move, signaling at least some inflation-targeting frameworks are treating the oil shock as requiring active tightening.
    • Tungsten prices have more than doubled this year amid Chinese export controls and rising military demand, illustrating how the conflict is amplifying pre-existing commodity tensions.

    The Strait of Hormuz: Escalation and Tentative Signals

    The Iran war entered its most dangerous phase this week before showing tentative signs of restraint by Friday. Israel struck Iran's South Pars gas field — the first attack on Iranian energy production facilities — prompting Iran to inflict significant damage on Qatar's Ras Laffan Industrial City on Thursday, sparking fires and causing damage that Qatar indicated could keep two LNG trains offline for up to five years. Additional strikes hit a gas facility in Abu Dhabi, set ablaze two Kuwaiti oil refineries, and caused damage near a Saudi refinery. Tehran designated energy facilities in Qatar, Saudi Arabia, and the UAE as "legitimate targets." Brent surged above $118 on Thursday. By Friday, Prime Minister Netanyahu stated Israel would no longer target energy infrastructure and said the war would end faster than people expect; Iran's foreign minister signaled that recent restraint was deliberate. Whether this constitutes a durable de-escalation or tactical repositioning remains genuinely uncertain. Prediction markets put the probability of a ceasefire by May 31 below 50% as of early in the week.

    Energy Markets: A Fragmented Supply Landscape

    The price signals across different crude grades and delivery points illustrate the geographic severity of the disruption. WTI traded near $96 on Thursday, Brent at $115, and Middle Eastern grades above $150 — a fragmentation that reflects a shortage most acute for Asian importers dependent on Gulf barrels. Ras Laffan supplied roughly a fifth of global LNG before the war; a restart now expected to take months rather than weeks is pressuring gas prices globally, with downstream effects on fertilizer production, power generation, and semiconductor manufacturing costs. Saudi Arabia appears to have rerouted some exports to above half of normal volumes via contingency infrastructure, offering partial relief. Russia has benefited materially, accelerating crude loading to capture elevated prices while a US sanctions waiver window remains open, with revenues from its key export blend hitting a record for weekly income since the Ukraine invasion began. Trump waived the Jones Act for 60 days, allowing foreign-flagged vessels to transport commodities between US ports, and the administration has ruled out banning oil and gas exports.

    Central Banks and Macro Divergence

    The week's central bank calendar was historically dense, covering approximately two-thirds of the global economy across eighteen policy meetings. All major central banks held rates, but the signal embedded in forward guidance shifted meaningfully toward caution. The Reserve Bank of Australia moved first, hiking on Tuesday in a split decision — an indication that at least some policymakers are treating the energy shock as warranting active tightening rather than patient observation. The Fed held Wednesday, the ECB and Bank of Japan on Thursday, with all three communicating heightened vigilance. Goldman Sachs economists offered a partially reassuring view, arguing that broader supply-chain disruption comparable to 2021–22 is unlikely, as non-energy Gulf trade represents only 1% of global commerce. However, the energy shock alone is sufficient to complicate deliberations materially. Bloomberg Economics estimated March inflation across advanced economies is running approximately 0.4 percentage points above pre-conflict projections, and BofA's global fund manager survey found institutional investors executing the largest overweight rotation into commodities since the 2022 Ukraine invasion.

    Alliance Fractures and Geopolitical Reconfiguration

    Trump's appeals for allied military support produced little tangible commitment across the week. Australia ruled out deploying vessels, Japan's defense minister cited constitutional constraints, and Germany, France, and the UK all declined to participate militarily. Europe's largest naval powers stated that reopening the Strait during active hostilities would require a deeply complex multinational capability not currently feasible. China's state media were openly dismissive. The episode has exposed meaningful fissures in the Western alliance architecture. Simultaneously, the transactional character of US demands — overlapping with tariffs and large investment pledge requirements — appears to be accelerating the formation of alternative bilateral arrangements among US allies. The Japan-South Korea rapprochement is the clearest example: both finance ministers agreed to expand bilateral economic cooperation including a review of their $10 billion currency swap, with public favorability in each direction described as at or near record highs. The IPCC faces an institutional challenge following US withdrawal of scientific funding, and New York's proposed rollback of its 2030 climate targets illustrates the political difficulty of sustaining decarbonization commitments when energy affordability pressures are acute.

  • US

    • S&P 500 declined roughly 3% over the week, with the Magnificent Seven nearing correction territory, as the Iran conflict and stagflationary pressures overshadowed a broadly benign February CPI print.
    • February payrolls recorded their first private-sector decline in five years, yet swap markets repriced toward Fed tightening — a textbook stagflation tension that will define the central bank's posture heading into next week's FOMC.
    • Amazon raised $37 billion in the fourth-largest US corporate bond sale on record; private credit stress deepened, with Morgan Stanley and Cliffwater capping fund redemptions and JPMorgan restricting lending to certain private credit vehicles.
    • The USTR filed sweeping Section 301 investigations against more than a dozen economies — including China, the EU, Mexico, India, and Taiwan — marking the first formal step to reconstitute the tariff architecture following last month's Supreme Court ruling.
    • Goldman Sachs raised its 12-month US recession probability by 5 percentage points to 25%, citing the oil shock, softening labor data, and narrowing Fed policy space.

    Labor Market and the Stagflation Bind

    The week's most consequential domestic data point arrived before it began: Friday's February payrolls report showed the first private-sector decline in five years, alongside a rising unemployment rate. Under ordinary circumstances, that combination would reinforce expectations for Federal Reserve easing and satisfy the administration's persistent pressure on the central bank to cut rates. Instead, implied fed funds rates in overnight index swap markets moved higher across the week, as oil-driven inflation expectations overwhelmed the labor market signal. Oxford Economics maintained its baseline of Fed inaction until June, while Goldman Sachs revised its first cut expectation further to September. The ICE BofA MOVE Index climbed to its highest level since June as traders dialed back rate-cut expectations materially. The February CPI print — released Wednesday — showed core inflation at 2.5% year-on-year, the lowest since the 2021 breakout, with shelter costs rising just 0.1% month-on-month. The figure would ordinarily comfort policymakers, but it is being overshadowed by a divergence: core PCE appears on track to print near 3.1% for January, the widest gap between the two gauges in decades, with Barclays, Morgan Stanley, and Bank of America projecting core PCE goods prices advancing at least 0.8% in February alone. With Kevin Warsh still unable to secure a clear Senate confirmation path for the Fed chair role, the central bank's leadership question remains unresolved at precisely the moment its reaction function is most consequential.

    Fiscal and Energy Cost Interaction

    Bloomberg Economics quantified the household stakes with uncomfortable precision: the "One Big Beautiful Bill" is estimated to deliver approximately $800 in additional tax refunds this year, while crude settling near $80 per barrel would impose roughly $650 in incremental energy costs — effectively neutralizing most of the gain. At $83 per barrel the benefit is entirely erased; at $110 the net household position worsens by approximately $1,960. The distributional concern compounds the aggregate: energy costs are regressive, and the top 20% of earners already account for over 59% of personal consumption, raising the prospect of amplified K-shaped dynamics as the conflict prolongs. Pre-conflict consumer data offered a partial counterpoint — Bank of America card spending rose 3.3% year-on-year in February, the strongest in over three years — but that strength predates gasoline reaching its highest level in more than 18 months. The 30-year Treasury yield approached 4.90% mid-week as investors priced in war financing costs and potential household energy subsidies, even as the February budget deficit narrowed at a slower pace than the prior month.

    Corporate and Credit Developments

    Amazon's $37 billion bond issuance — with a further $11.6 billion sought in European markets — underscored that investment-grade issuers with genuine AI infrastructure narratives retain market access. Oracle shares surged approximately 10% in extended trading Thursday after reporting strong AI cloud demand, reinforcing hyperscaler capital expenditure momentum. The contrast with private credit was stark: Morgan Stanley and Cliffwater capped redemptions from multi-billion-dollar funds as withdrawal requests exceeded permitted limits, JPMorgan moved to restrict lending to certain private credit vehicles after marking down software company loan valuations, and the Bloomberg US Corporate Bond Index's year-to-date returns fell barely above zero with investment-grade yield-to-worst reaching 4.9%. The Markit CDX North American Investment Grade Index posted its largest spread widening since last May. Adobe fell approximately 8.5% in pre-market trading Friday following CEO Shantanu Narayen's departure after 18 years and a lackluster revenue outlook, reflecting deepening investor concern about the company's competitive positioning in the AI era. Hims & Hers Health surged 49% Monday after Novo Nordisk agreed to distribute its weight-loss drugs on the telehealth platform, while airline stocks — American, Delta, and United — fell more than 3% on fuel cost exposure early in the week. The USTR's Section 301 filings against more than a dozen economies, with a public hearing set for May 5th, introduce a further layer of uncertainty into corporate supply chain planning and complicate the planned Trump-Xi summit. ---

    Asia

    • China's first two months of 2026 trade surplus reached a record $214 billion on export growth of 21.9% year-on-year, even as domestic consumption may have recorded its weakest non-pandemic start to a year.
    • Asian currencies came under significant pressure by Friday: the yen weakened to approximately 159.5 against the dollar — its softest since 2024 — South Korea's won neared its lowest since 2009, and the Indian rupee and Philippine peso reached historic lows.
    • China's strategic petroleum reserves at a record 851 million barrels in onshore commercial inventories provided a meaningful buffer against the Hormuz shock, even as Chinese refiners began canceling agreed refined-fuel export cargoes to manage domestic supply.
    • India secured a US sanctions waiver allowing resumed Russian crude purchases, providing near-term relief to refiners pivoting away from Gulf supply chains.
    • The Trump-Xi summit framework — preparatory talks in Paris over the weekend involving Bessent, Greer, and He Lifeng — is taking shape for a March 31–April 2 meeting in Beijing, with tariffs, Taiwan, fentanyl, and semiconductors on the agenda.

    Equity and Currency Markets

    Asian markets experienced the sharpest regional equity losses of any zone early in the week. The MSCI Asia-Pacific index fell 3.7% on Monday, extending its weekly decline to nearly 10%, before partially recovering Tuesday as Brent retreated from its intraday high and President Trump signaled a possible near-term conflict resolution. The Nikkei 225 dropped 5.2% Monday before rebounding 2.88% Tuesday; South Korea's Kospi fell 6% at the worst of the selloff.
