By Friday, sentiment had soured again, with the Hang Seng declining 0.98% and regional currencies under acute pressure. The Thai baht fell as much as 1.8% intraday Friday. The oil shock interacts with Japan's structurally weak yen to amplify import cost burdens, raising the risk of stagflation in an economy where activity was already sluggish; more than a third of surveyed economists now expect the Bank of Japan to raise rates in April. South Korea's won, Indian rupee, and Philippine peso all reached multi-year or historic lows against the dollar as the week progressed.

    China: Export Strength, Domestic Fragility, and Strategic Positioning

    China's trade data released Tuesday provided a striking counterpoint to broader regional anxiety: exports surged 21.9% year-on-year in the first two months of 2026, well above the 7.2% median estimate, with a record $214 billion trade surplus for the period, even as exports to the US fell 11%, reflecting tariff effects. Yet the divergence between China's external performance and domestic conditions is widening. Workers in Guangdong province report falling wages and disappearing jobs, with automation and AI increasingly substituting for human labor; worker protest activity rose 44% last year, with Guangdong the primary hotspot. China's consumer spending may have recorded its worst start to any year outside the pandemic, according to economists surveying ahead of the official data release. The National People's Congress concluded its annual budget deliberations with the 15th Five-Year Plan (2026–2030) prioritizing advanced manufacturing, semiconductors, and AI while maintaining a 4% of GDP fiscal deficit — a continuity document that disappointed investors hoping for meaningful household consumption stimulus. Chinese authorities also moved to restrict state enterprises from deploying OpenClaw AI applications on office computers, causing Minimax to fall 6.5% in Hong Kong and Zhipu to drop 6.1% on cybersecurity concerns. Beijing's record oil stockpiles, renewable investment, and diplomatic positioning — Foreign Minister Wang Yi describing 2026 as a potentially defining year for US-China relations — reflect a calculated effort to emerge from the crisis with strategic advantages intact, though Bloomberg Economics cautions that a sustained oil price above $108 per barrel could meaningfully drag on Chinese export demand via secondary global demand effects.

    India, Southeast Asia, and Regional Stress

    India's position evolved notably across the week. New Delhi had been reducing Russian oil purchases under US pressure since December, pivoting toward Gulf suppliers — precisely the supply chains most disrupted by the Hormuz closure. Washington's announcement of a one-month sanctions waiver allowing resumed Russian crude purchases provided material relief, with Indian refiners immediately absorbing available Urals spot cargoes and tankers carrying over 20 million barrels within a week's sailing time of Indian ports. Nevertheless, the rupee remains under pressure with capital outflows accelerating, and Indian equities have underperformed EM peers significantly. India is simultaneously conducting direct negotiations with Iran to secure safe passage for more than twenty tankers — a transactional engagement with Tehran that illustrates New Delhi's multi-vector foreign policy calculus. Across Southeast Asia, emergency energy conservation measures proliferated: Thailand ordered civil servants to work from home full-time, the Philippines shifted to a four-day government work week, Vietnam faces potential airfare increases of up to 70% as jet fuel costs tripled, and Philippine President Marcos framed his country's position plainly as "victims of a war we didn't choose nor want." Indian carriers hiked long-haul ticket prices 15%, and Fitch Ratings identified India and Pakistan as particularly exposed to deteriorating credit metrics from sustained high oil prices. ---

    Europe

    • ECB rate expectations shifted materially across the week, with swaps moving from pricing one 25 basis point hike in 2026 to two by week's end, and the first fully priced by June; the Bank of England shifted from pricing cuts to a 70% probability of a hike by year-end.
    • UK GDP was flat in January against consensus expectations of 0.2% growth — weaker than every analyst forecast — a pre-conflict data point that suggests the economy was already losing momentum before the current energy shock.
    • German bund futures slid to an almost 15-year low as overlapping pressures — rising yields, elevated energy costs, weak industrial data, and war financing concerns — weighed on Europe's benchmark sovereign market.
    • CDU lost the Baden-Württemberg regional election to the Greens, a state it had been projected to reclaim after 15 years of Green governance, compounding Chancellor Merz's domestic political difficulties.
    • European credit markets registered significant stress, with the iTraxx Crossover index crossing 300 basis points for the first time since June and nearly 150 European companies already having ceded control to direct-lending funds.

    Monetary Policy Repricing

    The ECB entered the week facing a genuinely uncomfortable policy configuration: an energy-driven inflation shock arriving atop a growth backdrop that was already fragile. By Monday, interest rate markets implied two full 25 basis point hikes in 2026, compared with one priced the prior Friday, with the first fully priced by June — a remarkable shift in a matter of days. ECB Governing Council member Peter Kazimir stated explicitly that the war risks forcing the bank to raise rates sooner than anticipated; colleague François Villeroy de Galhau acknowledged next week's meeting was not the moment for action but signaled the ECB would not permit inflation to become entrenched. ECB President Lagarde separately pledged the bank would act to prevent the conflict from inflicting the same inflationary pain as Russia's 2022 invasion — language that closes off accommodation without committing to tightening, a delicate balance. By week's end the ECB was expected to hold rates unchanged through 2027 in some analyst frameworks, reflecting the growth headwind balancing the inflation risk. For the Bank of England, money markets shifted from pricing cuts to a 70% probability of a hike by year-end, with ING and RSM UK estimating UK inflation could rise to more than double the 2% target if current energy price levels persist. The BoE and ECB face a symmetric dilemma: easing into an energy shock risks credibility loss, while tightening risks amplifying a slowdown.

    Growth and Industrial Backdrop

    UK GDP data released Friday delivered a downside surprise, with January output flat against a 0.2% consensus expectation — weaker than every forecast in the analyst survey. The figures predate meaningful impact from the Middle East conflict, suggesting the UK economy was losing momentum independently. Germany's industrial data at the start of the week compounded the difficult backdrop, with both industrial production and factory orders declining at the start of the year, tempering recovery expectations. Germany's chemical industry faces a structural crisis that predates the conflict — higher post-Ukraine energy costs, rising carbon prices, new Chinese capacity, and weakening demand have already forced plant closures — but elevated energy prices intensify the pressure materially. European chemical producers face an estimated €3 billion annually in additional natural gas costs if the post-war price surge persists. The Leuna chemical park in eastern Germany has become a visible test case of this dynamic. The VCI chemical industry association described "a spiral spinning in the wrong direction," with raw material shortages compounding direct energy cost effects. German bund futures slid to an almost 15-year low across the week, and Scope Ratings identified Italian fiscal credibility as at risk from a prolonged conflict.

    Politics and Credit Markets

    Chancellor Merz suffered a notable political setback as CDU lost the Baden-Württemberg regional election to the ruling Greens — a state CDU had been projected to reclaim after 15 years — reflecting broader voter unease with the governing coalition's performance. The iTraxx Europe investment-grade index reached its highest level since May, while the Crossover index crossed 300 basis points for the first time since June. Goldman Sachs analysis showed nearly 150 European companies have already ceded control to direct-lending funds, a measure of how private credit stress is materializing. Primary bond issuance paused as borrowers stood down from planned debt sales during the most acute volatility. The euro fell to its lowest level since August against the dollar by Friday, reflecting the continent's acute energy import exposure. EU Commission President von der Leyen described nuclear energy abandonment as "a strategic mistake," a pointed reference to Germany's reactor closures as energy security anxiety intensifies. The EU separately warned that inflation could exceed 3% this year if oil remains near $100 and gas stays elevated. In France, Marine Le Pen's effort to broaden National Rally's appeal to corporate and establishment elites is generating internal friction with the party's working-class base — a tension bearing monitoring given her proximity to the presidency in current polling. UK consumer sentiment fell to a four-month low in March per Barclays data, while Rheinmetall declined 4.5% after guiding below analyst sales expectations for 2026. The Swiss franc continued appreciating as a safe-haven destination, placing the SNB in a difficult position where analysts suggest negative interest rates may be revisited. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude surged intraday to $119.50 on Monday — the largest intraday percentage move since April 2020 — before partially retracing; oil held above $100 per barrel for much of the week after further Iranian escalation on Thursday.
    • The IEA announced an emergency release of 400 million barrels from member-country stockpiles — the largest in the agency's history — providing temporary relief but covering an estimated 25 days of disrupted supply at the high end of disruption estimates.
    • The traditional 60/40 portfolio hedge broke down, with government bond yields rising more than 25 basis points across the US, UK, Germany, Canada, and Australia since hostilities began as bonds and equities moved in tandem on stagflation concerns.
    • Russia is providing Iran with satellite imagery and drone-targeting intelligence according to multiple sources, while simultaneously benefiting from the near-elimination of the Urals discount to Brent — a fiscal windfall comparable to 2022 energy revenues.
    • The global aviation network absorbed one of its largest shocks since the pandemic, with over 46,000 flights canceled and economy-class Sydney-to-London round-trip fares rising more than 80%.

    The Oil Shock: Scale and Mechanism

    The Strait of Hormuz remains effectively at a near-standstill, with the IEA estimating the closure has cut the corridor's usual 20 million barrels per day of throughput by approximately 90%, forcing Gulf producers to shut in around 10 million barrels per day of output. Saudi Arabia, Iraq, the UAE, and Kuwait have each curtailed production as closed storage fills; QatarEnergy delayed a major LNG expansion to at least 2027 following drone attacks on the Ras Laffan facility, which produces roughly 20% of global LNG supply, driving European and Asian gas futures to their highest levels since early 2023. Citigroup estimates the effective supply removal at approximately 16 million barrels per day of crude and refined products — roughly 16% of world supply — a disruption that materially exceeds the 2022 Russian shock. The IEA's record 400-million-barrel coordinated release, along with Japan's separate unilateral release of approximately 80 million barrels beginning as early as March 16, provided temporary relief but the pace and logistics of deployment remain uncertain. Six-month Brent futures were pricing approximately $80 per barrel at week's end, embedding a meaningful probability of resolution that the military and diplomatic evidence does not yet fully support. Beyond crude, European natural gas futures jumped as much as 30% on Monday alone, aluminum briefly hit a near four-year high, and coal prices reached their highest level in over a year as gas substitution effects propagated through power markets.

    Geopolitical Architecture

    Iran's new supreme leader Mojtaba Khamenei — appointed Monday by the Assembly of Experts, with deep IRGC ties — explicitly vowed to keep Hormuz effectively closed and threatened additional fronts if US-Israeli strikes continue. Iran's ceasefire conditions — a permanent pledge from both the US and Israel never to strike again — appear incompatible with either government's stated positions, leaving the diplomatic path narrow. Prediction markets moved across the week from a 48% to a 52% probability that the conflict extends beyond April 30. Russia's posture adds strategic complexity: Putin has maintained a communication channel with Trump while reportedly providing Iran with satellite imagery and drone-targeting intelligence, and Moscow is considering ending most gas sales to the EU. The Urals discount to Brent has nearly disappeared, delivering a fiscal windfall that reduces pressure on Russia's war budget while US political attention is diverted from Ukraine. UK naval forces reported three vessels struck in the Strait of Hormuz and the Persian Gulf on Wednesday; Dubai International Airport briefly halted operations following drone strikes that injured four people; Iraq halted terminal operations and Oman evacuated ships from one of the last viable regional export ports on Thursday. BCA Research raises the uncomfortable possibility that even a ceasefire may not reopen Hormuz, as factions beyond Tehran's control could sustain drone harassment of shipping indefinitely.

    Secondary Effects: Food, Supply Chains, and the GCC

    The war's secondary consequences are accumulating across multiple systems. Iran accounts for approximately 5% and 11% of global urea and ammonia trade respectively; urea prices rose 25% since hostilities began, reaching levels last seen during Russia's Ukraine invasion, with crop prices rising in parallel and a global food inflation impulse expected to materialize with a lag. The conflict is disrupting approximately one-fifth of global air freight and compressing Asia-Europe container traffic. GCC economies face what may prove a structural setback to their diversification ambitions: Iranian strikes have targeted AWS facilities in the UAE and Bahrain, exposing physical infrastructure assumptions that underpinned Gulf-US technology partnerships, and the energy revenue curtailment from Hormuz interdiction reduces the available pool for outward investment commitments made to the Trump administration. Environmental consequences are also emerging: Israeli drone strikes on refineries near Tehran on March 8 triggered fires releasing toxic particulates that mixed with rainfall to produce contaminated black rain over a metropolitan area of approximately 18.5 million people, with analysts identifying over 300 incidents carrying environmental risk from the conflict to date. The IMF has urged policymakers to prepare for a prolonged conflict scenario. Societe Generale and Deutsche Bank identify the shock's persistence and central bank reaction functions — particularly the Fed's — as the two decisive variables for the global macro trajectory, a question unlikely to be resolved before next week's FOMC meeting.

  • US

    • S&P 500 futures fell approximately 1% on Monday before stabilizing through the week, with the index declining less than 1% overall — notably resilient relative to Asia and Europe.
    • The 10-year Treasury yield rose from roughly 3.97% on Monday to 4.163% by Friday, defying conventional haven dynamics as oil-driven inflation expectations suppressed rate-cut pricing.
    • ISM manufacturing prices-paid surged to their highest since 2022 in February, compounding the inflationary signal from an oil shock that pushed WTI above $84 and gasoline to $3.32 per gallon by Friday.
    • Private credit stress intensified, with Blackstone's flagship fund facing a record $3.8 billion redemption request and Fitch reporting a trailing 12-month default rate of 5.8% — the highest since tracking began.
    • The Supreme Court's invalidation of IEEPA-based tariffs reduced projected federal revenue by an estimated $1.7 trillion through 2036, complicating the fiscal outlook.

    Equity Markets

    US equity markets exhibited an unusual combination of resilience and internal stress throughout the week. The S&P 500 opened under pressure Monday, with futures off roughly 1% as the US-Israeli strikes on Iran unsettled investor sentiment, but the index held broadly flat through mid-week before edging lower Friday as WTI crude reached $84.46. The relative calm masked significant sectoral divergence: defense names surged on Monday — Lockheed Martin rose 7.1%, RTX gained 6.5%, and Northrop Grumman advanced 5.2% — while airlines sold off sharply, with United Airlines falling 5.7% and Delta declining 5.1% on higher fuel costs. Oil majors advanced, Exxon Mobil gaining roughly 5% and ConocoPhillips rising 6%. The US market's outperformance relative to Asia and Europe reflects a structural advantage: as a net energy exporter, the US is substantially less exposed to a sustained Strait of Hormuz disruption than economies that depend overwhelmingly on Gulf crude. That insulation is partial, however, and the week's events have placed a meaningful ceiling on any near-term equity rerating.

    Monetary Policy and Rates

    The bond market's behavior was the week's most analytically significant development. Rather than rallying on safe-haven demand, the 10-year Treasury yield rose from approximately 3.97% Monday to 4.163% by Friday — a dynamic that Société Générale's historical analysis of five oil supply shocks over the past fifty years suggests is consistent: Treasuries have underperformed at one-week, three-month, and six-month horizons following comparable episodes. The February ISM manufacturing survey added to the discomfort, with prices-paid surging to its highest level since 2022 and respondents explicitly citing domestic steel and aluminum tariffs as simultaneously raising input costs and compressing demand. Market-implied year-end fed funds rates reached their highest level in nine months, and former Treasury Secretary Janet Yellen characterized the situation as leaving the Fed "even more on hold." Gasoline prices rose 25 cents to $3.25 per gallon in a single week, reaching $3.32 by Friday — the highest under Trump's presidency — reversing what had been one of his administration's more tangible consumer affordability wins.

    Fiscal and Political Developments

    The fiscal backdrop deteriorated on two fronts. The Supreme Court's invalidation of IEEPA-based tariffs, which the Committee for a Responsible Federal Budget estimates reduces government revenue by $1.7 trillion through 2036, leaves the administration's replacement 10% levy recovering approximately $925 billion — a material gap on any trajectory. Federal debt accumulation continues regardless of the scenario modeled. Politically, the administration faces the uncomfortable reversal of three stated market preferences — lower oil, lower yields, and a weaker dollar — all of which moved in the wrong direction this week. The dollar reclaimed safe-haven status, yields edged higher, and pump prices hit a presidency high, complicating the domestic political calculus as midterm elections approach in November. Trump dismissed Homeland Security Secretary Kristi Noem on Friday, naming Oklahoma Senator Markwayne Mullin as her replacement, raising questions about institutional continuity.

    Corporate News

    In individual corporate developments, Morgan Stanley announced it is cutting approximately 3% of its global workforce, roughly 2,500 employees, citing individual performance. MongoDB shares fell 27% on a weaker-than-expected revenue forecast, dragging enterprise software broadly. Berkshire Hathaway's Class B shares slipped 1.7% after reporting a near-30% decline in operating profit in Warren Buffett's final quarter as CEO. Moderna gained 5.8% after settling Covid delivery-technology litigation for $950 million. Marvell Technology surged 12% in premarket trading Friday on accelerating data center demand. Executives at the Bloomberg Invest conference, including Apollo's Marc Rowan and Soros's Dawn Fitzpatrick, warned of "a painful 18 to 24 months" ahead for private credit and private equity as default risks rise and liquidity remains constrained. ---

    Asia

    • South Korea's Kospi fell 7.2% on Tuesday and a further 12% on Wednesday — the largest single-session decline in the index's history — before surging 10% on Thursday following activation of a 100 trillion won stabilization program.
    • China's National People's Congress set a GDP growth target of 4.5%–5%, the lowest since 1991, and unveiled a 15th Five-Year Plan emphasizing AI, technological self-reliance, and a pivot toward household consumption.
    • Global funds sold a net $11 billion of emerging Asian equities excluding China in the week — the largest outflow since March 2022 — with Taiwan absorbing $7.9 billion in withdrawals.
    • A US submarine sank the Iranian frigate IRIS Dena off Sri Lanka on Thursday, the first American submarine attack on a surface vessel since World War II, placing India in a diplomatically untenable position.
    • Mainland Chinese investors sold a record HK$27.7 billion of Hong Kong-listed shares in a single session, the largest net selling on record.

    South Korea: Historic Volatility

    The week's most extreme market moves occurred in South Korea. The Kospi — which had risen roughly 50% year-to-date, fueled by AI-driven optimism around Samsung Electronics and SK Hynix, retail margin participation, and active political encouragement from President Lee Jae Myung — suffered a two-day collapse of approximately 18%, with the 12% Wednesday decline representing the largest single-session fall in the index's history. Samsung fell 12% Wednesday, SK Hynix declined roughly 10%, and Hyundai Motor lost 16%. Bank of Korea Governor Rhee Chang Yong abandoned scheduled travel to Bangkok to convene an emergency meeting. President Lee, attending events in the Philippines, called an extraordinary cabinet session. The political consequences are significant: his public identification with the equity rally has made the rout a direct test of his credibility. The Thursday rebound of 10% following invocation of the stabilization fund illustrates the market's extreme sensitivity to policy signals, but the underlying volatility profile remains elevated.

    China: NPC and Conflict Exposure

    China's National People's Congress delivered the week's most consequential Asian policy signals. Premier Li Qiang's government work report set a growth target at its lowest level since 1991 while framing the reduction as a deliberate quality-over-quantity pivot. Officials made their strongest commitment yet to eliminating deflationary conditions in 2026, and the accompanying 15th Five-Year Plan doubled down on technology — emphasizing AI, brain-computer interfaces, and pharmaceutical innovation — as the primary growth driver. Defense spending rose 7%, the slowest pace since 2022. Beijing simultaneously instructed its largest refiners to suspend diesel and gasoline exports, reflecting precautionary energy conservation as Gulf crude flows remained disrupted. Diplomatically, China called for safe Strait of Hormuz passage — hardly surprising given that nearly half of China's crude imports transited the waterway as recently as December — while simultaneously preparing for preparatory trade talks with US counterparts in Paris next week. That the bilateral economic agenda continues despite the Iran crisis appears to signal that both sides treat it as structurally distinct from the geopolitical environment.

    India and the Maritime Dimension

    India faced an acute dilemma following the sinking of the Iranian frigate IRIS Dena off Sri Lanka Thursday — a vessel that had participated in an Indian Navy-hosted multilateral fleet review. The Indian rupee fell to a record low against the dollar, and Indian equity benchmarks erased all dollar-term gains since last April's Liberation Day market turmoil. The US Treasury's temporary waiver allowing Indian refiners to resume Russian crude purchases provided some economic relief while simultaneously highlighting India's strategic exposure and benefiting Moscow's war finances. Prime Minister Modi's call for "dialog and diplomacy" navigates carefully between Washington and Tehran but satisfies neither fully.

    Japan and Regional Periphery

    The Bank of Japan maintained its cautious posture, with a deputy governor declining Monday to signal any imminent rate increase, and a former official placing April move odds at approximately 50/50. Toyota's landmark settlement with Elliott Investment Management over Toyota Industries — at ¥20,600 per share, a 9.6% premium on the prior bid, valuing the deal at approximately ¥6.7 trillion — was the week's most significant Japanese corporate development, largely overshadowed by conflict-driven market moves. Australia's Reserve Bank Governor Michele Bullock signaled that patient inflation management has limits and a rate hike remains possible, underscoring that the energy shock compounds pre-existing inflation challenges across the region. ---

    Europe

    • European natural gas futures surged more than 50% on the week — the largest weekly move since the 2022 energy crisis — following Iranian drone strikes on Qatar's Ras Laffan LNG facility and the effective closure of the Strait of Hormuz.
    • The Stoxx Europe 600 fell 1.45% Monday and a further 3.16% Tuesday before partially recovering Wednesday, with banks and insurers — including Société Générale, UniCredit, Santander, and BNP Paribas — each declining more than 4% at their lows.
    • The ECB faces a sharpening stagflationary dilemma, with traders fully pricing a 2026 rate hike by Thursday and Bundesbank President Nagel stating the inflation impact of the Iran war currently outweighs the growth drag.
    • President Trump threatened to cut off trade with Spain after Prime Minister Sánchez denied Washington access to Spanish military bases, while UK Prime Minister Starmer publicly distanced Britain from US "regime change from the skies."
    • The UK's Office for Budget Responsibility indicated that Labour's commitment to raise defence spending to 3.5% of GDP by 2035 would require approximately £40 billion in additional outlays.

    Energy Shock and Growth Risks

    Europe entered the week as the region most structurally exposed to the Iran conflict outside of Asia. European natural gas prices surged more than 52% intraday on Monday — the largest single-day gain since the 2022 energy crisis — and extended gains through the week, breaching €50 per megawatt-hour by Thursday as Qatar's Ras Laffan facility remained offline. Approximately 30% of EU diesel and kerosene imports originate from the Middle East, with roughly two-thirds of those volumes transiting the Strait of Hormuz. Goldman Sachs estimates a month-long Hormuz halt could cause European gas prices to more than double. Bloomberg Economics has framed the scenario analysis in binary terms: a conflict lasting roughly a month may amount to a manageable headwind for a continent showing tentative recovery; a prolonged disruption would constitute a materially more serious challenge, with recession risk rising meaningfully. Euro-area fourth-quarter GDP was simultaneously revised lower, with trade weighing on output — a stagflationary combination that complicates any simple monetary policy response. The euro declined roughly 2% against the dollar across the week, consistent with the terms-of-trade deterioration that characterized the 2022 energy crisis.

    ECB Policy Dilemma

    The ECB's policy trajectory appears to be shifting in real time. As recently as last month, markets had been pricing a continuation of the easing cycle that began in 2024. By Thursday, traders were fully pricing a 2026 rate hike, and Bundesbank President Joachim Nagel was explicit that the inflation impact of the Iran war currently outweighs the growth drag — a statement that, if representative of the broader Governing Council, would mark a meaningful pivot. The dilemma is genuine: a supply-side energy shock simultaneously pushes inflation higher and suppresses growth, leaving the ECB with no clean policy response. One partial offset emerged from Germany, where unusually strong solar generation in March — with installations feeding over 40 gigawatts into the grid for five consecutive days — helped cap domestic electricity prices, illustrating the buffering capacity of domestic renewable generation during external supply shocks.

    UK Politics and Fiscal Position

    UK Chancellor Rachel Reeves delivered her spring fiscal statement against an unusually turbulent backdrop on Tuesday. Tax receipts came in approximately £22 billion ahead of OBR forecasts, driven by stronger-than-expected self-assessed income tax and VAT revenues, providing modest relief on gilt issuance pressure — though UK yields remain the highest in the G7. The statement was deliberately low-key and largely overshadowed by conflict developments. Prime Minister Starmer sharpened his public distancing from US military action, stating Britain does not support "regime change from the skies" and questioning the legal basis for the strikes. Trump publicly criticized Starmer for refusing US base access. A suspected drone attack on the UK's Cyprus base occurred hours after Starmer's announcement, illustrating the tangible costs of British association with the campaign even at arm's length. Over 100 Labour MPs separately wrote to the Home Secretary urging a rethink of immigration reforms, signaling parliamentary management strain on multiple fronts. The FTSE 100 outperformed materially throughout the week, with Citigroup upgrading UK equities to overweight, citing the index's structural tilt toward energy, commodities, defence, and defensives as an effective geopolitical hedge.

    Corporate and Country-Level Developments

    Spain emerged as a diplomatic flashpoint after Trump threatened to "cut off all trade" following Sánchez's refusal to grant military base access — a threat complicated by the fact that bilateral trade is governed by EU-level agreements. Spanish equities fell to their lowest in nearly three months, and Banco Santander executive chair Ana Botín sought publicly to downplay the rift. Adidas fell 7.6% in Frankfurt Wednesday after its profit forecast missed expectations due to US tariffs and currency headwinds. Novo Nordisk rose 3.5% after US regulators moved against copycat GLP-1 drugs. Italy's fiscal credibility received a modest setback after data confirmed it failed to meet the EU's 3% deficit ceiling last year. German Chancellor Friedrich Merz traveled to Washington on Tuesday for meetings covering tariff policy and the broader geopolitical situation. ---

    Global

    • Brent crude surged approximately 18% on the week, trading above $82 per barrel by mid-week, with US diesel futures on track for a weekly gain exceeding 40% and European natural gas up more than 50%.
    • The Strait of Hormuz effectively ceased functioning as an oil transit corridor by Thursday, with only two commercial vessel transits recorded in a 24-hour period and no oil shipments passing through at all.
    • Iran's geographic footprint of retaliation expanded to at least five countries, a US submarine sank the IRIS Dena off Sri Lanka, and NATO air defenses downed a ballistic missile over Turkey — the conflict acquiring genuinely global military characteristics.
    • The "Sell America, Buy Asia" macro trade that defined early 2026 sharply reversed, with the dollar recording its largest weekly gain in over a year and emerging market equities giving back recent outperformance at speed.
    • A new study published in Geophysical Research Letters provided the first statistically significant evidence of an acceleration in global warming, with the planet warming at approximately 0.35°C per decade since 2015 — nearly double the average rate between 1970 and 2015.

    The Conflict and Energy Markets

    The killing of Ayatollah Ali Khamenei in a US-Israeli strike represents the most consequential geopolitical rupture since Russia's invasion of Ukraine. Brent crude surged approximately 12.5% on Monday before partially retracing, then extended gains through the week as the Strait of Hormuz — through which approximately 20% of global oil exports and a comparable share of LNG transits — effectively closed to commercial shipping. The binding near-term constraint has proven to be the insurance market rather than an explicit military blockade: more than half the world's largest maritime insurance clubs announced withdrawal of war-risk coverage for Persian Gulf vessels starting Thursday, causing commercial shipping to self-suspend. Saudi Arabia's 550,000 barrel-per-day Ras Tanura refinery suspended operations following a drone strike, QatarEnergy shut LNG production at the world's largest export facility, and Iraq began cutting output as onshore storage filled. Aluminium Bahrain declared force majeure on some deliveries, with aluminum prices reaching their highest since 2022. Airlines canceled over 12,300 flights, stranding crews and equipment, with Gulf hub carriers — Emirates, Etihad, and Qatar Airways — collectively accounting for an estimated 13% of global air freight capacity. The range of oil price scenarios remains wide and genuinely uncertain. A brief conflict and rapid regime transition might return Brent toward $60 per barrel; a full Hormuz disruption sustained for weeks could push prices toward $108 or beyond in Bloomberg Economics' severe scenario; a middle case of constrained but continuing flows might see prices stabilize in the $80–$90 range. Goldman Sachs flagged the plausibility of Brent crossing $100 if low flows persist for another five weeks. President Trump announced US naval escorts and insurance guarantees for tankers transiting the strait — a partial solution at best, as ING analysts noted US Navy escorts risk becoming targets and it is ultimately individual ship captains who determine whether to sail.

    Monetary Policy in a Supply Shock

    The macro implications are particularly uncomfortable for central banks globally. A sustained $20-per-barrel oil shock could add up to one percentage point to global inflation, according to Société Générale, at a moment when most major economies have not yet returned inflation durably to target. The IMF's Kristalina Georgieva warned that a sustained 10% energy price increase would push global inflation up 40 basis points and shave 0.1–0.2% from growth. The traditional policy response to a supply-side shock is ambiguous: tightening to contain inflation risks compressing already-fragile growth, while easing to support activity risks embedding higher inflation expectations. Traders unwound rate-cut expectations across the US, UK, Turkey, and Hungary simultaneously — producing the counterintuitive result that sovereign bonds sold off alongside equities, leaving the dollar as the dominant haven vehicle. Gold, after initial gains to $5,385 per ounce Monday, wavered and was trading at a steep discount in Dubai by week's end, an unusual dislocation reflecting local market stress.

    Geopolitical Alignments and the Conflict's Endgame

    The conflict's endgame remains opaque. Trump's stated objectives shifted across the week — variously described as a campaign lasting four to five weeks, seeking regime change on a "Venezuela model," or opening negotiations with new Iranian leadership. Iran's own signals were contradictory: President Pezeshkian vowed retaliation while security chief Larijani publicly rejected US talks, even as the Wall Street Journal reported renewed nuclear negotiation overtures through Omani intermediaries, and a New York Times report of indirect Iranian-Washington contact prompted a temporary equity rebound Wednesday. The Assembly of Experts must choose Khamenei's successor — the choice between a continuity hardliner and a more pragmatic figure carrying profound implications for conflict duration. Russia has emerged as the clearest early economic beneficiary, with elevated energy revenues and India's return to Russian crude purchasing replenishing the Kremlin's finances at a critical juncture. The minerals competition between the US and China in sub-Saharan Africa — where China conducts roughly $200 billion in annual trade and controls an estimated 80% of Congo's copper output, approximately four times the US trade figure — adds a further dimension to the administration's resource-security logic. Markets are trading probabilities rather than resolved outcomes, a posture that appears likely to persist.

  • US

    • The Supreme Court's invalidation of Trump's IEEPA tariffs and the administration's rapid pivot to a replacement 15% global levy under Section 122 defined the week's policy landscape, with legal durability remaining in question throughout.
    • Nvidia's earnings beat failed to sustain a rally, AI disruption fears weighed on software names, and the S&P 500 closed February with its largest monthly decline since April — strikingly underperforming European and emerging-market peers.
    • A major winter storm paralyzed the Northeast early in the week, and US-Israel strikes on Iranian nuclear facilities Saturday introduced a new geopolitical and energy price risk into the domestic outlook.
    • The Treasury market delivered its best monthly performance in roughly a year, with long-dated bonds gaining 4%, confirming their haven role even amid fiscal sustainability debates.
    • Kevin Warsh's anticipated succession to the Fed chair drew increasing attention, with analysts mapping a potential shift in the dot-plot framework and a markedly more dovish tilt than his earlier public record suggested.

    Tariff Regime in Transition

    The week opened with the legal and practical fallout from the Supreme Court's invalidation of Trump's IEEPA tariff architecture. By Tuesday, a 10% global tariff under Section 122 of the Trade Act of 1974 had entered into force, subsequently raised to 15% by executive action. The legal footing is widely questioned: Section 122 applies specifically to "fundamental international payments problems," a threshold former IMF chief economist Gita Gopinath stated plainly the US does not meet, a view echoed by former Treasury official Jay Shambaugh, who noted that a genuine balance-of-payments crisis would be reflected in dollar weakness and capital flight — neither of which is occurring. The administration signaled it intends to slow-walk refunds on overturned levies, framing repayments as "corporate welfare," even as more than 1,500 refund lawsuits have been filed representing an estimated $170 billion in contested duties. Legal challenges appear likely, though courts may move too slowly to constrain tariffs that expire in 150 days under the statute.

    Equity Markets and the AI Disruption Narrative

    The week's dominant equity theme was a rotation away from US software and services names perceived as vulnerable to AI displacement. IBM suffered its steepest single-day decline in 25 years after Anthropic's Claude Code tool was shown to modernize COBOL, threatening IBM's mainframe franchise. C3.ai tumbled 24%, Trade Desk fell 17% in pre-market, Salesforce slid roughly 4%, Workday dropped 9.2%, and Duolingo fell 24% on below-consensus guidance. Against this, hardware and infrastructure names held up: Dell gained 9.8% on AI server demand, and Block surged as much as 27% after announcing it would cut nearly half its workforce as an AI productivity bet. Nvidia's earnings — with revenue guidance of approximately $78 billion beating consensus — produced a muted after-hours response, encapsulating the increasingly demanding valuation bar AI-adjacent equities now face. The S&P 500 options market reflected the broader caution, with the put-call skew rising to a two-year high and normalized two-month skew approaching the upper end of its five-year range.

    Monetary Policy and the Fed Transition

    The National Association for Business Economics conference devoted its agenda almost entirely to AI's labor market implications, with Fed Governor Lisa Cook suggesting the economy may be approaching the most significant reorganization of work in generations and warning that conventional rate tools cannot offset AI-driven structural unemployment. Richmond Fed President Tom Barkin echoed the caution. The more politically consequential monetary story, however, surrounds Kevin Warsh: Bloomberg Economics analysis confirmed a marked shift from his hawkish 2009–2011 positioning toward dovish messaging since 2025, and Deutsche Bank analysts sketched potential reforms under his expected tenure including revisions to the dot plot and a possible shift toward rolling 12- and 24-month rate horizons.

    The Iran Shock and Domestic Political Risk

    Saturday's joint US-Israel strikes on Iranian nuclear facilities arrived as the week's most consequential late development. For Trump, the political arithmetic is uncomfortable: having campaigned on an America-first, anti-interventionist platform, he has now presided over strikes in Venezuela, Iran, Nigeria, and now a direct assault on Iran's nuclear infrastructure. Polling already suggests Republicans face a difficult path to retaining both congressional chambers. The immediate domestic economic concern is energy: oil has risen nearly 20% year-to-date, and any further spike feeds directly into gasoline prices at a moment of elevated household financial stress. Trump acknowledged in an overnight video address that US casualties are possible, an admission that will intensify scrutiny if losses materialize. On the corporate side, Berkshire Hathaway's PacifiCorp faces approximately $55 billion in wildfire-related claims, and Greg Abel's first annual letter as Buffett's successor inherits a materially complex liability picture as climate-driven risk intensifies across the American West. ---

    Asia

    • Asian hardware and semiconductor names dominated inflows, with Taiwan Semiconductor up 30% year-to-date, Samsung up 70%, and SK Hynix up 57% as investors rotated away from US software toward AI infrastructure beneficiaries.
    • The People's Bank of China moved to temper yuan appreciation by removing the 20% reserve requirement on foreign-currency forward contracts, effective March 2, signaling concern that excessive currency strength could aggravate deflationary pressures.
    • Saturday's US-Israel strikes on Iran introduced immediate energy import risk for Asia's largest crude importers — China, Japan, South Korea, and India — all of which depend heavily on Middle Eastern supply transiting the Strait of Hormuz.
    • Japan's monetary policy outlook grew more contested, with Prime Minister Takaichi nominating reflationist academics to BOJ board vacancies and reports of her skepticism toward further rate hikes, sending the yen lower and pressuring JGBs.
    • South Korea's equity market capitalization surpassed France to rank ninth globally, while its National Pension Service posted an 18.82% return in 2025 — its strongest ever — though foreign funds sold a record net $4.7 billion of Kospi stocks on Friday after the benchmark's near-50% year-to-date run.

    China: Trade, Currency, and Geopolitical Complexity

    China emerged from the Supreme Court's IEEPA ruling as one of the clearer near-term beneficiaries, with Morgan Stanley estimating average US levies on Chinese goods fell to 24% from 32%. Hong Kong's Hang Seng China Enterprises index rallied 2.65% on Monday. The improved tariff arithmetic arrives ahead of President Xi Jinping's scheduled hosting of Trump in Beijing on March 31, materially altering the negotiating dynamic: Xi enters the summit with export volumes at full capacity, reduced average levies, and a US counterpart operating under legally constrained trade authority. Whether Beijing capitalizes on this position or maintains a measured posture to avoid fresh friction remains to be seen. China simultaneously warned against further tariff increases and issued new blacklistings of 20 Japanese entities including Mitsubishi Heavy affiliates — the first addition of Japanese firms to the list since its inception. A record $112 billion discrepancy between Chinese reported export data and US Customs arrivals for 2025 suggests that as much as a quarter of China-origin shipments may have bypassed US tariff controls, complicating revenue collection and any analytical assessment of the bilateral trade relationship. The PBoC's move to temper yuan appreciation by easing forward contract reserve requirements reflected concerns that China's record $1.2 trillion trade surplus continues to generate currency appreciation pressure at odds with an economy still grappling with deflationary dynamics. Lunar New Year data offered a mixed signal: 596 million tourist trips — 95 million more than a year prior — and record total tourism spending, but per-trip expenditure of 1,348 yuan fell marginally below the prior year, consistent with a consumer that is mobile but cautious.

    Japan: Policy Tensions Mount

    Japan's monetary policy outlook became more contested across the week. Reports that Prime Minister Takaichi had expressed apprehension over further BOJ rate increases in a meeting with Governor Ueda — taking a tougher stance than at their November meeting — sent the yen down approximately 1% to around 156 per dollar on Tuesday, reviving concerns that political resistance could constrain normalization. Later in the week, Takaichi nominated two reflationist academics to forthcoming BOJ board vacancies, reinforcing those concerns and sending the yen and JGBs lower. Notably, former BOJ Governor Haruhiko Kuroda — himself the architect of Japan's long-running easing experiment — called publicly for rate hikes and warned the yen may be "somewhat too weak," a striking inversion of his own legacy. On corporate governance, Toyota is preparing to unwind approximately ¥3 trillion ($19 billion) in cross-shareholdings with banks and financial institutions, a meaningful step in Japan's multi-year capital efficiency effort.

    South Korea and the Broader Regional Rotation

    South Korea's equity market continued to attract attention as a structural beneficiary of corporate governance reform and semiconductor cycle strength. The Kospi advanced 1.91% to 6,084 on Wednesday, with total market capitalization rising to $3.76 trillion, surpassing France to rank ninth globally. Overseas investors purchased a net $2.77 billion of Taiwan equities on Tuesday — the largest single-day foreign inflow since December 2005. The Bank of Korea held its benchmark rate at 2.5%, upgrading both growth and inflation forecasts and publishing new dot-plot projections. South Korea's fertility rate rose for a second consecutive year — a rare positive demographic signal — though foreign funds sold a record net $4.7 billion of Kospi stocks on Friday after the benchmark's near-50% year-to-date advance, suggesting some profit-taking in one of the world's best-performing markets. Saturday's Iran strikes introduced a new downside risk variable: as a major crude importer with limited domestic energy production, South Korea is acutely exposed to any sustained disruption in Strait of Hormuz flows. ---

    Europe

    • European equities outperformed their US counterparts for much of the week, with the Stoxx Europe 600 on course to close February with its eighth consecutive monthly gain — the longest winning streak since 2013 — driven by a rotation away from AI-disruption-exposed US names.
    • HSBC reported full-year pretax profit of $29.9 billion, beating expectations, with shares gaining 5.4% in London; by contrast, Diageo fell 5.8% after cutting its full-year organic net sales guidance for the second time this fiscal year.
    • The Green Party won the Gorton and Denton by-election in Manchester with 40.7% of the vote, with Reform UK second and Labour third — a result that illustrates the two-front political pressure on Keir Starmer's government.
    • Saturday's Iran strikes and the resulting Gulf airspace closures — including Emirates suspending all Dubai operations — introduced a new energy price and inflation risk for the eurozone, complicating the ECB's rate path.
    • The collapse of London-based Market Financial Solutions created acute exposure for Barclays (approximately £600 million), Atlas (£400 million), and Jefferies (£100 million), adding to broader stress narratives across non-bank lending.

    European Equity Outperformance

    The defining market theme of the week was European equities' relative strength against a US backdrop weighed by AI disruption fears. The Stoxx Europe 600 closed February up 3.7%, extending an eight-month winning streak not seen since 2013, with European companies announcing €85.7 billion in share buybacks year-to-date — the highest ever for a January-February period, according to Barclays data. Goldman Sachs promoted what it labeled a HALO basket — Heavy Assets, Low Obsolescence, spanning utilities, miners, industrials, and luxury goods — arguing these capital-intensive sectors are structurally insulated from AI displacement. The basket has outperformed capital-light peers by 35% since early 2025. This rotation reflects both the relative absence of high-multiple software names in European indices and a cyclical recovery that may be underappreciated by consensus. Germany provided some of the firmest datapoints: fourth-quarter GDP showed recovery taking hold, factory orders jumped in December by the most in two years, and the Ifo business confidence survey rose more than expected, suggesting early benefits from Merz's defense and infrastructure investment program are beginning to materialize, though the trajectory remains uncertain.

    Corporate Earnings and Stress

    HSBC's full-year results were the standout European corporate event, with pretax profit of $29.9 billion exceeding analyst expectations and the bonus pool rising 10% to $3.93 billion — the highest in at least a decade — as CEO Elhedery's pivot toward a more performance-differentiated compensation structure takes shape. The wealth division was the principal earnings driver, consistent with broader private banking flows into Asia. Diageo's second guidance cut of the fiscal year — projecting a 2–3% organic net sales decline — underscored how simultaneous consumer demand weakness in the US and China creates compounding risk for globally diversified consumer staples companies. Aston Martin announced plans to reduce its workforce by approximately 20%, citing Trump's tariffs among the complicating factors. The London Market Financial Solutions collapse, exposing Barclays, Atlas, and Jefferies, added to a broader narrative of stress in private credit and non-bank lending that spans both sides of the Atlantic this week.

    Politics and Trade Policy

    Labour's by-election loss in Gorton and Denton to the Green Party, with Reform UK second, illustrates the breadth of dissatisfaction with the Starmer government from both flanks. With local elections approaching and Starmer registering as the most unpopular UK prime minister by some modern polling measures, the political backdrop has become materially uncomfortable. Chancellor Reeves is expected to deliver a low-key economic update, with gilt issuance projected to be reduced — likely a constructive signal for markets given recent UK fiscal sustainability concerns. At the EU level, the European Parliament's trade chief moved to freeze ratification of the EU-US trade agreement pending clarification on Washington's revised framework, while an EU assessment confirmed Trump's 15% global tariff would push cumulative levies on certain EU exports above the EU-US agreement ceiling. Italy's industry minister escalated pressure on the Emissions Trading System, calling it "ineffective and harmful," following an 8% decline in European carbon prices after Merz indicated openness to revising or delaying the program — a signal with meaningful implications for climate policy credibility across the bloc. ---

    Global

    • Saturday's joint US-Israel strikes on Iranian nuclear facilities represent the week's most consequential geopolitical development, triggering Iranian missile responses against US bases in Qatar, Bahrain, and the UAE, Gulf airspace closures, and a sharp reassessment of Strait of Hormuz risk across energy and financial markets.
    • Brent crude had already risen approximately 18–20% year-to-date on US-Iran tensions before the strikes, with Bloomberg Economics estimating a full Hormuz closure could push oil to $108 per barrel.
    • Global debt reached $348 trillion at end-2025, a fresh record driven by a $29 trillion annual increase — the fastest accumulation since the pandemic — underscoring sovereign fiscal vulnerability in a persistently elevated rate environment.
    • The dollar's reserve currency status appears further entrenched near-term, with structural barriers facing both the euro and yuan as alternatives remaining unresolved despite concrete initiatives from both the ECB and Beijing.
    • US renewable power generation hit a record 1,162 terawatt-hours in 2025, representing 26% of total US electricity output, even as the net-zero political commitment has effectively collapsed across rich-nation energy communiqués.

    The Iran Shock and Energy Market Implications

    Saturday's US-Israel strikes on Iranian nuclear facilities, and Tehran's rapid missile responses against US military installations in Qatar, Bahrain, and the UAE, represent the most significant disruption to Middle Eastern geopolitical stability in recent years. Iran produces approximately 5% of global oil supply, and roughly 20% of global oil flows transit the Strait of Hormuz. Brent crude had already risen approximately 18–20% year-to-date as US diplomatic warnings intensified, and Bloomberg Economics now estimates a full Strait closure could push prices to $108 per barrel. OPEC+ was reportedly considering a larger-than-expected supply increase at its Sunday meeting, though the group's logistical capacity to compensate for a Hormuz disruption is constrained regardless of production decisions. Supertanker earnings had already surged to their highest levels since early 2020 in the days before the strikes, with very large crude carrier day-rates on the Middle East-to-China route exceeding $200,000 — a rise of more than 600% year-to-date — driven by elevated volumes of oil at sea and a concentrated accumulation of tonnage by South Korean shipowner Sinokor Group. The combination of a tight tanker market and a potential supply shock represents a compounding risk for Asian crude importers in particular.

    Global Monetary Policy and the Inflation Complication

    Prior to the Iran strikes, the dominant cross-regional monetary theme was a divergence between central banks making progress on inflation and the ECB and several Asian central banks beginning or contemplating easing cycles. The energy price shock introduced by the strikes complicates that calculus materially. For the ECB, having spent two years managing an inflation shock partly attributable to energy price volatility following Russia's invasion of Ukraine, the prospect of another cost-push episode is unwelcome. French and Spanish February inflation data surprised to the upside even before the strikes, reinforcing the case for caution. The Bank of Korea held at 2.5% while upgrading growth forecasts, but South Korea's acute energy import dependence places it among the most exposed economies to a sustained oil price spike. The broad implication is that easing cycles anticipated across multiple central banks may be deferred or compressed if Hormuz disruption proves durable — a scenario financial markets have begun, but not fully, priced.

    Reserve Currency Dynamics

    The week produced substantive developments on the long-running debate over dollar alternatives. The ECB unveiled a repo facility extending euro liquidity to most foreign central banks — the institution's most concrete step toward promoting the euro's international role — with notable support from Italian central bank governor Panetta and Bundesbank President Nagel, who indicated openness to a European sovereign bond creating a liquid pan-European benchmark asset. China's President Xi reiterated ambitions for a broader global role for the renminbi. Yet the structural barriers facing both currencies remain formidable: foreign ownership of Chinese government bonds stands at just under $400 billion, compared with $9.3 trillion for Treasuries, reflecting capital controls, limited market liquidity, and a banking sector that holds rather than trades fixed income. German Bund annual trading volumes represent less than two weeks of equivalent US Treasury turnover. As events unfolded over the weekend, dollar-denominated assets continued to attract global safe-haven flows, further entrenching reserve currency dynamics that periodic "sell America" rhetoric has done little to structurally alter.

    China's Belt and Road and the Trade Architecture

    China's Belt and Road Initiative appears to be entering a new expansionary phase after a period of retrenchment attributed partly to Covid-era constraints. Total BRI-related engagements reached an all-time high of $214 billion in 2025, with both average deal size and geographic reach at record levels, concentrated in central Asia, energy, metals, and technology. The "small-but-beautiful" era declared in 2021 appears to have been a transitional posture rather than a structural recalibration. Meanwhile, the broader global trade architecture remains unsettled following the Supreme Court's invalidation of Trump's IEEPA duties and the contested legal legitimacy of their Section 122 replacement. Indonesia, which signed a bilateral trade deal with the US just days before the ruling, found its arrangement's status uncertain within days. The British Chambers of Commerce estimated UK exporters face up to £3 billion in additional annual costs affecting some 40,000 companies as a result of losing their prior preferential arrangements — a concrete illustration of the distributional consequences of tariff regime instability.

  • US

    • Supreme Court strikes down "Liberation Day" reciprocal tariffs in a 6-3 ruling Friday, potentially cutting the effective US tariff rate by more than half.
    • FOMC minutes revealed surprising hawkishness, with several members flagging the possibility of rate increases if inflation remains elevated — markets had priced a more neutral posture.
    • AI-driven capital rotation intensified: Palo Alto Networks fell 7.5% on weak guidance while Nvidia rose on a Meta deployment deal; software names broadly underperformed hardware infrastructure.
    • Treasury markets posted their best week in months, yet a growing minority of managers question the consensus case for two or more Fed cuts, with "no landing" now seen as the modal scenario by some survey respondents.
    • January industrial production rose 0.7%, the largest monthly gain in nearly a year, though the data predate the full effect of subsequent tariff escalation.

    Monetary Policy and Inflation

    The week's most significant US macro development arrived Wednesday when FOMC minutes signaled that several members last month considered the possibility of rate increases rather than cuts, should inflation prove sticky. The hawkish tilt stood in contrast to market expectations of a more neutral posture and prompted a paring of easing bets. The dollar posted its best week in four months as a result. The underlying inflation picture nonetheless remained constructive heading into Friday's PCE release — the trimmed mean and median CPI sit at their lowest levels since 2021, Atlanta Fed sticky-price CPI fell to 3%, and Bloomberg Economics' city-level analysis showed the January national print averaged 2.16% across the twelve BLS-tracked metros, with the West South Central region below 1% and Dallas recording its first deflationary reading since the pandemic. One caveat merited attention: new car prices posted their largest January jump in years, masked by falling used-car prices, suggesting early tariff pass-through to consumers may only now be materializing. Friday's PCE — delayed since last November's government shutdown — carried unusual weight given the index had been tracking above core CPI near 3%.

    Tariff Ruling and Trade Architecture

    The Supreme Court's Friday ruling invalidating the emergency-powers statutory basis for reciprocal tariffs was the most consequential domestic policy development of the Trump presidency to date. The 6-3 decision struck the "Liberation Day" duties imposed on goods from dozens of countries, with Bloomberg Economics estimating potential importer refunds of up to $170 billion — a fight now remanded to lower courts. The White House indicated alternative statutory authorities remain available, and markets were not caught entirely off-guard given legal challenges had been widely anticipated. The ruling nonetheless materially alters the near-term trade architecture. The US-Indonesia bilateral deal — in which Jakarta committed to facilitate roughly $33 billion in US goods purchases in exchange for lower tariffs — was finalized this week, suggesting the administration will pursue country-by-country arrangements to reconstruct leverage.

    Equity Markets and AI Capital Cycle

    The AI capital expenditure cycle produced its starkest internal contradiction this week. A BofA global fund manager survey showed for the first time in a generation that a majority of money managers fear corporate overinvestment rather than underinvestment — a direct consequence of the $600 billion-plus in hyperscaler capex projected for 2026, which is visibly transforming cash-rich tech balance sheets into more leveraged ones. Single-name CDS on Meta and Alphabet became actively traded contracts, a structural shift worth monitoring. Goldman's software pair-trade basket — long infrastructure names including Cloudflare, CrowdStrike, and Oracle against shorts in Salesforce, Monday.com, and DocuSign — encapsulates the bifurcation. Apple's 40-day correlation with the Nasdaq 100 fell to roughly 0.2, its lowest since 2006, effectively functioning as a tech-sector haven. DRAM prices rose 75% from December to January, contributing to Cisco's worst share performance in nearly four years after weak guidance.

    Fiscal Dynamics

    New CBO projections showed net interest payments rising from $1 trillion this year to $2.1 trillion by 2036, reaching 4.6% of GDP — a ratio that alone exceeds Treasury Secretary Bessent's 3% total deficit target for 2029. The figures did not trigger an immediate bond market response, with the CBO projecting the 10-year yield moving from 4.1% to only 4.4% over the decade, though analysts noted the sheer scale of outstanding debt may constrain future administrations' fiscal options. Separately, Kevin Hassett's public criticism of a New York Fed study on tariff incidence — calling it "an embarrassment" and suggesting its authors should be "disciplined" — drew scrutiny as an unusual attack on independent economic research. Private equity also featured: with roughly $7 trillion in AUM, holding periods at a 20-year high of 4.4 years, and LP pressure mounting over distribution shortfalls, a meaningful clearing event appears unlikely to arrive quickly despite a modest pickup in IPO and strategic deal activity. ---

    Asia

    • Former South Korean President Yoon Suk Yeol was sentenced Wednesday to life in prison for insurrection following his late-2024 martial law declaration; prosecutors had sought the death penalty.
    • Japan's Prime Minister Takaichi formally took office following a re-election vote and announced a $36 billion investment commitment in US oil, gas, and critical minerals — the first concrete tranche of the $550 billion bilateral framework with the Trump administration.
    • Indonesia's institutional credibility deteriorated sharply: MSCI placed its emerging-market classification under review, FTSE Russell followed, Moody's cut the credit outlook to negative, and the rupiah weakened to levels last seen nearly three decades ago.
    • India's AI Impact Summit in New Delhi concluded with AI infrastructure pledges rising to at least $270 billion by week's end, up from $140 billion at the outset, anchored by commitments from Reliance, Adani, and international partners.
    • DRAM prices rose 75% from December to January, with Samsung and SK Hynix among the principal beneficiaries as AI accelerator demand crowds out conventional buyers.

    China: Policy Signals and Market Bifurcation

    Xi Jinping's call early in the week for domestic demand to serve as China's "main driver" of economic growth represents a rhetorical reinforcement of a long-stated rebalancing objective — though concrete fiscal follow-through remains the operative test, as the IMF's Article IV assessment noted that household consumption has hovered near 40% of GDP essentially unchanged since 2005. Chinese AI capital formation showed no sign of slowing: Moonshot targeted a $10 billion valuation in a new funding round, more than doubling the $4.3 billion mark set weeks earlier in a round backed by Alibaba and Tencent. As Hong Kong markets reopened after the Lunar New Year break on Thursday, generative AI startups Zhipu and MiniMax Group surged as investors rotated into pure AI plays; robotics firms attracted buying interest following their Spring Festival gala showcase. The broader Chinese market nonetheless drifted lower as earnings season approached with subdued expectations, and the Pentagon's brief — and quickly reversed — decision to add Alibaba, BYD, and Baidu to its list of companies deemed to be aiding the Chinese military served as a reminder of how abrupt and unpredictable US-China tech friction can be, with Alibaba falling more than 3% in Hong Kong on the news before the designation was withdrawn.

    Japan: Fiscal Ambition and BOJ Watch

    Japan's economic news flow carried two competing signals. Fourth-quarter real GDP expanded just 0.2% annualized, far below the 1.6% consensus, with consumer spending rising only 0.1% — data that reinforces the case for Prime Minister Takaichi's expansionary fiscal posture and complicates the BOJ's path toward further normalization. Against that, manufacturing PMI remained in expansionary territory for a third consecutive month, and Takaichi's $36 billion US investment commitment — anchored by a large natural gas facility in Ohio — serves multiple strategic functions: visible progress on a trade framework, energy security diversification, and a potential buffer against tariff escalation. BOJ board nominations for the seats vacated by dovish member Asahi Noguchi and consensus voter Junko Nakagawa are expected as soon as February 25, and given Takaichi's historical association with reflation, the appointments will be parsed closely by JGB and yen markets. Mizuho Financial fell roughly 5% Monday and 3.8% Tuesday following disclosure of a regulatory investigation into suspected insider trading at its brokerage unit.

    South Korea, Southeast Asia, and India

    South Korea's Yoon verdict formally closes the most severe constitutional crisis the country has faced in decades, though an appeal is expected and political recalibration continues. In Indonesia, the compounding nature of governance erosion became legible in market terms this week: the replacement of technocrats with loyalists, a family member appointed as Bank Indonesia deputy governor, and a flagship school lunch program costing an estimated $28 billion annually combine to produce a currency under severe pressure, credit outlook deterioration, and index review — all simultaneously. Bank Indonesia held rates at 4.75% as expected but faces a familiar central bank dilemma: rate cuts are warranted by inflation but precluded by currency weakness. India presented a sharply contrasting picture. The AI Impact Summit demonstrated genuine convening power, HSBC's flash survey showed activity accelerating in February, the country formally joined a US-led critical minerals initiative, and Prime Minister Modi used the occasion to press an AI sovereignty agenda resting on domestic language models, shared compute, and semiconductor ambitions. Malaysia's exports rose at their fastest pace in over three years in January. Australia's BHP delivered earnings at the upper end of expectations, with copper contributing more than half of group profit for the first time; the Reserve Bank's February minutes confirmed its rate hike reflected a downgraded view of potential output, making it the first major DM central bank to tighten in 2026. ---

    Europe

    • European equities set fresh records mid-week, with the Stoxx 600 reaching 627-628; strategists caution that eight consecutive months of gains and five-year-high valuations leave limited room for further multiple expansion.
    • Christine Lagarde is reportedly considering an early ECB departure before her October 2027 mandate expires, accelerating a succession debate with significant policy and political dimensions.
    • UK unemployment rose to its highest level since the pandemic while wage growth eased, prompting traders to fully price half a point of Bank of England cuts by year-end.
    • EU carbon allowances fell to their lowest since July as Slovakia, Germany, and France openly clashed over ETS reform at the Brussels summit; the Commission pledged a reform proposal for Q3.
    • Airbus fell 6.7% Thursday after disclosing Pratt & Whitney engine delivery shortfalls that will force reduced aircraft output.

    ECB Succession and Monetary Policy

    The week's most consequential European institutional development was the acceleration of ECB succession planning. Reports emerged that Christine Lagarde may step down before her mandate's formal October 2027 conclusion, calibrated around the French presidential election in April 2027 — a timing designed to preserve outgoing President Macron's ability to influence the appointment before a potential hard-right outcome. An ECB spokesperson confirmed only that Lagarde "has not taken any decision regarding the end of her term." Hawkish board member Isabel Schnabel stated she intends to serve her full term; Spain is actively campaigning for its own candidate; Dutch governor Klaas Knot appears the most plausible consensus choice. The succession process threatens to expose familiar fault lines between hawks and doves, and between national and supranational interests, at a delicate moment. The euro area flash PMI for February surprised to the upside, with manufacturers recording their best performance since 2022 driven by an unexpected improvement in Germany, and Q4 negotiated wages rose 3% — a data point the ECB will monitor carefully as it balances disinflation progress against residual services-sector price pressures.

    UK: Labour Market and Fiscal Crosscurrents

    The UK data week delivered a clear dovish impulse. Unemployment rose to its highest level since the pandemic, youth unemployment climbed above the EU average for the first time, and wage growth eased further — together prompting traders to fully price 50 basis points of Bank of England cuts by year-end, with some analysts citing March as an increasingly plausible timing for the first move. Against that, the UK recorded its largest budget surplus on record during the period, boosted by surging capital gains tax receipts and lower debt servicing costs, offering a brief fiscal respite for a government navigating difficult spending decisions. Prime Minister Starmer reversed plans to cancel some May local elections following internal backlash — the latest in a series of U-turns — while also outlining regulation requiring AI chatbots to comply with child online safety laws. The arrest of Prince Andrew on suspicion of misconduct in public office added institutional pressure at an already sensitive moment for the monarchy.

    Corporate Developments

    The week produced a notable dispersion of corporate outcomes. Airbus's disclosure that Pratt & Whitney has failed to deliver engines in sufficient volume to meet production targets is a material setback, shifting accountability toward the supply chain and complicating an already complex order backlog management challenge. Bayer agreed to pay more than $7 billion to resolve Roundup cancer litigation, potentially closing a years-long legal overhang. Nestlé gained 4.5% in Zurich after new CEO Philipp Navratil signaled a turnaround on track, guiding for 3-4% organic revenue growth in 2026. Moncler rallied 13% on results described as significantly ahead of estimates, lifting LVMH and Hermès. Hapag-Lloyd agreed to acquire ZIM Integrated Shipping at roughly $4.2 billion, sending ZIM shares up 35%. Norsk Hydro fell 4.4% Monday on Goldman and RBC downgrades, Pinewood Technologies tumbled 32% after Apax abandoned its bid citing market conditions, and Novo Nordisk's approximately 5,000 job cuts — among the largest layoffs in Danish history — began to weigh on Danish consumer sentiment. In Denmark, Prime Minister Frederiksen is reportedly weighing a snap election to capitalize on recovered standing following the Greenland dispute.

    Carbon Markets and Geopolitical Repositioning

    The EU carbon market faces its sternmost political test in years. Allowances fell to their lowest since July as leaders openly clashed at the Brussels summit, with Slovakia's Fico calling for suspension, Germany's Merz initially floating revision before softening, and Macron defending the program. JPMorgan analysts flagged that the resulting uncertainty is already impairing long-term green capex planning regardless of where reforms ultimately land. The broader European geopolitical repositioning continued in parallel: eurozone finance ministers pushed for a larger global role for the euro, the BofA fund manager survey showed the largest-ever overweight on the euro versus the dollar, and Sweden tentatively reopened the euro adoption debate — a shift framed through geopolitical rather than monetary lenses, reflecting the recalibration of the transatlantic relationship following US Secretary of State Rubio's weekend comment that the US-Europe alliance "has to change." ---

    Global

    • Brent crude surged 4.3% on Wednesday to re-cross $70 per barrel as US-Iran tensions escalated, with prediction markets assigning roughly 70% probability to a US military strike by week's end.
    • The IEA Paris ministerial summit ended without a joint communiqué as the US threatened to exit unless the agency abandons its net zero framing — a significant fracture given Washington co-founded the institution in the 1970s.
    • The Supreme Court's tariff ruling removed — at least temporarily — the single largest source of global trade policy uncertainty, though the White House signaled it will seek alternative statutory authorities.
    • India's AI Impact Summit saw infrastructure pledges rise from $140 billion at the outset to $270 billion by conclusion, with AI sovereignty emerging as the defining strategic theme for non-US, non-Chinese economies.
    • OpenAI is close to finalizing a funding round exceeding $100 billion, valuing the company above $850 billion — among the largest private financings on record.

    US-Iran: Two-Track Strategy and Oil Markets

    The US-Iran dynamic dominated global market attention across the week. Indirect nuclear talks opened Monday in Geneva, with US Special Envoy Steve Witkoff and Iranian Foreign Minister Araghchi holding what both sides described as constructive initial sessions, with Iran expected to return detailed proposals within two weeks. The diplomatic opening coincided with Iranian naval exercises that briefly closed portions of the Strait of Hormuz Monday — a calibrated reminder of Tehran's leverage. The tone shifted materially by Thursday: President Trump stated Iran has ten to fifteen days to reach an agreement and that the US is "ready, willing, and able to rapidly fulfill its mission, with speed and violence, if necessary." Iran responded it would "defend itself and respond like never before." The Wall Street Journal reported the administration is evaluating a limited early strike to pressure rather than preempt. Complicating military planning, the UK reportedly withheld access to Diego Garcia — a critical Indian Ocean base — for potential operations. Vice President Vance acknowledged talks had gone "well in some ways" while noting Iranian negotiators had not accepted core US red lines. WTI, which had opened the week near $63, moved toward $66 by Friday. The trajectory of negotiations over the coming two weeks is the single most consequential near-term variable for global energy markets and risk assets.

    Energy Markets and Tanker Dynamics

    Beyond the geopolitical risk premium, energy markets reflected structural repositioning. South Korea's Sinokor group has accumulated roughly 120 VLCCs over the past two months, helping push benchmark VLCC earnings above $120,000 per day — a more than fourfold increase over the month. Port of Los Angeles export volumes fell 8% year-on-year in January, with soybean shipments to China down 80% as Argentine and Brazilian suppliers locked in multi-month contracts that effectively displace US exporters for at least another agricultural cycle. Japan's $36 billion US energy investment commitment — anchored in the bilateral trade framework — represents a tangible win for the administration's bilateral deal architecture. The IEA fracture, meanwhile, appears genuine: the Paris ministerial ended without the customary joint communiqué, with only a Netherlands-authored summary published after US Energy Secretary Chris Wright assailed the agency's net zero framing. Members found limited common ground on critical minerals and energy security, but the institutional fault lines will likely persist.

    AI Sovereignty as Strategic Competition

    The week's other defining global theme was the emergence of AI sovereignty as an explicit policy objective across non-US, non-Chinese economies. India's AI Impact Summit — the first such gathering held in the Global South — crystallized a common structural concern: the most consequential AI systems are controlled by a small number of US and Chinese entities, and access to them can in principle be interrupted. The 2024 UniSuper incident, in which a Google cloud misconfiguration cut off Australian pension fund members, served as a reference point for technology dependency risk.
